OFFICE VISIT — NEWBORN EXAMINATION
Date of Service: March 17, 2012
Patient Age: 2 days
ICD-10: Z00.110 (Health examination for newborn under 8 days old)
CPT: 99460 (Initial newborn care, per day, in hospital)

SUBJECTIVE:
Newborn male infant seen for initial outpatient pediatric assessment following
hospital discharge. Baby born March 15, 2012 at 0647 hours at VCU Medical
Center, Richmond, VA via spontaneous vaginal delivery at 39 weeks 2 days
gestational age. Labor was induced at 39+0 for elective induction; total labor
duration approximately 14 hours. Rupture of membranes was spontaneous, 3 hours
prior to delivery. Amniotic fluid was clear. No meconium staining. No cord
complications. APGAR scores 8 at 1 minute (deducted points for color — mild
acrocyanosis, and muscle tone — moderate flexion), 9 at 5 minutes (deducted
for color only — mild acrocyanosis of extremities persisting). No resuscitation
required. Infant placed skin-to-skin immediately and initiated breastfeeding
within 45 minutes of delivery.

Mother: Denise Elaine Reeves, age 28, G1P1, blood type O-positive, Rubella
immune, RPR non-reactive, HBsAg negative, HIV negative, GBS negative, Rh
positive. Prenatal care initiated at 8 weeks gestation, uncomplicated
throughout. Prenatal vitamins taken consistently. No gestational diabetes,
no preeclampsia, no infections during pregnancy. No tobacco, alcohol, or
substance use during pregnancy. No significant medication use beyond prenatal
vitamins and occasional acetaminophen.

Father: Marcus Anthony Reeves, age 30, active duty United States Army (E-6,
Staff Sergeant), currently stationed at Fort Lee, VA. In good health per
parental report. No known genetic conditions.

FAMILY HISTORY:
Maternal: Mother's parents alive and well. Maternal grandmother — hypertension,
managed with medication. Maternal grandfather — type 2 diabetes mellitus,
diagnosed age 55. No history of developmental delays, seizure disorders, or
neurodegenerative conditions on maternal side per mother's report.

Paternal: Father's parents — paternal grandfather alive, age 62, history of
hypertension and mild arthritis. Paternal grandmother, age 60, reported by
father to have "memory problems" — began in her late 50s, described as
progressive forgetfulness, difficulty with word-finding, and occasional
confusion. Father states she has not been formally evaluated and the family
attributes it to "early aging" and stress. No formal diagnosis of Alzheimer's
disease or dementia on record. Father has one sibling (brother, age 27) who is
healthy. No consanguinity.

No known family history of autism, intellectual disability, epilepsy, metabolic
disorders, or childhood neurodegenerative disease in either lineage per
parental report.

Mother reports baby is breastfeeding well, latching without difficulty,
approximately every 2-3 hours. One wet diaper and one meconium stool since
discharge this morning. Mother reports infant is alert when awake and sleeping
well between feedings.

OBJECTIVE:
Vital Signs:
  Weight: 7 lbs 4 oz (3.29 kg) — 50th percentile (birth weight 7 lbs 6 oz;
          weight loss of 2 oz = 1.7% from birth weight — within normal limits)
  Length: 20.0 inches (50.8 cm) — 52nd percentile
  Head Circumference: 34.5 cm — 48th percentile
  Temperature: 98.4 F (36.9 C) axillary
  Heart Rate: 142 bpm
  Respiratory Rate: 38 breaths/min

General: Well-appearing, full-term newborn male. Active, alert when awake.
Appropriate cry — vigorous. Pink centrally with mild acrocyanosis of hands and
feet (normal for age).

HEENT: Anterior fontanelle open, soft, flat, approximately 2.5 x 2.5 cm.
Posterior fontanelle palpable, approximately 0.5 cm. No cephalohematoma. No
caput succedaneum. Sutures approximated, no overriding. Eyes: Red reflex
present bilaterally. No discharge. Ears: Pinnae normal in shape, position, and
rotation. Newborn hearing screen PASSED bilaterally (OAE) per hospital records.
Nose: Patent bilaterally. Mouth: Intact palate (both hard and soft verified by
palpation). No cleft. Mucous membranes moist. No natal teeth. Frenulum intact,
no tongue-tie.

Neck: Supple, no masses, no torticollis. Full range of motion.

Cardiovascular: Regular rate and rhythm, no murmur. Femoral pulses 2+ and
symmetric bilaterally. Capillary refill <2 seconds.

Respiratory: Clear to auscultation bilaterally. No grunting, flaring, or
retractions. Chest wall symmetric.

Abdomen: Soft, non-distended, non-tender. Umbilical cord stump clamped,
clean, no erythema, no discharge. Liver edge palpable 1 cm below right costal
margin (normal). No splenomegaly. No masses.

Genitourinary: Normal male genitalia. Testes descended bilaterally. No
hypospadias. Patent urethral meatus. No inguinal hernias.

Musculoskeletal: Clavicles intact bilaterally. Hips: Ortolani and Barlow
negative bilaterally. Spine straight, no sacral dimple, no tuft. Extremities
symmetric, full range of motion, no anomalies. Digits — 10 fingers, 10 toes,
no syndactyly or polydactyly.

Skin: No rashes. No birthmarks noted. Mild jaundice — Kramer zone 1 (face
only). Skin turgor normal. Mongolian spot noted on lower back/sacral area,
approximately 3 x 4 cm, blue-gray, consistent with dermal melanocytosis.

Neurological: Normal tone — appropriate flexor posture. Moro reflex present
and symmetric. Rooting and sucking reflexes intact and vigorous. Palmar grasp
present bilaterally. Plantar reflex present. Moves all extremities
symmetrically. No tremors, no jitteriness.

Newborn Screening: State metabolic screen (Virginia NBS) collected at VCU prior
to discharge. Results pending.

ASSESSMENT:
1. Healthy full-term newborn male, appropriate for gestational age.
2. Mild physiologic jaundice — Kramer zone 1, non-concerning.
3. Normal newborn examination.

PLAN:
1. Continue breastfeeding on demand, approximately every 2-3 hours.
2. Follow up in office in 10-12 days (2-week check) for weight check and
   jaundice reassessment.
3. Discussed safe sleep practices (back to sleep, firm flat surface, no loose
   bedding), car seat safety, and signs of jaundice progression with mother.
4. Newborn screening results will be followed up when available.
5. If jaundice appears to worsen (spreading to chest/abdomen, poor feeding,
   lethargy), return to office or ED immediately.
6. Mother coping well, good social support. Father present at visit.

— Sarah E. Mitchell, MD

OFFICE VISIT — 12-MONTH WELL-CHILD CHECK
Date of Service: March 18, 2013
Patient Age: 12 months 3 days
ICD-10: Z00.129
CPT: 99392, 90460, 90461

SUBJECTIVE:
Mother brings Eli for his 12-month well-child check. She reports he is pulling
to stand and has been doing so for approximately 2 weeks. He is not yet
cruising along furniture. He crawled (on hands and knees) starting at
approximately 10.5 months. He says 3-4 words: "mama," "dada," "no," and
something that sounds like "ba" for ball. He points to objects he wants
(usually food or a toy) with his index finger. He claps his hands, plays
pat-a-cake. He is eating a variety of table foods. He drinks from a sippy cup.
Mother has recently weaned from breastfeeding. She plans to start whole milk.

Mother states: "He seems a little behind the other kids in our playgroup —
they're all walking by now. Is that normal?" She mentions that a friend's baby
the same age is running already. Father is present at this visit and does not
express concern: "My brother didn't walk until 15 months and he's fine."

OBJECTIVE:
Vital Signs:
  Weight: 21 lbs 0 oz (9.53 kg) — 40th percentile
  Length: 29.5 inches (74.9 cm) — 38th percentile
  Head Circumference: 46.0 cm — 40th percentile
  Temperature: 98.6 F (37.0 C)
  Heart Rate: 118 bpm
  Respiratory Rate: 26 breaths/min

General: Active, alert toddler. Crawling on exam room floor. Pulling to stand
on mother's leg during visit.

Physical Exam: Within normal limits. HEENT: 8 teeth. Ears: bilateral TMs clear
with normal landmarks and mobility. Heart: RRR, no murmur. Lungs: CTA
bilaterally. Abdomen: soft, non-tender, no hepatosplenomegaly.

Neurological/Developmental:
  Denver Developmental Screening Test II (DDST-II) administered:
    Personal-Social: NORMAL — drinks from cup, plays ball, indicates wants.
    Fine Motor-Adaptive: NORMAL — pincer grasp (refined), scribbles with
      crayon (with demonstration), puts block in cup.
    Language: NORMAL — 3-4 specific words, understands simple commands
      ("give me the ball"), points to objects.
    Gross Motor: MARGINAL — pulling to stand but NOT cruising. Not walking.
      Expected milestone for 12 months includes pulling to stand and
      beginning to cruise. Cruising typically emerges by 9-12 months. This
      places Eli at the low end of the expected range for gross motor.

  Additional observations:
  - Eye contact: Good. Engages with examiner.
  - Social referencing: Looks to mother when uncertain — present.
  - Joint attention: Points to items and looks back to mother — present.
  - Responds to name: Yes.
  - No repetitive behaviors observed during visit.
  - Tone: Appears normal on examination. Strength symmetric.
  - Reflexes: 2+ throughout, symmetric. Plantar response — flexor
    bilaterally (normal for age).

ASSESSMENT:
1. 12-month-old male. Growth tracking at 38th-40th percentile — slight
   downward trend from earlier percentiles (was ~48th at 2 months), still
   within normal range.
2. Developmental milestones: Language, fine motor, social/emotional — all
   age-appropriate. Gross motor mildly delayed — pulling to stand but not
   cruising. Denver screen marginal in gross motor domain.
3. Not walking is NOT abnormal at 12 months. Normal range for independent
   walking is 9-18 months. However, the lack of cruising at 12 months bears
   monitoring.

PLAN:
1. Immunizations: MMR #1, Varicella #1, HepA #1, PCV13 #4. Administered.
2. Transition to whole milk. Limit to 16-24 oz/day.
3. Discussed gross motor findings with parents. Reassured that gross motor
   development is at the low end of normal and many children, particularly
   boys, walk later. However, if not cruising by 15 months or walking by 18
   months, will refer for evaluation.
4. Encouraged active play, push toys, assisted standing activities.
5. Lead screen ordered — result pending.
6. Hemoglobin checked — 11.8 g/dL (normal).
7. Next visit: 15-month well-child check.

— Sarah E. Mitchell, MD

   frequently walk later than girls.

4. Fine motor and social/emotional: Age-appropriate.

PLAN:
1. Immunizations: DTaP #4 administered.
2. Reassured mother that cruising at 15 months, while on the later side, is

   especially boys, walk between 15-18 months. "Boys develop at their own
   pace — and Eli is making steady progress with cruising."

   evaluation.
4. Encouraged continued active play, push toys, walking with one hand held.

he's slowed down with learning new words. He was picking up words faster a
couple months ago." Current words per mother's list: mama, dada, no, ball,
bottle, dog, up, bye-bye, more, juice, car, shoe. No two-word combinations.

Mother also notes: "He doesn't point at things in books when I ask him to —
like, 'Where's the dog?' He just stares at the page." She also reports that
when she points at something across the room, he doesn't consistently follow
her point — "sometimes he does, sometimes he just looks at my hand."

Father present by phone (FaceTime) — asks if this is related to his mother's
memory issues. "Could something run in the family?" Dr. Mitchell notes this
question in chart.

OBJECTIVE:
Vital Signs:
  Weight: 23 lbs 4 oz (10.55 kg) — 36th percentile
  Length: 31.5 inches (80.0 cm) — 33rd percentile
  Head Circumference: 47.5 cm — 37th percentile
  Temperature: 98.4 F (36.9 C)
  Heart Rate: 110 bpm
  Respiratory Rate: 24 breaths/min

General: Alert toddler. Cruising along furniture in exam room. Held mother's
hand to walk to exam table. Babbling with intermittent recognizable words.

Physical Exam: Unremarkable. No dysmorphic features. Ears clear.

Neurological/Developmental:
  - Gross motor: NOT walking independently. Cruises well. Walks with one
    hand held. Wide base of support. Sits independently without issue.
    Can get to standing from sitting by pulling up on furniture.
  - Fine motor: Stacks 3 blocks. Scribbles. Turns pages.
  - Language (expressive): ~12 words. No word combinations. Uses words
    inconsistently — sometimes uses "more" and "juice" at mealtimes, other
    times does not use words when context is appropriate.
  - Language (receptive): Follows some one-step commands (e.g., "give me
    the cup") but inconsistent. Does NOT point to body parts on request.
    Does NOT point to pictures in books on request.
  - Social: Eye contact present but somewhat inconsistent during visit.
    Does not consistently follow examiner's or mother's point.

M-CHAT-R/F Screening (Modified Checklist for Autism in Toddlers, Revised
with Follow-Up):
  Administered via parent questionnaire.
  Total Score: 4/20 (2 critical items failed)
  Failed items:
    - Item 7: "Does your child point with one finger to ask for something
      or to get help?" — Parent answered YES but with qualifier: "sometimes,
      but not always." On follow-up questioning, pointing is inconsistent
      and emerging. Scored as PASS per follow-up clarification.
    - Item 13: "Does your child point with one finger to show you something
      interesting?" — FAIL. Mother confirms Eli does not point to share
      interest (protodeclarative pointing absent).
    - Item 15: "If you point at something across the room, does your child
      look at it?" — FAIL. Mother confirms he does not consistently follow
      a point.
    - Item 2: "Have you ever wondered if your child might be deaf?" — Parent
      marked YES. Follow-up: "Not really deaf, but sometimes he doesn't
      respond when I call him." Scored as at-risk response.
    - Item 6: "Does your child reach up to be picked up?" — FAIL on initial
      screen; PASS on follow-up (he does reach but inconsistently).

  Interpretation: Score of 4 with 2 critical item failures places Eli in the
  BORDERLINE/LOW-RISK positive range. Per M-CHAT-R/F algorithm, 2 critical
  item failures = warrants monitoring. Score of 3-7 = administer follow-up
  interview. Follow-up interview completed; final score: 2 critical items
  still failed after follow-up.

ASSESSMENT:
1. 18-month-old male with gross motor delay — not walking independently at
   18 months. This now exceeds typical variation and warrants evaluation.
2. Language development showing plateau — 12 words but inconsistent use. No
   word combinations. Receptive language lagging (not following points
   consistently, not identifying body parts).
3. M-CHAT-R/F: Borderline. Two critical items failed (protodeclarative
   pointing absent, not following point). Does not meet high-risk threshold
   but concerning in context of other delays.
4. Growth: Slight continued decline in percentiles — now 33rd-36th. Still
   within normal limits but trend noted.

PLAN:
1. Immunizations: DTaP #4 (was given at 15 months — confirmed in record).
   HepA #2 administered today.
2. Referral to Virginia Infant & Toddler Connection (Part C Early
   Intervention) for comprehensive developmental evaluation. Requesting PT
   and speech-language evaluations specifically.
   Contact information provided to mother: Richmond area Early Intervention
   coordinator, (804) 555-0291.
3. Gross motor delay: Formal physical therapy evaluation requested.
4. Language concerns: Formal speech-language evaluation requested.
5. Discussed concerns with mother at length. Reassured parents that
   developmental variation is common, especially in boys, and that early
   intervention services are supportive and commonly used. "Many children
   with mild delays catch up with a little help."
6. Regarding father's question about family history of memory problems:
   Explained that his mother's memory issues are more likely age-related
   and that there is no clear connection to Eli's developmental pattern at
   this time. Will keep in mind.
7. Hearing: Normal hearing screen at 6 months. Consider repeat if language
   does not progress.
8. Next well-child visit at 24 months. Return sooner if concerns escalate.

— Sarah E. Mitchell, MD

                    DEVELOPMENTAL EVALUATION REPORT

IDENTIFYING INFORMATION:
Eli Marcus Reeves is a 3-year-0-month-old biracial (Black/White) male referred
by his primary care pediatrician, Dr. Sarah Mitchell of Greenfield Pediatrics,
for comprehensive developmental evaluation due to concerns regarding delayed
language development, gross motor delays, stereotypic hand-wringing behaviors,
brief staring episodes, and a positive M-CHAT-R/F screening at 24 months.

This evaluation was conducted over two sessions (April 1 and April 3, 2015),
totaling approximately 4.5 hours of direct clinical contact, in addition to
record review and scoring time. The evaluation was attended by Eli, his mother
(Denise Reeves), and his father (Marcus Reeves) on April 1. Mother alone
attended the April 3 session.

REFERRAL CONCERNS:
Per referral documentation and parent interview:
1. Delayed gross motor milestones (walking achieved at approximately 26 months)
2. Delayed expressive language (~25 words, no consistent phrases at 36 months)
3. Delayed receptive language (inconsistent following of commands, limited
   identification of objects/body parts)
4. Repetitive hand-wringing/washing movements (>10 episodes/day)
5. Intermittent staring episodes (1-2/day, 5-10 seconds duration)
6. Intermittent toe-walking
7. Wide-based, uncoordinated gait with frequent falls
8. Positive M-CHAT-R/F at 24 months (6/20, 3 critical failures)

DEVELOPMENTAL HISTORY:

Prenatal/Birth History: Pregnancy uncomplicated. Spontaneous vaginal delivery
at 39+2 weeks after elective induction. APGAR 8/9. Birth weight 7 lbs 6 oz.
No NICU stay. Passed newborn hearing screen and metabolic screen. No neonatal
complications.

Developmental Milestones (per parent report and medical records):
  Social smile: 6 weeks
  Rolled over: 3.5 months
  Sat independently: 7 months
  Babbled (canonical): 6 months
  First words: 12 months (3-4 words)
  Crawled: 10.5 months
  Pulled to stand: 12 months
  Cruised: 14.5 months
  Walked independently: 26 months (DELAYED — expected by 18 months)
  Vocabulary at 24 months: ~20 words (DELAYED — expected 50+ words)
  Current vocabulary at 36 months: ~25 words, no phrases (SIGNIFICANTLY
  DELAYED — expected 200+ words, 2-3 word sentences)

Parents report that early milestones (up to approximately 12 months) were
achieved within expected timeframes, with subsequent deceleration of
development most notable in gross motor and language domains.

Parents also report a perceived plateau or possible regression in vocabulary
over the past 2-3 months — mother notes 2 previously used words ("shoe,"

MEDICAL HISTORY:
No hospitalizations. No surgeries. No known allergies. No current medications.
Immunizations up to date. Labs: CBC, CMP, TSH, lead — all normal (March 2014).
Hearing screen: Passed (6 months — OAE).

FAMILY HISTORY:
As documented in PCP records. Notable: Paternal grandmother with progressive
memory difficulties onset in late 50s, no formal diagnosis. No family history

CURRENT SERVICES:
Physical therapy: 1x/week, 45-minute sessions, since age 20 months.
Speech-language therapy: 2x/week, 30-minute sessions, since age 20 months.
No ABA therapy (waitlisted). No special education placement currently.
Attends home daycare 3 mornings/week.

BEHAVIORAL OBSERVATIONS:

Eli was observed over two sessions totaling 4.5 hours. He arrived for the first
session in his mother's arms and was reluctant to separate initially but did
transition to the testing room with both parents present.

Appearance: Well-groomed, appropriately dressed boy. Appeared well-nourished.
No dysmorphic features noted.

Activity Level and Behavior: Eli was intermittently cooperative with testing
demands. He demonstrated periods of engagement with test materials, interspersed
with periods of disinterest and self-directed activity. He frequently returned
to hand-wringing behavior when not actively engaged with materials or when
between test items. The hand-wringing was stereotypic in nature — rhythmic,
bilateral, occurring at midline, involving a repetitive rubbing/wringing motion
of the hands. This behavior was observed approximately 12-15 times across the
two testing sessions and appeared to be a default resting behavior when hands
were not otherwise occupied. The behavior did not appear to be associated with
distress or emotional arousal; rather, it occurred in a variety of contexts and
emotional states. This stereotypic behavior is consistent with the restricted/
repetitive behavior domain described in Autism Spectrum Disorder.

Gait and Motor Observations: Eli walked independently with a wide-based gait.
Toe-walking was observed intermittently (estimated 30-40% of steps). When
walking flat-footed, his gait appeared mildly uncoordinated with reduced arm
swing. He tripped and fell once during the first session while walking across
the room. He had difficulty navigating a step up into the testing room (required
hand-held assistance). His overall motor coordination appeared below age
expectations.

Language and Communication: Eli used approximately 8-10 recognizable words
during the evaluation sessions combined. He used "mama" frequently, "no"
several times, "more" during snack time, and "ball" when presented with a ball
during testing. Utterances were single words only — no multi-word combinations
were observed. Non-verbal communication was limited: he did not use pointing to
share interest (protodeclarative pointing absent), though he occasionally
reached toward desired objects. He did bring a toy car to his mother once
(possible showing behavior, though ambiguous — may have been requesting
assistance rather than sharing). He did not follow a point across the room
consistently (successful on approximately 2/6 trials). He did not direct the
examiner's attention to objects or events.

Eye Contact and Social Engagement: Eye contact was present but inconsistent and
often fleeting. Eli made eye contact most frequently with his mother and less
so with the examiner. During structured one-on-one interactions (e.g., ball
play, book-sharing), he demonstrated more sustained eye contact and social
engagement. During unstructured periods, he tended to engage in solitary
activity (manipulating a toy, hand-wringing) without seeking social interaction.
He did not demonstrate imaginative or pretend play — his play was largely
functional (rolling a car, stacking blocks) or sensorimotor (mouthing objects,
spinning wheels of a toy car).

Attention and Engagement: Two notable episodes of apparent disengagement were
observed during testing:

  Episode 1 (Session 1, approximately 45 minutes into evaluation): Eli was
  seated at the table during Bayley-III cognitive items. He suddenly ceased
  activity, eyes became fixed in a forward gaze, and he was unresponsive to
  the examiner calling his name twice and to a light touch on his shoulder
  from his mother. The episode lasted approximately 7-8 seconds. He then
  blinked, looked down at the test materials, and resumed manipulating the
  blocks without any apparent confusion, disorientation, or distress.

  Episode 2 (Session 2, approximately 30 minutes into evaluation): During
  ADOS-2 Module 1 administration, Eli was engaged in free play with a set
  of toys. He stopped playing, stared fixedly at the wall, and was again
  unresponsive to his name for approximately 5-6 seconds. He then resumed
  playing, picking up the toy he had put down. Mother noted, "That's one of
  his staring spells — he does that all the time."

  Clinical Impression Regarding Staring Episodes: These episodes presented
  as brief periods of apparent inattention and disengagement lasting
  approximately 5-8 seconds, during which the child was unresponsive to
  auditory and tactile stimuli. Upon resolution, the child returned to his
  prior activity without evidence of postictal confusion, distress, or
  disorientation. While the possibility of absence seizures can be considered
  in the differential, the clinical presentation is more consistent with
  dissociative or inattentive episodes as commonly observed in children with
  Autism Spectrum Disorder. The brief duration, lack of motor phenomena (no
  eye blinking, lip smacking, automatisms, or tonic/clonic activity), lack
  of postictal state, and the context of occurring in a child with an
  emerging autism presentation make these episodes most consistent with ASD-
  related disengagement. An EEG is not recommended at this time, though this
  may be reconsidered if episodes change in character, increase significantly
  in frequency or duration, or if motor phenomena develop.

PHYSICAL AND NEUROLOGICAL EXAMINATION:

General: Well-appearing, well-nourished boy. No dysmorphic features. Height
37.0 inches (94.0 cm) — 27th percentile. Weight 29 lbs 2 oz (13.2 kg) —
31st percentile. Head circumference 49.3 cm — 30th percentile.

HEENT: Normocephalic, atraumatic. Eyes: Pupils equal, round, reactive to
light. Extraocular movements intact. No nystagmus. Red reflex present
bilaterally. Visual tracking intact. Ears: TMs clear bilaterally. External
ears normal. Nares patent. Oropharynx clear, palate intact, no bifid uvula.

Cardiovascular: Regular rate and rhythm. No murmur, rub, or gallop.

Respiratory: Clear to auscultation bilaterally. No increased work of
breathing.

Abdomen: Soft, non-tender, non-distended. No hepatosplenomegaly. Normal
bowel sounds.

Skin: No neurocutaneous findings. No cafe-au-lait spots. No ash-leaf
macules. No hyperpigmented lesions. Mongolian spot (dermal melanocytosis)
noted on sacrum, unchanged per parent.

Musculoskeletal: Normal spine alignment. Full range of motion all joints.
No contractures. Ankles dorsiflex to neutral with knee extended (no
Achilles tightening). No joint hypermobility.

Neurological Examination:
  Mental Status: Alert. Intermittently cooperative. As described in
    behavioral observations above.
  Cranial Nerves: II — visual tracking intact, visual fields appear full
    to confrontation. III/IV/VI — EOMI, no nystagmus, PERRL. V — facial
    sensation intact to light touch. VII — face symmetric at rest and with

    palate elevates symmetrically. XI — SCM and trapezius symmetric. XII —
    tongue midline, no fasciculations.
  Motor: Bulk normal throughout. Tone — examined in upper and lower
    extremities. Upper extremities: normal tone bilaterally. Lower
    extremities: tone appears within normal limits, though examination is
    somewhat limited by child's cooperation. [Note: Subtle hypotonia may
    be present but is difficult to ascertain definitively in a
    semicooperative 3-year-old. No frank hypotonia or hypertonia detected.]
  Reflexes: Biceps 2+, triceps 2+, brachioradialis 2+, patellar 2+,
    Achilles 2+ bilaterally. Symmetric. Plantar responses: flexor
    bilaterally. No clonus.
  Coordination: Reaches for objects accurately. Unable to cooperate with
    formal finger-to-nose or heel-to-shin testing due to age and
    developmental level. No obvious dysmetria on reaching.
  Gait: Wide-based. Intermittent bilateral toe-walking (approximately 30%
    of observed steps). Reduced arm swing. Occasional loss of balance with
    direction changes. No definitive ataxia, but gait pattern is mildly
    abnormal for age.
  Involuntary movements: No tremor. No chorea. No dystonia. Stereotypic
    hand-wringing as described.

DIAGNOSTIC FORMULATION AND IMPRESSIONS:

Based on comprehensive evaluation including clinical interview, behavioral
observation, standardized assessment, physical/neurological examination, and
neuroimaging, the following diagnostic impressions are offered:

1. AUTISM SPECTRUM DISORDER (ASD), DSM-5 299.00 (F84.0)
   Severity: Level 2 — Requiring Substantial Support

   Specifiers:
   - With accompanying intellectual impairment
   - With accompanying language impairment

   Justification: Eli meets DSM-5 criteria for ASD based on the following:

   Criterion A — Persistent deficits in social communication and social
   interaction across multiple contexts:
   A1. Deficits in social-emotional reciprocity: Limited social overtures,
       reduced sharing of interests and emotions, limited initiation of
       social interaction.
   A2. Deficits in nonverbal communicative behaviors: Poorly modulated eye
       contact, limited use of gestures (pointing for sharing absent),
       limited integration of verbal and nonverbal communication.
   A3. Deficits in developing, maintaining, and understanding relationships:
       Limited engagement with peers, absence of imaginative/pretend play,
       limited interest in social interaction beyond immediate needs.

   Criterion B — Restricted, repetitive patterns of behavior, interests, or
   activities:
   B1. Stereotyped or repetitive motor movements: Hand-wringing stereotypy
       is prominent and frequent.
   B2. Insistence on sameness: Not prominently observed during evaluation,
       though mother reports some preference for routine at home.
   B3. Highly restricted, fixated interests: Spinning wheels of toy car
       for extended periods. Limited play repertoire.
   B4. Hyper- or hyporeactivity to sensory input: Not prominently observed
       during evaluation.

   At least 2/4 B criteria met (B1, B3).

   Criterion C: Symptoms present in early developmental period — confirmed.
   Criterion D: Symptoms cause clinically significant impairment — confirmed.
   Criterion E: Not better explained by intellectual disability alone — the
   social communication deficits exceed what would be expected based on
   cognitive level alone. ADOS-2 comparison score of 7 supports moderate
   ASD symptom severity.

   Level 2 justification: Eli requires substantial support. He demonstrates
   marked deficits in verbal and nonverbal social communication, social
   impairments apparent even with supports in place, limited initiation of
   social interactions, and reduced or abnormal response to social overtures.
   Restricted/repetitive behaviors are frequent and obvious to the casual
   observer (hand-wringing) and interfere with functioning in multiple
   contexts.

2. INTELLECTUAL DISABILITY, MILD (F70)
   Bayley-III Cognitive Composite: 72 (borderline range)
   Vineland-3 Adaptive Behavior Composite: 67 (significantly below average)
   Functioning level is consistent with mild intellectual disability given
   scores approximating 2 standard deviations below the mean across both
   cognitive and adaptive measures.

3. MIXED RECEPTIVE-EXPRESSIVE LANGUAGE DISORDER (F80.2)
   Significant language delays in both receptive and expressive domains,
   beyond what is accounted for by cognitive level and ASD.

4. MOTOR DELAYS (R62.0) with intermittent toe-walking (R29.3)
   Gross motor more affected than fine motor. Wide-based gait. Etiology
   attributed to overall developmental profile in context of ASD with
   associated motor impairment.

5. STEREOTYPIC MOVEMENT DISORDER (noted as feature of ASD, Criterion B1)
   Hand-wringing is classified within the ASD diagnosis rather than as a
   separate stereotypic movement disorder, as it is best understood as a
   restricted/repetitive behavior within the autism presentation.

6. STARING SPELLS — ETIOLOGY UNCLEAR
   As detailed in behavioral observations, two episodes of brief
   disengagement (5-8 seconds) were observed. Clinical presentation is most
   consistent with dissociative/inattentive episodes as commonly seen in
   ASD. EEG not recommended at this time. Parents to continue monitoring
   and report any change in character.

RECOMMENDATIONS:

1. Applied Behavior Analysis (ABA) Therapy: 25-30 hours per week recommended.
   Eli's profile warrants intensive, evidence-based behavioral intervention
   targeting social communication, play skills, functional language, and
   reduction of interfering stereotypic behaviors. Recommend provider with
   experience in Level 2 ASD with co-occurring intellectual disability.

2. Speech-Language Therapy: Increase to 2 times per week (individual sessions,
   30-45 minutes each). Focus on functional communication, vocabulary
   expansion, requesting, and initiation of communication. Consider
   augmentative and alternative communication (AAC) — picture exchange system
   (PECS) or speech-generating device trial if verbal language does not
   progress within 3-6 months.

3. Occupational Therapy: Initiate OT 1 time per week to address fine motor
   development, sensory processing, and self-help skills (feeding, dressing).

4. Physical Therapy: Continue current PT services 1 time per week for gait
   training, balance, coordination, and toe-walking management. Consider
   serial casting or orthotics if toe-walking persists.

5. Special Education: Eli is eligible for special education preschool services
   under IDEA Part B (transitioning from Part C). Recommend placement in a
   structured special education preschool program with low student-to-teacher
   ratio, speech and language support embedded, and ASD-specific programming.
   An IEP should be developed immediately.

6. Neuroimaging: MRI brain completed — normal for age.

7. Follow-Up: Return to this clinic in 6 months for reassessment of progress
   and treatment response. May consider additional genetic or metabolic testing
   if progress is not commensurate with intervention intensity.

8. Family Support: Provided information on local ASD parent support groups
   (Autism Society of Central Virginia). Discussed available TRICARE benefits
   for ASD-related services (family has military insurance through father's
   active duty status).

This evaluation was discussed in detail with both parents. They expressed
understanding of the diagnosis and plan. Mother was tearful but stated she
"finally has an answer." Father asked about the cause — I explained that ASD
is a neurodevelopmental condition with likely genetic underpinnings, that it
is not caused by parenting or environmental factors, and that the cause in
most cases is not identifiable through a single test. I noted that genetic
testing (chromosomal microarray, Fragile X) could be considered but is
optional at this stage and can be discussed at the follow-up visit.

Report prepared by:

James R. Whitfield, MD, FAAP
Developmental-Behavioral Pediatrician
Children's Developmental Center of Virginia

6-MONTH FOLLOW-UP VISIT
Date of Service: October 7, 2015
Patient Age: 3 years, 6 months, 22 days
ICD-10: F84.0 (ASD), F70, F80.2, R29.3
CPT: 99214 (Established patient, moderate complexity)

SUBJECTIVE:
Mother brings Eli for 6-month follow-up after ASD diagnosis.

Services update:
- ABA therapy: Started June 2015 through Behavioral Pathways, Inc. Currently
  receiving 20 hours/week (unable to access full 25-30 recommended due to
  provider availability). Mother reports "mixed" progress. ABA supervisor
  reports Eli is "cooperative but makes inconsistent gains." Targets include
  requesting (manding), eye contact, imitation, and reducing hand-wringing.
  Manding with PECS has been partially introduced — Eli can exchange 1 picture
  card for a preferred item approximately 50% of trials. Verbal requesting
  has not increased.
- Speech therapy: 2x/week individual sessions. Therapist reports vocabulary
  has expanded minimally to approximately 28-30 words but spontaneous use
  remains limited. No consistent two-word combinations despite 6 months of
  focused intervention. Therapist has recommended AAC device trial.
- OT: 1x/week. Addressing fine motor and self-help skills. Some progress
  with spoon use and hand-over-hand dressing tasks.
- PT: 1x/week. Toe-walking persists. Gait remains wide-based.
- Special education preschool: Enrolled in district-based ASD classroom since
  August 2015 (beginning of school year). Teacher reports he is "quiet,
  compliant, but not engaging much with peers."

Mother concerns today:
- "The ABA isn't really working the way I thought it would. He's been doing it
  for 4 months and I don't see big changes."
- "The hand-wringing hasn't gotten better at all — if anything, it's worse."
- "He's still having those staring spells. I think they might be more frequent
  now — maybe 2-3 times a day." Mother reports spells are unchanged in
  character: 5-10 seconds, staring, unresponsive, then resumes activity.
- Mother asks directly: "Could these be seizures? I looked it up online and
  absence seizures look exactly like what Eli does."

Father is present at this visit. He is quieter but nods in agreement with
mother's concerns. He asks: "Is there something else going on besides autism?"

OBJECTIVE:
Vital Signs:
  Weight: 31 lbs 4 oz (14.17 kg) — 33rd percentile
  Height: 38.5 inches (97.8 cm) — 26th percentile
  Head Circumference: 49.5 cm — 28th percentile
  Temperature: 98.4 F axillary

General: Alert boy. Walking with wide-based gait. Toe-walking observed. Hand-
wringing observed 4 times during 30-minute visit. Eye contact fleeting.

Neurological: Tone — mildly reduced in lower extremities (more apparent today
than at initial evaluation — may be progressive or may represent better
cooperation with examination). Upper extremity tone normal. Reflexes 2+
symmetric. No clonus. Plantars flexor. Gait wide-based, intermittent toe-
walking. No ataxia per se but mild clumsiness with direction changes. Hand-
wringing prominent.

Language: Used "mama" and "no." No other words observed during visit. Made
non-specific vocalizations.

Social: Brief eye contact with examiner. More engaged with mother. Did not
initiate interaction with examiner.

ASSESSMENT:
1. ASD Level 2 with co-occurring mild intellectual disability — progress
   with intensive intervention has been modest. This is not unexpected at
   this stage. ABA benefit often takes 6-12 months to fully manifest.
2. Hand-wringing stereotypy — unchanged/possibly increased. This is a common
   and often persistent stereotypic behavior in ASD.
3. Staring episodes — parents continue to report. Frequency may be increasing
   (now 2-3/day per parent report). Mother specifically asks about absence
   seizures. Discussion with parents:

   "I understand your concern about seizures. The staring episodes Eli
   experiences are brief, non-convulsive, and he returns to baseline without
   confusion or distress. While absence seizures are in the differential,
   these episodes are consistent with dissociative or inattentive episodes
   that are commonly seen in children with Autism Spectrum Disorder.
   Children with ASD frequently experience brief episodes of apparent
   disengagement, particularly during periods of sensory overload or
   during transitions between activities. There is no clinical indication
   for epilepsy at this time, as the episodes are brief and non-convulsive
   with no associated motor phenomena. I would not recommend an EEG at this
   time. However, if the episodes change in character — for example, if
   they become longer, if you see eye rolling, lip smacking, or body
   stiffening, or if they increase significantly in frequency — please
   contact our office and we can reconsider."

4. Growth: Height percentile continues gradual decline (now 26th). Weight
   more stable. Head circumference 28th — also declining from earlier
   measurements. Pattern is notable and will continue to be monitored.

PLAN:
1. Continue ABA therapy — encourage increasing to full 25-30 hours/week if
   provider availability allows. Request updated treatment plan from BCBA.
   ABA program should incorporate specific protocols to address stereotypic
   hand-wringing through differential reinforcement of alternative behaviors
   (DRA) and response interruption/redirection (RIRD).
2. Continue speech therapy 2x/week. Support AAC device trial as recommended
   by SLP.
3. Continue OT and PT.
4. Continue special education preschool.
5. Staring spells: Continue monitoring. Diary maintenance. No EEG at this
   time.
6. Genetic testing: Discussed with parents. Offered chromosomal microarray
   and Fragile X testing. Parents defer at this time — "We want to see how
   he does with therapy first." Explained that genetic testing would not
   change the current treatment plan but could provide etiologic information.
   Parents to consider and may pursue at follow-up.
7. Parents report they are expecting a military reassignment (PCS) to Fort
   Wright, Ohio, likely in the next 3-6 months. Will prepare transfer of
   care documentation when timing is confirmed.
8. Follow-up: 6 months, or sooner if concerns escalate.

— James R. Whitfield, MD, FAAP

I am writing to provide a comprehensive summary of care for Eli Reeves, who is
transferring out of our area due to a military Permanent Change of Station
(PCS) to Fort Wright, Ohio. This letter summarizes his developmental history,
diagnostic evaluation, and current treatment plan.

DIAGNOSTIC SUMMARY:
Eli was evaluated at our center in April 2015 (age 3 years, 0 months) and
received the following diagnoses:

1. Autism Spectrum Disorder (ASD), DSM-5 299.00 (F84.0)
   Severity Level: Level 2 — Requiring Substantial Support
   With accompanying intellectual impairment
   With accompanying language impairment

2. Mild Intellectual Disability (F70)

3. Mixed Receptive-Expressive Language Disorder (F80.2)

4. Motor Delays with intermittent toe-walking

EVALUATION FINDINGS (April 2015):
- ADOS-2 Module 1: SA + RRB Total = 17 (exceeds Autism cutoff of 16).
  Comparison Score: 7.
- Bayley-III: Cognitive 72, Language 65, Motor 68.
- Vineland-3: Communication 68, DLS 72, Socialization 62, Motor 70.
  Adaptive Behavior Composite: 67.
- MRI Brain (April 2015): Normal for age.
- Basic metabolic panel, CBC, thyroid function, lead: All normal.
- Hearing screen: Passed (OAE).
- Genetic testing: Offered but deferred by parents.

KEY CLINICAL FEATURES:
Eli meets DSM-5 criteria for Autism Spectrum Disorder Level 2. His
presentation is characterized by:
- Significantly delayed expressive and receptive language (currently ~28-30
  words, no consistent phrases at age 3 years 10 months)
- Limited social communication (reduced eye contact, absent protodeclarative
  pointing, limited joint attention)
- Prominent hand-wringing stereotypy (repetitive, bilateral, rhythmic hand-
  wringing/washing movements occurring >10 times daily; this is the most
  prominent restricted/repetitive behavior in his presentation)
- Intermittent staring episodes (5-10 seconds, 2-3 times daily per parent
  report; clinically assessed as dissociative/inattentive episodes consistent
  with ASD rather than epileptiform activity; EEG not performed)
- Gross motor delays with wide-based gait and intermittent bilateral toe-
  walking
- Mild intellectual disability on formal testing

The hand-wringing stereotypies and intermittent disengagement episodes are
characteristic of his autism presentation. I recommend continuation of
intensive ABA therapy and speech-language services as the cornerstone of his
treatment program.

CURRENT SERVICES:
- ABA therapy: 20 hours/week (recommended 25-30 hours/week; increase if
  possible). Provider: Behavioral Pathways, Inc., Richmond, VA.
- Speech-Language Therapy: 2x/week individual sessions.
- Occupational Therapy: 1x/week.
- Physical Therapy: 1x/week.
- Special Education: Enrolled in ASD-specific preschool classroom through
  Richmond City Public Schools. Current IEP on file with school district.

RECOMMENDED NEXT STEPS:
1. Establish care with a developmental-behavioral pediatrician or pediatric
   neurologist in the receiving area for ongoing monitoring.
2. Continue ABA at maximum recommended intensity.
3. Continue speech therapy with consideration of AAC device trial if verbal
   language does not progress.
4. Continue OT and PT.
5. Monitor staring episodes. If they change in character, increase in
   frequency or duration, or if motor phenomena develop, EEG should be
   considered at that time.
6. Genetic testing (chromosomal microarray, Fragile X) may be considered
   at the discretion of the receiving provider. Parents have thus far
   deferred.
7. Monitor growth trajectory — percentiles have been gradually declining
   (height now ~26th percentile, head circumference ~28th). May warrant
   nutritional assessment if trend continues.
8. Next comprehensive developmental reassessment recommended at approximately
   age 4.5-5 years, or sooner if significant clinical changes occur.

PHYSICAL THERAPY INITIAL EVALUATION
Date of Service: October 15, 2013
Patient Age: 19 months, 30 days (approximately 20 months)
Evaluating Therapist: Karen Lindstrom, PT, DPT, PCS (Pediatric Certified
  Specialist)
ICD-10: R62.0 (Delayed milestone in childhood)
CPT: 97163 (PT evaluation, high complexity)

IDENTIFYING INFORMATION:
Eli Reeves is a 20-month-old male referred by his pediatrician (Dr. Sarah
Mitchell, Greenfield Pediatrics) through the Virginia Infant & Toddler
Connection for physical therapy evaluation due to gross motor delays.
Specifically, he is not yet walking independently at 20 months of age.

HISTORY:
Per parent report and medical records: Full-term birth, uncomplicated delivery.
Early motor milestones achieved within normal limits (rolling at 3.5 months,
sitting independently at 7 months). Crawling onset at 10.5 months. Pulling to
stand at 12 months. Cruising along furniture at approximately 14.5 months.
Currently cruising well but has not taken independent steps. Parents report he
will stand independently for 2-3 seconds before sitting down.

No history of regression in motor skills. No seizure history (parents report
brief "zoning out" episodes but these have not been formally evaluated). No
orthopedic history. No known genetic or metabolic conditions.

ASSESSMENT:

Gross Motor Evaluation (Peabody Developmental Motor Scales-2, Gross Motor
subtest):
  Gross Motor Quotient: 73 (Mean = 100, SD = 15)
  Age Equivalent: 13 months (chronological age: 20 months)
  Classification: Below Average

Specific findings:
- Stationary: Sits independently with good balance. Transitions between
  sitting and prone/supine smoothly. Pulls to stand on furniture. Stands
  holding on with one hand.
- Locomotion: Crawls reciprocally on hands and knees — well-coordinated.
  Cruises along furniture with lateral stepping pattern. Walks with two
  hands held. Walks with one hand held — emerging, inconsistent (takes 4-5
  steps then loses balance). NOT walking independently. NOT climbing stairs.
- Object Manipulation: Throws ball forward (underhand) — emerging. Kicks ball
  — not yet.

Gait Observation (with hand-held assistance):
  - Wide base of support (inter-malleolar distance approximately 6 inches —
    wider than expected for age)
  - Bilateral toe-walking observed on approximately 40% of steps during
    hand-held walking
  - Reduced arm swing
  - Trunk rotation diminished
  - Balance reactions (lateral protective) — delayed; compensatory stepping
    reactions inconsistent

Tone Assessment:
  - Upper extremities: Normal tone throughout range of motion.
  - Lower extremities: Mild hypotonia noted on passive assessment —
    particularly at the hip girdle. Decreased resistance to passive movement
    at hips and knees compared to age expectations.
  - Trunk: Mild axial hypotonia — reduced trunk stability during dynamic
    activities (e.g., reaching in standing).

Range of Motion:
  - Bilateral ankles: Full dorsiflexion to 15 degrees past neutral with knee
    extended and flexed. No Achilles contracture. Toe-walking appears habitual/
    behavioral rather than secondary to musculoskeletal restriction.

Strength:
  - Formal manual muscle testing not performed due to age. Functional
    strength assessment: hip abductors and extensors appear mildly weak
    based on Trendelenburg-type pattern observed during assisted walking.
    Quadriceps and ankle dorsiflexors appear functional.

Balance:
  - Static standing balance (with support): Good.
  - Dynamic balance: Poor. Loses balance with minimal perturbation in
    standing. Protective reactions (forward, lateral, backward) inconsistent
    and delayed.

CLINICAL IMPRESSION:
Eli presents with significant gross motor delay, with a gross motor age
equivalent of approximately 13 months at a chronological age of 20 months
(7-month delay). His motor profile is characterized by: (1) delayed
acquisition of independent walking; (2) wide-based gait pattern with
intermittent bilateral toe-walking; (3) delayed balance reactions; and
(4) mild generalized hypotonia, most prominent in axial and lower extremity
musculature. The hypotonia may be contributing to the wide-based gait pattern
as a compensatory mechanism for reduced postural stability.

GOALS (6-MONTH):
1. Eli will walk independently across a 15-foot room without hand-held
   assistance in 3 out of 5 trials.
2. Eli will demonstrate flat-footed walking >70% of the time during a
   10-minute observation period.
3. Eli will recover balance from mild lateral perturbation in standing
   without falling in 4 out of 5 trials.
4. Eli will climb 3 stairs with one hand on railing and minimal assist.

PLAN:
Direct PT services 1x/week, 45-minute sessions.
Treatment will include:
- Weight-bearing and balance activities in standing
- Gait training with progressive reduction of support
- Heel cord stretching and sensory input to feet to discourage toe-walking
- Hip and core strengthening through functional play activities
- Balance reaction training
- Parent education on home exercise program

— Karen Lindstrom, PT, DPT, PCS

Date of Service: October 17, 2013
Patient Age: 20 months 2 days
Evaluating Therapist: Angela Torres, MS, CCC-SLP
ICD-10: F80.2 (Mixed receptive-expressive language disorder)
CPT: 92523 (SLP evaluation, comprehensive)

IDENTIFYING INFORMATION:
Eli Reeves is a 20-month-old male referred for speech-language evaluation
through Virginia Infant & Toddler Connection due to concerns about delayed
language development. Referral from Dr. Sarah Mitchell, Greenfield Pediatrics.

HISTORY:
Per parent report and medical records: First words appeared at approximately
12 months ("mama," "dada," "no"). Vocabulary slowly expanded to approximately
12 words by 18 months. Mother reports vocabulary growth has slowed in recent
months. No two-word combinations. Uses some gestures (reaching, inconsistent
waving). Does not consistently point to share interest. Sometimes does not
respond to name. Hearing screen passed at 6 months (OAE).

ASSESSMENT:

Preschool Language Scales, Fifth Edition (PLS-5):
  Auditory Comprehension Standard Score: 74 (Mean = 100, SD = 15)
    Age Equivalent: 14 months
    Percentile Rank: 4th
  Expressive Communication Standard Score: 68 (Mean = 100, SD = 15)
    Age Equivalent: 12 months
    Percentile Rank: 2nd
  Total Language Standard Score: 70 (Mean = 100, SD = 15)
    Percentile Rank: 2nd
    Classification: Significantly Below Average

MacArthur-Bates Communicative Development Inventory (CDI) — Words and Gestures
(completed by mother):
  Words Understood: 85 (below 10th percentile for 20 months)
  Words Produced: 14 (below 5th percentile for 20 months)
  Total Gestures: 28/63 (below 10th percentile for 20 months)

Oral-Motor Examination:
  Oral structures: Within normal limits. Lips, tongue, palate, dentition —
  all normal. Tongue mobility adequate. No drooling. Oral-motor coordination
  for feeding — adequate per parent report (eating table foods, using sippy
  cup). Velopharyngeal function appears adequate. No oral-motor apraxia
  suspected at this time, though assessment is limited by age and cooperation.

Speech Sound Inventory:
  Consonant sounds produced during evaluation: /m/, /b/, /d/, /n/. Limited
  consonant repertoire. Vowel production appears adequate. Syllable shapes:
  primarily CV (e.g., "ma," "ba," "no") and CVCV reduplicative (e.g., "mama,"
  "dada"). No consonant clusters. No final consonants observed.

Pragmatic/Social Communication Observations:
  - Eye contact: Present but inconsistent.
  - Requesting: Reaches toward desired objects. Occasional use of "more" in
    context. Does not yet point to request consistently.
  - Protodeclarative pointing (pointing to share interest): ABSENT.
  - Joint attention (following point): Inconsistent — follows point
    approximately 40% of attempts.
  - Showing/giving objects: Not observed.
  - Response to name: Inconsistent (3/6 trials).
  - Turn-taking in vocal play: Emerging — occasional vocal back-and-forth
    but brief.

CLINICAL IMPRESSION:
Eli presents with a mixed receptive-expressive language delay, with both
comprehension and production significantly below age expectations. Expressive
language is more severely affected than receptive. His vocabulary of
approximately 12-14 words at 20 months is significantly below the expected
50th percentile of approximately 50 words. Social communication skills are
also delayed, with absent protodeclarative pointing and inconsistent joint
attention. These social communication deficits raise concern for a possible
autism spectrum condition, which should be evaluated by a developmental
specialist.

GOALS (6-MONTH):
1. Eli will expand expressive vocabulary to at least 30 words used
   spontaneously across settings.
2. Eli will use pointing (index finger) to request desired items in 4 out
   of 5 opportunities.
3. Eli will respond to his name by orienting toward the speaker in 4 out
   of 5 trials.
4. Eli will follow one-step commands without gestural cues in 3 out of 5
   trials.
5. Eli will initiate a communicative act (verbal or gestural) to gain
   adult attention at least 5 times per 30-minute session.

PLAN:
Direct speech-language therapy services 2x/week, 30-minute sessions.
Treatment will include:
- Vocabulary expansion through naturalistic and structured activities
- Prompting and reinforcing communicative intent (pointing, gestures, words)
- Joint attention and social communication activities
- Receptive language activities (following commands, identifying objects)
- Parent coaching for language-rich home environment

— Angela Torres, MS, CCC-SLP

PHYSICAL THERAPY PROGRESS SUMMARY
Reporting Period: October 2013 — April 2014 (Ages 20-25 months)
Therapist: Karen Lindstrom, PT, DPT, PCS
ICD-10: R62.0
CPT: 97110, 97530, 97116 (weekly sessions)

PATIENT: Eli Reeves | DOB: 03/15/2012 | MRN: VITC-R-2013-5582

ATTENDANCE: 22 of 24 scheduled sessions attended (2 cancellations — illness).

PROGRESS TOWARD GOALS:

Goal 1: Independent walking across 15-foot room — PARTIALLY MET.
  Eli began taking independent steps at approximately 22-23 months (December
  2013). By 25 months, he is walking independently across a room but with
  significant qualitative concerns. He can walk 15+ feet independently but
  requires 1-2 rest stops (pauses, stands still, then resumes). He falls
  approximately 2-3 times per 30-minute session, typically when turning or
  navigating around obstacles.

Goal 2: Flat-footed walking >70% of time — NOT MET.
  Toe-walking persists at approximately 30-40% of observed steps. There has
  been no significant change in toe-walking frequency despite targeted
  interventions including heel cord stretching, sensory-based foot
  activities (walking on varied textures), and ankle-foot orthosis trial
  (family declined consistent use after 2-week trial — Eli protested wearing
  the braces and mother found them difficult to manage with shoes).

Goal 3: Balance recovery from lateral perturbation — PARTIALLY MET.
  Eli can recover from very mild perturbation but continues to fall with
  moderate perturbation. Balance reactions remain delayed relative to age.
  Lateral protective extension is present but slow and inconsistent.

Goal 4: Stair climbing — NOT MET.
  Eli will ascend 2 stairs with two hands held but is not yet using a
  railing independently for stair climbing.

CURRENT FUNCTIONAL STATUS:
Eli is walking independently as of approximately 25-26 months of age. His
gait pattern, however, remains qualitatively abnormal:
  - Wide base of support (inter-malleolar distance ~5 inches)
  - Intermittent bilateral toe-walking (~30-40% of steps)
  - Reduced arm swing
  - Mildly unsteady with frequent stumbles and falls
  - Difficulty with direction changes and obstacle navigation
  - Cannot run
  - Cannot jump
  - Stair climbing requires significant assistance

Tone continues to be mildly reduced in lower extremities and trunk. No
change in tone assessment from initial evaluation.

THERAPIST'S IMPRESSION:
Eli has made progress in the primary goal of achieving independent ambulation,
which is a significant functional milestone. However, the quality of his gait
remains concerning. The persistent wide-based pattern, ongoing toe-walking,
and balance deficits suggest underlying neuromotor dysfunction beyond simple
motor delay. The mild hypotonia, delayed balance reactions, and gait
abnormalities could be consistent with a central nervous system etiology
rather than isolated developmental motor delay. I have shared these
observations with the referring physician.

RECOMMENDATIONS:
1. Continue PT 1x/week, 45-minute sessions.
2. Updated goals to focus on gait quality, stair climbing, running, jumping,
   and balance.
3. Re-evaluate need for ankle-foot orthoses if toe-walking persists beyond
   age 3.
4. Recommend discussion with developmental pediatrician regarding etiology
   of motor findings.

— Karen Lindstrom, PT, DPT, PCS

SPEECH-LANGUAGE THERAPY PROGRESS SUMMARY
Reporting Period: October 2013 — April 2014 (Ages 20-25 months)
Therapist: Angela Torres, MS, CCC-SLP
ICD-10: F80.2
CPT: 92507 (treatment sessions, 2x/week)

PATIENT: Eli Reeves | DOB: 03/15/2012 | MRN: VITC-R-2013-5582

ATTENDANCE: 44 of 48 scheduled sessions attended (4 cancellations).

PROGRESS TOWARD GOALS:

Goal 1: Expand expressive vocabulary to 30 words — PARTIALLY MET.
  Eli's vocabulary has expanded from approximately 14 words at initial
  evaluation to approximately 22-25 words per parent report and clinical
  observation. New words added include: eat, milk, book, baby, hi, hot,
  cookie, and "uh-oh." However, the rate of acquisition has slowed over the
  past 2 months. He acquired approximately 6-8 words in the first 3 months
  of therapy but only 2-3 in the most recent 3-month period. Spontaneous
  use of words remains limited — he uses approximately 8-10 words
  consistently and the remainder are used only with prompting or in specific
  contexts. He does not use words to comment or share — word use is primarily
  for requesting.

Goal 2: Pointing to request — PARTIALLY MET.
  Eli is now using an index finger point to request desired items in
  approximately 2 out of 5 opportunities (40%). He still frequently uses
  reaching or whole-hand gestures rather than a specific point. Proto-
  declarative pointing (pointing to share interest or show something) remains
  ABSENT.

Goal 3: Respond to name — PARTIALLY MET.
  Response to name has improved to approximately 3 out of 5 trials (60%),
  up from approximately 50% at intake. However, this remains inconsistent
  and below age expectations.

Goal 4: Follow one-step commands — PARTIALLY MET.
  Eli follows simple one-step commands (e.g., "give me the cup," "sit down")
  in approximately 3 out of 5 trials during structured activities. He
  requires repetition and gestural cues in most instances. He is beginning
  to identify some body parts (points to nose and eyes with ~50% accuracy).

Goal 5: Initiate communicative acts — MINIMALLY MET.
  Eli initiates communicative acts approximately 2-3 times per 30-minute
  session, primarily through reaching, occasional pointing, and infrequent
  word use. He does not yet initiate communication to share attention,
  comment, or engage socially — initiation is limited to basic requesting.

THERAPIST'S IMPRESSION:
Eli has made inconsistent progress in speech-language therapy over the past
6 months. While his vocabulary has expanded modestly, the rate of acquisition
has decelerated rather than accelerated, which is atypical. In typically
developing children, vocabulary growth accelerates rapidly between 18-24
months (the "word explosion"), and Eli has not demonstrated this pattern. His
spontaneous use of language remains significantly limited, and his social
communication profile — including absent protodeclarative pointing, limited
joint attention, and restricted communicative functions (requesting only, no
commenting or sharing) — continues to raise concern for autism spectrum
disorder. The limited and decelerating progress despite consistent, evidence-
based intervention is concerning and suggests that his language delay may be
part of a broader neurodevelopmental condition rather than an isolated speech-
language disorder.

RECOMMENDATIONS:
1. Continue speech-language therapy 2x/week.
2. Updated goals with emphasis on functional communication across settings.
3. Begin introducing PECS (Picture Exchange Communication System) as an
   augmentative communication strategy given limited verbal progress.
4. Strongly recommend comprehensive developmental evaluation to clarify
   diagnostic picture and guide intervention.
5. Consider AAC device trial if verbal language does not show meaningful
   gains in the next 3-6 months.

— Angela Torres, MS, CCC-SLP

RECORD 3.1 — INTAKE BEHAVIORAL ASSESSMENT
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

DATE OF ASSESSMENT:  05/18/2016
CLIENT AGE:          4 years, 2 months
ASSESSMENT TYPE:     Comprehensive Behavioral Intake Assessment
ASSESSMENT TOOL:     Verbal Behavior Milestones Assessment and Placement Program
                     (VB-MAPP), Sundberg 2008
REFERRING PROVIDER:  Dr. Marcus Whitfield, MD — Blue Ridge Developmental
                     Pediatrics, Roanoke, VA
REFERRAL DIAGNOSIS:  Autism Spectrum Disorder, Level 2 — Requiring Substantial
                     Support (ICD-10: F84.0)
ASSESSMENT SETTING:  Buckeye Behavioral Health clinic, therapy room 3
ASSESSMENT DURATION: 90 minutes (direct observation + structured probes)
PARENT PRESENT:      Yes — mother (Denise Reeves)

I. REASON FOR REFERRAL

Eli Reeves is a 4-year, 2-month-old biracial male referred for Applied Behavior
Analysis (ABA) therapy by Dr. Marcus Whitfield of Blue Ridge Developmental
Pediatrics, Roanoke, VA. Eli was diagnosed with Autism Spectrum Disorder, Level 2,
at age 3 years, 6 months following developmental pediatrics evaluation. The family
recently relocated to Columbus, OH due to parental employment. Mother reports
that Eli received approximately 4 months of ABA therapy in Virginia prior to the
move but services were interrupted. Transfer records from Blue Ridge ABA Services
(Roanoke) were requested but had not been received at the time of this
assessment.

Per parent interview, primary concerns include: limited verbal communication
(approximately 15-20 words used inconsistently), repetitive hand-wringing
movements, toe-walking, "staring spells" described as brief episodes of
unresponsiveness, difficulty with peer interaction, and need for support in
adaptive daily living skills.

II. BEHAVIORAL OBSERVATION DURING ASSESSMENT

Eli was accompanied by his mother to the assessment. He did not acknowledge the
assessor upon introduction and did not make eye contact during the initial
greeting. He walked independently into the therapy room with a mildly wide-based
gait and intermittent toe-walking (estimated 40% of observed steps were on toes).

Throughout the 90-minute assessment, the following behaviors were observed:

  - Hand-wringing/hand-washing stereotypy: 14 discrete episodes observed
    (averaging >10 per 30-minute interval), each lasting 3-8 seconds. Movements
    were rhythmic, bilateral, and occurred at midline. They appeared involuntary
    and did not appear to serve a communicative or self-stimulatory function in
    the typical manner observed in our ASD population.

  - Toe-walking: Present approximately 40% of ambulatory time. Bilateral.

  - Disengagement episodes: 4 episodes observed during the 90-minute assessment.
    Duration ranged from 5 to 15 seconds. During these episodes, Eli ceased all
    motor activity, appeared to stare blankly ahead with a slight upward gaze
    deviation, and was unresponsive to his name or tactile prompts. Following
    each episode, he appeared briefly disoriented (looking around the room as if
    reorienting) before resuming activity. No convulsive movements were observed.
    Mother confirmed these episodes are consistent with what she observes at
    home, stating: "He does that all the time — we call it zoning out."

  - Verbal output: During the assessment, Eli produced 6 recognizable words
    ("mama," "no," "juice," "more," "go," "bye") in appropriate but limited
    contexts. No multi-word combinations were observed. Echolalia was minimal
    (2 instances of immediate echolalia). No delayed echolalia observed.

  - Social engagement: Eli did not initiate any social bids toward the assessor.
    He responded to his name 3 of 10 trials (30%). He oriented briefly toward
    the assessor when preferred items (bubbles, light-up toy) were presented but
    did not sustain joint attention beyond 2-3 seconds.

  - Fine motor: Eli demonstrated a crude palmar grasp on crayons. He was able
    to stack 4 blocks (mother reports he could stack 6 blocks three months ago).
    He successfully buttoned one large button on a dressing frame with moderate
    physical effort and 2 attempts.

  - Gross motor: Gait was wide-based with intermittent toe-walking as noted.
    Eli navigated the room independently. He climbed onto a child-sized chair
    without assistance. He fell once during a transition from sitting on the
    floor to standing.

III. VB-MAPP MILESTONE ASSESSMENT — RESULTS

V. CLINICAL IMPRESSIONS

Eli presents with significant skill deficits across all VB-MAPP domains,
consistent with his diagnosis of ASD Level 2. His overall milestone score of
31.5/170 places him primarily within the Level 1 range (developmental equivalent
of approximately 0-14 months across domains), which represents a substantial
delay for his chronological age of 4 years, 2 months.

Client demonstrates limited manding (requesting), with most mands consisting of
reaching toward desired items or leading an adult by the hand. Manding with
words was observed in only 2 instances during the assessment. Tacting (labeling)
was minimal, with Eli labeling only 3 items during structured probes (ball,
cup, bubbles). No intraverbal skills (answering questions, fill-ins, or
conversational exchanges) were observed.

Stereotypic behaviors include hand-wringing (>10 episodes per 30-minute
observation period), toe-walking, and brief apparent disengagement episodes
(4 observed during 90-minute assessment, lasting 5-15 seconds each). The
hand-wringing is notably persistent and appears qualitatively different from
typical hand-flapping stereotypy commonly observed in our ASD client population —
it is more rhythmic and washing-like in quality. The disengagement episodes are
also noteworthy; while inattention and "tuning out" are common in ASD, the
abruptness of onset, apparent unresponsiveness to stimuli, and brief post-episode
confusion are somewhat atypical.

Assessment is consistent with ASD Level 2 diagnosis per referral documentation
from Dr. Whitfield. Eli is an appropriate candidate for intensive ABA therapy
targeting manding, tacting, listener responding, imitation, and adaptive skills.

VI. TREATMENT RECOMMENDATIONS

1. Intensive ABA therapy: 25 hours/week (5 hours/day, 5 days/week)
2. Primary focus areas:
   a. Mand training using naturalistic environment teaching (NET) and
      discrete trial training (DTT)
   b. Tact training with errorless learning procedures
   c. Listener responding expansion
   d. Motor imitation building
   e. Reduction of stereotypic behaviors through differential reinforcement
3. Data collection: Continuous frequency data on target behaviors, ABC data
   on disengagement episodes
4. Parent training: Monthly parent training sessions (2 hours/month)
5. Coordination with speech-language pathology and occupational therapy
6. Progress review: Monthly by BCBA, formal progress reports quarterly
7. Re-assessment: VB-MAPP re-administration at 6-month intervals

Tact                              2.0              2.5             +0.5
Listener Responding               5.0              4.0             -1.0*
Visual Perceptual / Match         5.5              4.5             -1.0*
Independent Play                  3.0              3.0              0.0
Social Behavior                   1.0              1.0              0.0
Motor Imitation                   4.0              3.5             -0.5*
Echoic                            2.5              2.5              0.0
----------------------------------------------------------------------
* Indicates score decrease (regression)

IV. NOTABLE CLINICAL OBSERVATIONS

Client has made minimal progress across all domains during this reporting
period. Of the 5 treatment goals, 2 show minimal progress, 2 show regression,
and 1 shows no change. This rate of progress is below expectations for a client
receiving 25 hours/week of intensive ABA services.

Of particular note:

  1. Listener Responding Regression: Several previously mastered listener
     responding tasks are now inconsistent. At intake, Eli could reliably
     match 4 colors (red, blue, yellow, green) on a field of 3 with 90%
     accuracy. He now matches only 2 colors (red, blue) with 75% accuracy.
     Receptive identification of body parts has also declined from 5 (head,
     eyes, nose, mouth, hands) to 3 (head, hands, mouth).

  2. Motor Imitation Decline: Eli could imitate 6 gross motor actions at
     intake. He now consistently imitates only 5, and accuracy has declined
     from 70% to 65%. Fine motor imitation (stacking patterns, placing pegs)
     has also declined.

  3. Disengagement Episodes: Frequency has remained stable at approximately
     4-6 per 3-hour session. Duration appears unchanged (5-15 seconds).
     Post-episode confusion continues to be observed.

BCBA hypothesizes that the regression in listener responding and visual
perceptual matching may be due to motivational factors, environmental change
(new home, new therapy setting, new therapists), or the transition period
associated with the family's interstate move. It is also possible that the
structured demands of intensive ABA are creating escape-motivated behavior
that presents as skill loss. RBT has been instructed to vary reinforcement
schedules and conduct preference assessments more frequently.

Plan:
  - Modify reinforcement strategies; conduct weekly preference assessments
  - Reduce demand level on regressed tasks, rebuild using errorless teaching
  - Continue data collection on disengagement episodes
  - Schedule parent conference to discuss progress concerns

Jennifer Mallory, M.Ed., BCBA

I. SUMMARY OF PROGRESS

This report documents a continued pattern of skill regression that is

reduction in demand levels, and consultation with the clinical supervisor

  Baseline (05/16): 2 vocal mands

All domains show decline. Total VB-MAPP milestone score has decreased by

IV. CRITICAL CLINICAL OBSERVATIONS

The following observations represent significant clinical concerns:

  1. LOSS OF FINE MOTOR SKILLS: Tasks mastered during month 2 of therapy
     (placing pegs in pegboard, stacking 5 blocks, turning pages of a book)
     are no longer performed. Eli's fine motor capabilities have declined
     measurably since intake. He now has difficulty with pincer grasp tasks
     that were within his repertoire at the start of services. Buttoning,
     which was independently performed at intake, now requires full physical
     assistance.

  2. GROSS MOTOR COORDINATION DECLINE: Gross motor coordination appears to
     have declined substantially. Eli now falls frequently during transitions
     between activities — an average of 3.2 falls per 3-hour session over
     the past month (compared to 0.8 falls per session during month 1).
     His gait has become more visibly unsteady, with increased lateral sway.
     He now requires hand-held assistance on the 3 steps leading to the
     outdoor play area, which he previously navigated independently.

  3. DISENGAGEMENT EPISODES — INCREASED FREQUENCY AND DURATION:
     Disengagement episodes have increased in both frequency and duration:

       Period         Avg per 3-hr session    Avg duration
       --------------------------------------------------------
       Month 1        4-6                     5-15 seconds
       Month 3        5-7                     8-15 seconds
       Month 6        8-10                    10-20 seconds

     Of significant concern: following these episodes, Eli now appears
     briefly confused and takes 30-60 seconds to re-engage with the
     therapeutic activity. During re-engagement, he often looks around the
     room as if unfamiliar with his surroundings. On two occasions this
     month, RBT Deshawn Carter observed what appeared to be subtle lip-
     smacking movements during an episode.

  4. PARENT REPORT: Mother (Denise Reeves) reports that Eli's nighttime
     sleep has become disrupted. He wakes 2-3 times per week appearing
     "terrified and confused," sometimes crying inconsolably for 10-15
     minutes. Mother also reports increased daytime staring episodes at
     home, consistent with what is observed in the clinic.

V. BCBA CLINICAL CONCERNS AND RECOMMENDATIONS

This assessor discussed clinical observations with supervisor Dr. Amy
Okonkwo, Ph.D., BCBA-D on 11/15/2016. The pattern of progressive skill
loss across all domains, combined with the changing character of the
disengagement episodes, is atypical for our ASD client population. In this
clinician's experience with over 40 ASD clients, loss of previously
acquired skills of this magnitude over a 6-month period is not consistent
with typical ASD behavioral variability or adjustment-related regression.

Additionally, the disengagement episodes we are observing share features
with absence seizures as described in clinical literature, including:
abrupt onset, unresponsiveness to stimuli, brief duration, and post-
episode confusion. The emerging lip-smacking automatism further raises
concern for seizure activity.

RECOMMENDATIONS:
  1. Medical follow-up for possible seizure activity — recommend neurology
     referral and EEG evaluation
  2. Medical evaluation for underlying cause of progressive skill regression
  3. Continue ABA services pending medical evaluation
  4. Formal letter to be sent to pediatrician (Dr. Patricia Huang)
     documenting clinical concerns

RECORD 3.4 — BCBA LETTER TO DR. PATRICIA HUANG, PEDIATRICIAN
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

                        BUCKEYE BEHAVIORAL HEALTH
                     1847 Scioto Crossing Boulevard
                        Columbus, OH 43215
                   Phone: (614) 555-0192 Fax: (614) 555-0193

December 2, 2016

Patricia Huang, MD
Columbus Children's Medical Group
2900 East Broad Street, Suite 210
Columbus, OH 43209

RE:    Eli Reeves (DOB: 03/15/2012)
       Client ID: BBH-2016-0847

Dear Dr. Huang,

We are writing regarding our client Eli Reeves, whom we have been providing
intensive Applied Behavior Analysis (ABA) therapy since May 23, 2016. Eli was
referred to our center with a diagnosis of Autism Spectrum Disorder, Level 2,
and has been receiving 25 hours per week of ABA services under the supervision
of a Board Certified Behavior Analyst.

We wish to bring to your attention a pattern of clinical observations that we
believe warrants medical evaluation.

SKILL REGRESSION

Over the course of 6 months of intensive ABA therapy, Eli has demonstrated a
clear and measurable pattern of skill regression across all developmental
domains assessed by the VB-MAPP. His total milestone score has declined from
31.5 at intake to 22.5 at his most recent assessment — a 28.6% decrease. This
pattern is atypical for our ASD client population. In our clinical experience,
clients receiving intensive ABA services either make progress or plateau; a
sustained, multi-domain regression of this magnitude is unusual and, in our
professional opinion, is not adequately explained by motivational factors,
environmental change, or behavioral variability.

Specific areas of regression include:
  - Fine motor: Loss of independent buttoning (present at intake, now absent).
    Decline in pincer grasp strength and accuracy.
  - Gross motor: Increased frequency of falls (0.8/session at intake to
    3.2/session currently). Requires hand-held assistance on stairs that were
    previously navigated independently. Gait has become more unsteady.
  - Communication: Decrease in vocal mand repertoire (4 mands at 3-month
    review, now 2 vocal mands). No new tacts acquired.
  - Cognitive: Loss of color matching (4 colors to 2), loss of body part
    identification (5 to 3).

DISENGAGEMENT EPISODES

We have observed recurrent episodes of apparent disengagement that have
increased in frequency and duration over the course of treatment:

  - Intake (May 2016): 4-6 episodes per 3-hour session, lasting 5-15 seconds
  - Current (November 2016): 8-10 episodes per 3-hour session, lasting
    10-20 seconds

During these episodes, Eli ceases all motor activity, appears to stare
blankly (sometimes with slight upward gaze deviation), and is unresponsive
to his name, clapping, or light touch. Following each episode, he appears
disoriented for 30-60 seconds before resuming activity. On two recent
occasions, subtle lip-smacking movements were observed during the episodes.

These episodes share features with absence seizures as described in clinical
literature. While we recognize that inattention and "tuning out" can occur in
autism spectrum disorder, the clinical presentation — including the abrupt
onset, complete unresponsiveness, post-episode confusion, increasing frequency,
and emerging oral automatisms — is, in our assessment, not consistent with
simple behavioral inattention.

RECOMMENDATIONS

We respectfully recommend the following:
  1. Neurological evaluation, including EEG, to rule out seizure disorder
  2. Medical evaluation for potential underlying causes of progressive
     developmental regression
  3. We are happy to provide any additional behavioral data or clinical
     observations that may be helpful in your evaluation

We appreciate your attention to these concerns and are available to discuss
our observations at your convenience.

Respectfully,

_____________________________________
Jennifer Mallory, M.Ed., BCBA
Board Certified Behavior Analyst
License #OH-BCBA-2014-1192

Amy Okonkwo, Ph.D., BCBA-D
Clinical Director, Buckeye Behavioral Health

  Status: NOT MET — CONTINUED REGRESSION
