IV. CRITICAL CLINICAL OBSERVATIONS

  1. FINE MOTOR: Eli demonstrates a clear pattern of progressive fine motor
     skill loss that is not consistent with typical ASD behavioral
     trajectories. He can no longer button independently (skill present at
     intake). Difficulty with pincer grasp tasks has worsened — he now uses
     a raking grasp for small objects and frequently drops items. He can no
     longer place pegs in a pegboard (mastered during month 2 of therapy).
     Writing/drawing: at intake, Eli could produce a crude circular scribble;
     he now makes only brief, irregular marks and frequently loses grip on
     the crayon.

  2. GROSS MOTOR: Gait has become significantly more ataxic over the past
     6 months. Eli now walks with a wide base and notable lateral instability.
     He requires hand-held assistance on stairs (previously independent
     with railing). Falls have increased to an average of 4.8 per 3-hour
     session. Running is no longer observed — Eli was able to run (clumsily
     but independently) at intake; he now does not attempt to run. He
     requires assistance transitioning from floor to standing.

  3. DISENGAGEMENT EPISODES — CONTINUED ESCALATION:

       Period              Avg per 3-hr session    Avg duration
       -------------------------------------------------------
       Intake (05/16)      4-6                     5-15 seconds
       3-month (08/16)     5-7                     8-15 seconds
       6-month (11/16)     8-10                    10-20 seconds
       12-month (05/17)    12-15                   10-25 seconds

     Episodes are now sometimes accompanied by subtle bilateral eye
     fluttering lasting 2-5 seconds. Post-episode confusion has become
     more pronounced — Eli now takes 1-2 minutes to re-engage following
     longer episodes. On 3 occasions during this period, Eli slumped
     slightly in his chair during an episode, suggesting possible brief
     loss of postural tone.

  4. PARENT REPORT: Mother reports continued nighttime wakings (now 4-5
     times per week). Eli is "harder to wake up in the morning" and has
     decreased appetite. Mother reports that family members who had not
     seen Eli for several months were "shocked" by his decline. Maternal
     grandmother stated: "Something is wrong — this isn't just autism."

V. CLINICAL ASSESSMENT AND RECOMMENDATIONS

Client demonstrates a clear pattern of skill loss that is not consistent
with typical ASD behavioral trajectories. Over 12 months of intensive ABA
services (approximately 560 hours), Eli has not acquired any new skills and
has lost the majority of skills present at intake. This degree of regression
cannot be attributed to motivational factors, demand avoidance, environmental
adjustment, or typical ASD behavioral variability.

This clinician reiterates and strengthens the recommendation made in our
letter to Dr. Huang dated December 2, 2016: Eli requires comprehensive
medical and neurological evaluation. The progressive nature of the skill
loss across motor, communicative, and cognitive domains, combined with the
escalating disengagement episodes (which this clinician believes are
consistent with seizure activity), suggests an underlying medical condition
that may be separate from or in place of the ASD diagnosis.

We strongly recommend:
  1. Urgent neurology referral with EEG
  2. Comprehensive medical workup for causes of progressive developmental
     regression
  3. Consideration of metabolic, genetic, or neurodegenerative etiologies

I. DISCHARGE SUMMARY

Eli Reeves received 14 months of intensive ABA therapy (25 hours/week
authorized) at Buckeye Behavioral Health. Services were provided by RBT
Deshawn Carter under the supervision of BCBA Jennifer Mallory, M.Ed., with
clinical oversight by Dr. Amy Okonkwo, Ph.D., BCBA-D.

II. TREATMENT OUTCOMES

Client did not achieve any VB-MAPP milestones during 14 months of intensive
ABA therapy. A progressive decline in previously acquired skills was observed
across all developmental domains:

                              Intake Score    Discharge Score    Change
                              (05/2016)       (07/2017)

----------------------------------------------------------------------

III. CLINICAL STATEMENT

This pattern of sustained, progressive, multi-domain skill regression over
14 months of intensive behavioral intervention is inconsistent with the
expected trajectory for ABA therapy in autism spectrum disorder. In the
clinical experience of this BCBA (8 years, >40 ASD clients) and the
supervising BCBA-D (15 years, >150 ASD clients), we have not observed a
pattern of this nature in a client whose primary diagnosis is ASD.

Clients with ASD receiving intensive ABA therapy typically demonstrate either
measurable skill acquisition or plateau. Sustained regression of this
magnitude — particularly the progressive loss of motor skills (fine and
gross), the escalating disengagement episodes with seizure-like features,
and the decline in cognitive/perceptual skills — warrants comprehensive
medical and neurological evaluation to identify a possible underlying
neurodegenerative, metabolic, or genetic condition.

IV. REFERRAL AND COORDINATION

    documenting concerns — copy in chart
  - Verbal communication with Dr. Huang's office on 01/15/2017 and
    04/10/2017 reiterating concerns
  - Neurology referral reported by parent to be pending
  - Complete therapy records, data sheets, and VB-MAPP assessments
    released to parent (Denise Reeves) upon signed authorization

V. DISCHARGE RECOMMENDATIONS

  1. Comprehensive neurological evaluation including EEG
  2. Medical workup for progressive developmental regression
  3. Continued speech-language and occupational therapy services to
     maintain current skill levels
  4. If medical evaluation identifies a treatable condition and Eli
     stabilizes or improves, ABA services may be reconsidered
  5. Transition planning with school district for appropriate educational
     placement and supports

SUBJECT: Incident Report — Eli Reeves (DOB: 03/15/2012, Grade: 1st)

Dear Dr. Huang,

I am writing to inform you of an incident that occurred today during
Eli Reeves' first-grade classroom session.

At approximately 10:15 AM, Eli's 1:1 aide (Ms. Thompson) reported that
Eli suddenly stopped responding during a tabletop activity. Per the aide's
report and my own observation upon arrival to the classroom (approximately
2 minutes after onset):

  - Eli was seated in his adapted chair at his desk
  - He stopped all movement and became completely unresponsive
  - His eyes appeared to flutter rapidly for approximately 10-15 seconds
  - The episode lasted approximately 30 seconds total
  - When he "came back," he did not appear to know where he was for
    about one minute — he looked around the room with a confused
    expression and did not respond to his name or to Ms. Thompson
  - He then began to cry quietly for approximately 2 minutes
  - After approximately 3-4 minutes total, he returned to his baseline
    level of alertness and engagement
  - No convulsive movements were observed
  - He did not fall from his chair
  - His color remained normal throughout

This is the third such episode observed at school this month. Previous
episodes occurred on 11/05/2018 and 11/09/2018, each lasting approximately
15-25 seconds with similar features. Staff are concerned that these may
be seizures.

We have documented these episodes in Eli's school health file. His IEP
team has been notified.

We are requesting medical guidance regarding:
  1. Whether these episodes are seizures
  2. What school staff should do when an episode occurs
  3. Whether a seizure action plan should be developed for school
  4. Whether any medication changes or evaluations are pending

Thank you for your attention to this matter.

Sincerely,
Karen Delgado, RN, BSN
School Nurse, Maple Grove Elementary

---

DR. HUANG'S RESPONSE (faxed 11/19/2018):

Dear Ms. Delgado,

Thank you for your detailed report regarding Eli Reeves. Your observations
are consistent with the episodes his mother has been reporting and that
I have witnessed in the office.

Eli has a history of these staring/unresponsive episodes. He was evaluated
by a neurologist (Dr. Robert Chen, Ohio Neurology Associates) in April
2018. An MRI of the brain was performed and did not show acute pathology.
The neurologist assessed the episodes as likely behavioral in nature in
the context of his autism diagnosis.

However, given the increasing frequency and the features you describe
(eye fluttering, post-episode confusion, crying), I share your concern
that these may represent seizure activity. I will recommend follow-up
with the neurologist regarding the increased frequency of episodes and
will request EEG evaluation.

In the meantime, please implement the following school seizure
precautions:
  1. If an episode occurs, ensure Eli is in a safe position (seated or
     supported). Do not restrain him.
  2. Time the episode from onset to resolution.
  3. Note any motor features (eye fluttering, twitching, stiffening).
  4. Note duration of post-episode confusion.
  5. Document and report to parent and this office.
  6. If any episode involves convulsive movements lasting >2 minutes,
     loss of consciousness with fall, or failure to return to baseline
     within 5 minutes — call 911.

I will work with the family to develop a formal seizure action plan
once neurology follow-up has occurred.

Thank you for your vigilance and thorough documentation.

Sincerely,
Patricia Huang, MD
Columbus Children's Medical Group

HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS:
Eli Reeves is a 6-year, 1-month-old biracial male with a diagnosis of

  is unresponsive to name or touch. Post-episode confusion lasts 1-2
  minutes. Mother has recently observed occasional brief arm twitching
  (2-3 seconds) at the end of some episodes. One episode was witnessed by
  Dr. Huang during the 03/2018 well-child visit.

  Nocturnal events: Eli wakes 3-5 nights per week appearing frightened and
  disoriented. Episodes typically occur 1-3 hours after sleep onset.
  Duration of confusion/distress: 10-20 minutes per mother. No witnessed
  convulsive movements during nocturnal events, though mother acknowledges
  she is not always present at onset.

  Mother's concerns: "Something else is going on besides autism. He is
  losing abilities. Autistic kids are supposed to get better with therapy,
  not worse. I think the staring spells are seizures."

PAST MEDICAL HISTORY:
  - ASD Level 2 (diagnosed age 3.5, Virginia)
  - Late walking (26 months)
  - MRI brain age 3: reportedly normal (report not available)
  - No hospitalizations, no surgeries

NEUROLOGICAL EXAMINATION:

  Mental Status: Eli is alert but appears less vigilant than expected for
  age. He is seated in his mother's lap. He does not acknowledge the
  examiner or respond to verbal greeting. He does not make eye contact.
  He is cooperative with the examination in a passive manner — does not
  resist but does not actively participate. Limited verbal interaction:
  said "mama" once during the 40-minute encounter. Did not follow any
  verbal commands during the examination. Continuous bilateral hand-
  wringing stereotypy observed throughout.

  Cranial Nerves:
    CN II: Pupils equal, round, reactive to light bilaterally (3mm to
      2mm). Visual fields: blinks to threat bilaterally, suggesting
      intact peripheral fields grossly. Fundoscopic examination attempted
      but limited by cooperation — brief view of right disc appeared
      normal, left disc not adequately visualized.
    CN III, IV, VI: Extraocular movements — eyes track a bright toy
      through full horizontal range. Vertical tracking limited by
      cooperation. No nystagmus observed in primary gaze or with
      lateral gaze. No ptosis.
    CN V: Facial sensation appears intact — withdraws to light touch
      bilaterally. Masseter strength not formally tested.
    CN VII: Face symmetric at rest and with crying (cried briefly during
      fundoscopic attempt). Mild drooling noted — may suggest subtle
      oromotor weakness.
    CN VIII: Turns to sound bilaterally (clapped hands). Formal
      audiometry not performed.
    CN IX, X: Palate elevates symmetrically. Gag reflex present
      bilaterally. Voice (limited vocalizations): no hoarseness noted.
    CN XI: SCM and trapezius strength not formally tested — appears
      symmetric.
    CN XII: Tongue midline on protrusion (brief, observed during cry).
      No fasciculations.

    Bulk: Mildly decreased muscle bulk globally, particularly in
      distal lower extremities.
    Tone:
      Upper extremities: Decreased tone (hypotonia) bilaterally.
        Mild but definite.
      Lower extremities: Mildly increased tone (spasticity) bilaterally,
        greater in ankle plantarflexors. Velocity-dependent. Bilateral
        ankle equinus contracture present.
    Strength: Formal manual muscle testing not possible due to limited
      cooperation. Observed functional strength:
      - Able to lift arms against gravity and sustain briefly (4/5
        estimated)
      - Able to kick legs against gravity (4/5 estimated)
      - Grip: weak, unable to sustain grasp on examiner's finger for
        >2 seconds
      - No obvious asymmetry

    Biceps:     2+ bilaterally
    Triceps:    2+ bilaterally
    Patellar:   3+ bilaterally (brisk)
    Achilles:   3+ bilaterally (brisk), with 2-3 beats of clonus
                bilaterally (unsustained)
    Plantar:    Equivocal bilaterally (difficult to interpret given
                contracture and withdrawal response)

  Coordination:
    Finger-nose testing attempted: Eli reached toward the examiner's
    finger with a toy as target. Marked dysmetria observed — overshooting
    by approximately 3-4 cm on multiple attempts. Intention tremor
    noted in the terminal phase of reaching.
    Poor pincer grasp for age — unable to pick up a small bead; uses
    crude raking grasp.
    Rapid alternating movements: not testable due to cooperation.

  Gait:
    Observed with AFOs in place and mother holding one hand. Wide-based
    gait with significant lateral sway. Steps are short and shuffling.
    Bilateral toe-walking despite AFOs. Occasional foot-dragging noted,
    left > right. Eli took approximately 15 steps before requiring a
    rest. Without mother's hand, took 2 steps then lost balance
    (caught by mother).
    Heel walking: unable.
    Tandem gait: unable.
    Romberg: unable to assess due to cooperation and inability to stand
    with feet together.

  Stereotypic hand-wringing movements observed continuously throughout
  the examination. These are rhythmic, bilateral, and occur at midline.
  They appear involuntary and are not suppressible with distraction.

  During the examination, one staring episode was witnessed. At
  approximately the 25-minute mark, Eli suddenly ceased hand-wringing,
  became motionless, and stared ahead with subtle upward gaze deviation.
  Episode lasted approximately 12 seconds. He was unresponsive to
  name, clapping, and light touch on the shoulder during the episode.
  Following the episode, he appeared confused — looked around the room,
  appeared not to recognize his surroundings, and took approximately
  60 seconds to resume baseline behavior (hand-wringing, looking around).
  No convulsive movements were observed during this episode.

ASSESSMENT:

Eli is a 6-year, 1-month-old male with a diagnosis of ASD Level 2 who
presents with parental and provider concerns regarding developmental
regression and recurrent staring spells.

Neurological examination is notable for:
  1. Generalized mild hypotonia (upper > lower)
  2. Mild spasticity in lower extremities with ankle equinus contracture
  3. Brisk patellar and Achilles reflexes with unsustained ankle clonus
  4. Dysmetria and intention tremor on reaching
  5. Wide-based, ataxic gait requiring assistive support
  6. Poor fine motor skills with weak grip
  7. Continuous stereotypic hand-wringing
  8. Minimal verbal output
  9. Witnessed staring episode with post-episode confusion

Regarding the staring spells: The differential diagnosis includes absence
seizures versus behavioral inattention/dissociation, which is commonly
observed in patients with ASD. The episode witnessed during today's visit
was brief (12 seconds), non-convulsive, and self-limited. The post-episode
confusion, while concerning, can also be seen with behavioral dissociative
episodes in neurodevelopmental disorders. Given the brief, non-convulsive
nature, I will obtain an MRI of the brain to evaluate for structural causes
of the regression and staring spells. If the MRI is unrevealing and
episodes worsen (increased frequency, increased duration, or development
of convulsive features), I will proceed with EEG evaluation.

The developmental regression is concerning but may be attributable to the
natural history of ASD in the setting of moderate-severe intellectual
disability. Some degree of skill loss or plateau is not uncommon in this
population, particularly in the context of limited therapeutic response.

ICD-10 CODES:
  F84.0  — Autistic disorder
  R40.4  — Transient alteration of awareness (staring spells)
  R62.59 — Other and unspecified lack of expected normal physiological
            development (developmental regression)
  R26.0  — Ataxic gait
  G80.0  — Spastic diplegic cerebral palsy — rule out (pending imaging)

PLAN:
  1. MRI Brain without and with contrast — ordered (scheduling to follow).
     Evaluate for structural abnormalities, white matter disease,
     posterior fossa pathology, or other causes of regression and ataxia.
  2. Laboratory studies: not ordered at this time. Basic labs (CBC, lead)
     recently normal per Dr. Huang. If MRI demonstrates abnormalities
     suggestive of metabolic disease, will order targeted metabolic workup.
  3. EEG: will consider if MRI is unrevealing and staring spells worsen
     or develop convulsive features. At this time, the brief, non-
     convulsive nature of the episodes does not mandate emergent EEG.
  4. Return to clinic after MRI for review of results and further planning.
  5. In the interim: continue current supports (AFOs, school services,
     dysphagia precautions). If any convulsive seizure activity or
     prolonged episode (>2 minutes), present to ED.

  - Sagittal T1-weighted (pre- and post-contrast)
  - Axial T1-weighted (pre- and post-contrast)
  - Axial T2-weighted
  - Axial FLAIR (fluid-attenuated inversion recovery)
  - Axial DWI (diffusion-weighted imaging) with ADC map
  - Axial SWI (susceptibility-weighted imaging)
  - Coronal T2-weighted

COMPARISON: No prior imaging available at this facility. Per clinical
history, patient reportedly had an MRI at age 3 (2015) at an outside
facility that was normal. Outside images were not available for comparison.

FINDINGS:

  Ventricles and CSF Spaces:
    - Lateral ventricles are mildly prominent, left slightly greater than
      right. The ventricular configuration is smooth without irregular
      margins. The frontal horns, bodies, and occipital horns are mildly
      dilated.
    - Third ventricle: upper limits of normal in size (6 mm).
    - Fourth ventricle: normal in size and configuration.
    - No periventricular signal abnormality on FLAIR to suggest
      transependymal CSF flow or acute hydrocephalus.
    - Cerebral sulci are within normal limits for age.

  Cerebral Hemispheres:
    - Gray-white matter differentiation is preserved.
    - No focal signal abnormality in the cerebral white matter on T2
      or FLAIR sequences.
    - No restricted diffusion on DWI/ADC to suggest acute ischemia.
    - No abnormal enhancement following contrast administration.
    - Cortical thickness and gyral pattern appear normal.
    - No heterotopia or cortical malformation identified.

  Cerebellum:
    - Cerebellar hemispheres appear grossly normal in signal intensity.
    - Cerebellar folia are within normal limits for age.
    - No focal lesion.
    - Vermis is intact.
    NOTE: In retrospective review, cerebellar volume may be at the lower
    end of normal for age; however, without prior comparison imaging
    available at this facility, this cannot be definitively assessed as
    abnormal. The cerebellar appearance is within the range of normal
    variation for a 6-year-old.

  Brainstem:
    - Normal in size and signal intensity.
    - No focal lesion.

  Corpus Callosum:
    - Intact. Normal thickness and signal intensity throughout.

  Sella/Pituitary:
    - Normal.

  Extra-axial Spaces:
    - No extra-axial collection or mass.
    - No midline shift.

  Skull and Orbits:
    - Normal calvarium. No fracture.
    - Orbits are normal. Globes are symmetric.

  Vascular:

  Post-Contrast:

IMPRESSION:

RE:    Eli Reeves (DOB: 03/15/2012)

Dear Dr. Huang,

Thank you for referring Eli Reeves to our practice for evaluation of
developmental regression and staring spells in the context of autism
spectrum disorder.

I evaluated Eli on April 10, 2018. Neurological examination was notable for
generalized mild hypotonia in the upper extremities, mild spasticity in the
lower extremities with ankle equinus contracture, brisk lower extremity
reflexes with unsustained ankle clonus, and an ataxic, wide-based gait
requiring support. Fine motor skills were impaired, with poor pincer grasp
and weak grip. Continuous stereotypic hand-wringing was observed. Verbal
output was markedly limited (one word during the encounter). One staring
episode was witnessed during the visit, lasting approximately 12 seconds,
without convulsive features, with brief post-episode confusion.

MRI of the brain performed on April 24, 2018 demonstrates mildly prominent
lateral ventricles that are most likely a developmental variant. There is
no evidence of acute intracranial pathology, white matter disease,
posterior fossa abnormality, or mass lesion. The MRI does not demonstrate
a structural cause for the developmental regression or staring episodes.

Given the absence of structural pathology on imaging and the well-
established diagnosis of autism spectrum disorder, the staring spells
are most likely behavioral in nature — representing episodes of inattention
or dissociation that are commonly observed in individuals with ASD and
intellectual disability. The developmental regression, while concerning, may
reflect the natural trajectory of ASD with moderate intellectual disability,
where some skill loss or plateau can occur particularly in the context of
environmental change and limited therapeutic response.

I recommend continued monitoring of the staring episodes. If the episodes
increase significantly in frequency or duration, if any convulsive activity
is observed (tonic stiffening, clonic jerking, loss of consciousness with
fall), or if the family or school reports a marked escalation in symptoms,
please re-refer for EEG evaluation. At this time, given the clinical
presentation and normal structural imaging, I do not believe emergent EEG
is indicated.

If the developmental regression continues to progress, consideration may
be given to metabolic or genetic evaluation, though the current clinical
picture is most consistent with ASD-related regression.

I appreciate the opportunity to participate in Eli's care. Please do not
hesitate to contact my office if further questions or concerns arise.

Sincerely,

     (loud noises, crowded spaces) but is generally calm in structured,
     predictable environments.

  C. Motor Skills:
     Fine motor: Eli demonstrates a crude palmar grasp on writing
     instruments. He can stack 3-4 blocks. He can turn pages of a board
     book (one at a time, with effort). He can button large buttons on a
     dressing frame (inconsistent — succeeds approximately 60% of
     attempts). He has difficulty with small manipulatives (beads, pegs).
     Per OT assessment: fine motor skills approximately 18-24 month level.

     Gross motor: Eli walks independently with a wide-based gait and
     intermittent bilateral toe-walking. He navigates stairs with railing
     and step-to pattern. He falls approximately 2-3 times per school day
     during transitions. He does not run smoothly — attempts to run
     result in stumbling. He can climb onto child-sized furniture. He
     does not jump, hop, or skip.
     Per OT assessment: gross motor skills approximately 20-26 month level.

  D. Adaptive Behavior:
     Eli requires assistance with most self-care tasks. He can wash his
     hands with 1-2 verbal prompts. He feeds himself with a spoon (some
     spillage). He drinks from an open cup with occasional spilling. He
     is not toilet trained. He requires assistance with dressing but can
     pull up elastic-waist pants with help and button large buttons
     (inconsistently). He does not manage zippers or snaps.

  E. Cognitive/Academic:
     Eli matches 4 colors (red, blue, yellow, green) on a field of 3.
     He identifies 5 body parts (head, eyes, nose, mouth, hands). He
     sorts objects by one attribute (color or shape, not both). He does
     not identify letters, numbers, or shapes by name. He does not count
     objects. He attends to structured activities for approximately
     2-3 minutes before disengaging.
     Per school psychologist assessment: cognitive functioning estimated
     in the 60-75 range (mild to moderate intellectual disability).

  F. Health/Medical:
     Per school nurse and parent report: Eli experiences staring episodes
     (4-6 per day, lasting 5-15 seconds) with brief post-episode
     confusion. Medical evaluation is ongoing. No formal seizure diagnosis.
     See Health Plan.
     Bilateral toe-walking — no orthotics at this time.
     Behavioral health: ABA therapy at Buckeye Behavioral Health
     (25 hrs/week, outside school hours).

II. MEASURABLE ANNUAL GOALS

  Goal 1 — Communication (Expressive):
  By 11/13/2017, Eli will use words, signs, or PECS to request (mand)
  10 preferred items/activities across at least 3 different settings
  (classroom, lunch, playground) with 80% accuracy over 3 consecutive
  data collection days.
  Baseline: 4-6 mands using words or signs

  Goal 3 — Social Skills:

IV. ACCOMMODATIONS AND MODIFICATIONS

  1. Visual schedule displayed at student's desk and reviewed at each
     transition
  2. Sensory breaks every 30 minutes or as needed (sensory room or quiet
     area)
  3. Reduced auditory input during instruction (preferential seating,
     noise-reducing headphones available)
  4. Modified materials (larger manipulatives, adaptive writing tools)
  5. Extended time for all tasks and transitions
  6. 1:1 adult support for all transitions, including hallway and
     bathroom
  7. Supervision near stairs due to fall risk
  8. Modified lunch setup (chair with lateral supports for positioning)
  9. Communication board with picture symbols available at all times
  10. If staring episode occurs: ensure safety, do not restrain, time
      episode, document and report to school nurse

  identified supports and services, the inclusive preschool setting is
  the least restrictive environment appropriate for Eli at this time.

RECORD S.3 — SCHOOL PSYCHOLOGIST RE-EVALUATION REPORT
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

                    FRANKLIN COUNTY EDUCATIONAL SERVICE CENTER
                    MAPLE GROVE ELEMENTARY SCHOOL
                    PSYCHOLOGICAL EVALUATION REPORT

STUDENT:              Eli Reeves
DOB:                  03/15/2012
AGE AT TESTING:       6 years, 0 months
GRADE:                Kindergarten
DATE(S) OF TESTING:   03/12/2018 and 03/14/2018
EVALUATOR:            Theresa Williams, Ph.D.
                      Licensed School Psychologist (#OH-PSY-2010-4817)
REFERRAL SOURCE:      IEP Team (Annual Review, 01/22/2018)
REASON FOR REFERRAL:  Comprehensive re-evaluation due to significant
                      developmental regression across all domains observed
                      during the 2016-2017 and 2017-2018 school years.

I. BACKGROUND INFORMATION

Eli Reeves is a 6-year, 0-month-old biracial (Black/White) male currently
enrolled in kindergarten at Maple Grove Elementary School. He receives
special education services under the disability category of Autism, with
an IEP in place since November 2016.

Eli was diagnosed with Autism Spectrum Disorder, Level 2, at age 3 years,
6 months by Dr. Marcus Whitfield at Blue Ridge Developmental Pediatrics
in Roanoke, Virginia. At that time, a Bayley Scales of Infant and Toddler
Development, Third Edition (Bayley-III) was administered (age 3 years,
5 months), yielding the following composite scores:
  - Cognitive: 72 (3rd percentile — borderline)
  - Language: 62 (1st percentile — extremely low)
  - Motor: 68 (2nd percentile — extremely low)

The family relocated to Columbus, Ohio in May 2016. Eli received intensive
ABA therapy (25 hrs/week) at Buckeye Behavioral Health from May 2016 to
July 2017. Per ABA discharge summary, Eli did not make progress and
demonstrated "progressive decline in previously acquired skills across
all developmental domains."

Since enrollment at Maple Grove Elementary (Fall 2016), the IEP team has
documented progressive regression in communication, motor, adaptive, and
cognitive skills. Eli has not met any IEP goals and has lost multiple
baseline skills. The IEP team requested this re-evaluation to determine
current cognitive functioning and to investigate the nature and extent of
the regression.

II. EVALUATION PROCEDURES

The following assessments and procedures were utilized:

  1. Wechsler Intelligence Scale for Children, Fifth Edition (WISC-V)
     — partial administration
  2. Vineland Adaptive Behavior Scales, Third Edition (Vineland-3)
     — Parent/Caregiver Rating Form (completed by Denise Reeves)
  3. Review of educational records, IEP documentation, and ABA therapy
     records
  4. Behavioral observations during testing
  5. Teacher interview (Lauren Mitchell, M.Ed.; Brian Kowalski)
  6. Parent interview (Denise Reeves)

III. BEHAVIORAL OBSERVATIONS DURING TESTING

Testing was conducted over two sessions (03/12/2018 and 03/14/2018)
in the school psychology office. Eli was accompanied to the testing room
by his 1:1 aide (Ms. Thompson). He walked with a wide-based, unsteady
gait; the aide held his hand for support.

Eli did not acknowledge the examiner or make eye contact during either
session. He sat in an adapted chair with lateral supports. Continuous
bilateral hand-wringing was observed throughout both sessions.

Eli's verbal output during testing was extremely limited — he said "no"
twice and "mama" once across both sessions. He did not respond to verbal
directions or questions from the examiner.

Multiple staring episodes were observed: 6 episodes across the two
testing sessions (total testing time approximately 90 minutes). Episodes
lasted 10-25 seconds. During episodes, Eli became completely motionless
and unresponsive. Eye fluttering was observed during 3 of the 6 episodes.
Post-episode, Eli appeared disoriented for 1-3 minutes. These episodes
significantly impacted the testing process.

Eli demonstrated very limited engagement with test materials. He
occasionally looked at picture stimuli but did not point, verbalize, or
otherwise indicate responses to most items. When physically guided to
manipulate blocks or objects, he had difficulty maintaining grasp and
dropped materials frequently.

Given the significant limitations in Eli's ability to engage with
standardized testing procedures, only a partial WISC-V administration was
possible. Results should be interpreted as an ESTIMATE of cognitive
functioning and may UNDERESTIMATE Eli's true abilities due to the
confounding effects of motor impairment, communication limitation, and
possible seizure activity during testing.

IV. COGNITIVE ASSESSMENT — WISC-V (PARTIAL ADMINISTRATION)

The following subtests were attempted:

  Subtest                  Attempted    Completed    Scaled Score
  ----------------------------------------------------------------
  Block Design             Yes          Partial      (1)*
  Similarities             Yes          No           Unable to score
  Matrix Reasoning         Yes          Partial      (2)*
  Digit Span               Yes          No           Unable to score
  Coding                   Yes          No           Unable to score
  Vocabulary               Yes          No           Unable to score
  Figure Weights           No           ---          ---
  Visual Puzzles           Yes          Partial      (1)*
  Picture Span             Yes          Partial      (2)*
  Symbol Search            Yes          No           Unable to score

  * Scores in parentheses represent estimated scaled scores based on
    partial administration. These are approximations only.

  Subtests not completed due to: inability to provide verbal responses
  (Similarities, Vocabulary); inability to manipulate pencil for timed
  tasks (Coding, Symbol Search); inability to sustain attention due to
  staring episodes (Digit Span — episode occurred during administration).

  ESTIMATED COMPOSITE SCORES (based on available subtests — interpret
  with extreme caution):

  Index                              Estimated Score    Percentile    CI (95%)
  ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
  Full Scale IQ (FSIQ)               58*               0.3           52-67
  Visual Spatial Index (VSI)          55*               0.1           49-66
  Fluid Reasoning Index (FRI)         60*               0.4           54-70
  * Prorated/estimated based on partial administration. These scores do
    NOT meet standard administration criteria and should be interpreted
    as approximate estimates of cognitive functioning only.

  CLASSIFICATION: Estimated FSIQ of 58 falls in the Extremely Low range
  (Intellectual Disability — Moderate).

V. ADAPTIVE BEHAVIOR — VINELAND-3 (Parent/Caregiver Rating Form)

  Completed by: Denise Reeves (mother)
  Date completed: 03/10/2018

  Domain                    Standard Score    Percentile    Adaptive Level
  ------------------------------------------------------------------------
  Communication                  42              <1         Low
    Receptive                    8 (v-scale)
    Expressive                   6 (v-scale)
    Written                      5 (v-scale)
  Daily Living Skills            48              <1         Low
    Personal                     7 (v-scale)
    Domestic                     6 (v-scale)
    Community                    5 (v-scale)
  Socialization                  46              <1         Low
    Interpersonal Relationships  6 (v-scale)
    Play and Leisure Time        5 (v-scale)
    Coping Skills                7 (v-scale)

    Fine Motor                   5 (v-scale)
  ------------------------------------------------------------------------
  Adaptive Behavior Composite:   43              <1         Low

  Adaptive Behavior Composite of 43 falls in the Extremely Low / Low
  range, consistent with moderate-to-severe adaptive behavior deficits.

VI. COMPARISON WITH PRIOR COGNITIVE ASSESSMENT

  Assessment        Age       Instrument     Cognitive Score    Classification
  ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
  2015 (Virginia)   3y 5m     Bayley-III     72 (Cognitive)     Borderline
  2018 (current)    6y 0m     WISC-V (est.)  58 (FSIQ est.)     Extremely Low

  SCORE CHANGE: Decline of approximately 14 standard score points
  (approximately 1 standard deviation) over a 2.5-year period.

  INTERPRETATION: While some degree of score difference between the
  Bayley-III and WISC-V is expected due to differences in test construction,
  norms, and the skills assessed, the magnitude of the decline observed
  here (>1 SD) is unusual and exceeds what would be expected from
  instrument differences alone. Research on Bayley-III to WISC-V prediction
  suggests that Bayley cognitive scores tend to slightly overestimate later
  IQ scores by approximately 3-7 points in children with developmental
  delays. Even accounting for this, the observed decline of 14 points is
  notable and suggests genuine loss of cognitive abilities.

  This pattern of cognitive decline, combined with the documented regression
  in motor, communication, and adaptive skills across ABA therapy records,
  IEP progress monitoring, and clinical observation, represents a
  significant and concerning trajectory.

VII. SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS

Eli Reeves is a 6-year-old male with a diagnosis of Autism Spectrum
Disorder who demonstrates significant cognitive, communicative, motor,
and adaptive deficits. Current estimated cognitive functioning falls in
the Extremely Low range (estimated FSIQ 58), representing a meaningful
decline from his Bayley-III cognitive score of 72 obtained 2.5 years ago.

Of primary concern is the pattern of progressive skill regression
documented by multiple sources (ABA therapy, IEP progress data, parent
report, clinical observation) across all developmental domains. This
regression is significant in both scope (affecting motor, communication,
cognitive, and adaptive domains simultaneously) and magnitude (loss of
multiple previously acquired skills including buttoning, stair navigation,
color matching, and multiple receptive language tasks).

While some degree of regression can occur in some presentations of autism
spectrum disorder — particularly during periods of transition or stress,
and some ASD subtypes (e.g., regressive autism) involve an early period
of skill loss typically occurring between 15-24 months — the ongoing,
progressive, multi-year pattern of skill loss observed in Eli is atypical
for ASD. In most cases of regressive autism, the regression occurs in early
toddlerhood and is followed by a plateau or gradual skill re-acquisition,
not by continued progressive decline through school age.

The school psychologist notes the following concerns that warrant
medical investigation:

  1. The progressive, multi-domain nature of the regression (motor,
     cognitive, communicative, adaptive) occurring over a 2+ year period
     and continuing at age 6 years
  2. The significant decline in cognitive scores (estimated 14-point
     decrease) exceeding what would be expected from assessment instrument
     differences
  3. The staring episodes observed during testing, which have features
     concerning for seizure activity (abrupt onset, unresponsiveness,
     eye fluttering, post-episode confusion)
  4. The progressive motor deterioration (gait instability, loss of fine
     motor skills, emerging spasticity per medical records) which is not
     a typical feature of ASD

RECOMMENDATIONS:

  1. EDUCATIONAL: Change in disability category should be considered.
     While Autism remains appropriate as a primary category, the IEP team
     should consider adding "Multiple Disabilities" or "Traumatic Brain
     Injury/Other Health Impairment" as the presentation evolves and
     additional medical information becomes available.

  2. EDUCATIONAL: Change in placement recommended — from inclusive
     kindergarten with 1:1 aide to self-contained special education
     classroom (multiple disabilities unit) with reduced student-to-
     teacher ratio and access to related services within the classroom.

  3. MEDICAL: Comprehensive medical evaluation is strongly recommended
     to investigate the cause of the progressive developmental regression.
     While this school psychologist is not qualified to make medical
     diagnoses, the pattern observed is, in this evaluator's professional
     opinion, atypical for ASD alone and warrants investigation for
     possible neurological, metabolic, or genetic conditions that may be
     contributing to or causing the regression.

  4. RELATED SERVICES: Increase in speech-language therapy, occupational
     therapy, and addition of physical therapy services recommended to
     address declining motor function.

  5. ASSISTIVE TECHNOLOGY: Formal assistive technology evaluation
     recommended given declining communication and motor skills.

  6. RE-EVALUATION: If medical evaluation reveals an underlying condition,
     re-evaluation of educational classification and services should be
     conducted promptly.

                     END OF MEDICAL RECORDS — SECTION 2
                    PATIENT: ELI REEVES (DOB: 03/15/2012)
                         AGES 4 YEARS – 7 YEARS
                    COVERING DATES: 05/2016 – 03/2019

  SECTION SUMMARY:
  This section documents the critical period during which Eli Reeves
  received 14 months of intensive ABA therapy with no progress and
  progressive regression, was evaluated by a pediatrician who monitored
  but deferred, and was seen by a neurologist who ordered MRI but not
  EEG. Multiple providers (BCBA, school psychologist, school nurse)
  raised concerns about possible seizure activity and atypical
  regression. The ASD diagnosis was never questioned despite mounting
  evidence of a progressive neurological process.

  KEY MISSED OPPORTUNITIES IN THIS SECTION:
  1. EEG was never ordered despite multiple providers observing and
     documenting episodes consistent with absence seizures
  2. Neurologist accepted ASD diagnosis and attributed regression to
     ASD natural history
  3. MRI read as "mildly prominent ventricles — likely normal variant"
     without comparison to age-3 MRI (not available at Ohio facility);
     possible early cerebellar volume loss was not appreciated
  4. No metabolic workup was ordered despite progressive multi-domain
     regression
  5. No genetic testing was considered
  6. Pediatrician deferred to neurology; neurologist deferred further
     workup pending "worsening" — creating a gap in which no one
     pursued the underlying cause
  7. Wait times for specialty care (3-6 months for neurology) resulted
     in significant delays during a period of rapid decline

  TOTAL RECORDS IN SECTION 2: 20 documents from 4 providers + school
  NEXT SECTION: Section 3 — Ages 7-10 (Ohio, continued)

RECORD 6.1 — ORTHOPEDIC INITIAL CONSULTATION
Date of Service:    July 22, 2019
Patient Age:        7 years, 4 months
Visit Type:         New Patient Consultation
Referring Provider: Patricia Huang, MD (Pediatrics)
ICD-10:             M41.114 (Juvenile idiopathic scoliosis, thoracic region)
                    F84.0 (Autism spectrum disorder)
                    R26.0 (Ataxic gait)

──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

                    NATIONWIDE CHILDREN'S HOSPITAL
                Department of Orthopedic Surgery
                    Pediatric Spine & Gait Clinic

HISTORY OF PRESENT ILLNESS:
Eli is a 7-year-old mixed-race male referred by Dr. Patricia Huang (Pediatrics)
for evaluation of progressive gait abnormality and concern for developing
scoliosis. Per mother, Danielle Reeves, Eli was diagnosed with autism spectrum
disorder at age 3 and has had progressive motor difficulties over the past
2–3 years. She reports he was walking independently by 15 months but began
stumbling frequently around age 5. Falls have increased to 3–4 times daily.
He now requires assistance on stairs and uneven surfaces. Mother states she
has noticed his spine appears "curved" when bathing him, which prompted the
referral.

Mother reports Eli has also had worsening speech, now limited to short 2–3
word phrases, and episodes of "staring" that occur 2–3 times per day lasting
10–20 seconds. She is increasingly concerned that his difficulties extend
beyond his ASD diagnosis.

PAST MEDICAL HISTORY:
  - Autism spectrum disorder (diagnosed age 3, Dr. Yuen, Nationwide Children's)
  - Speech/language delay
  - Gross motor delay
  - History of frequent falls

PAST SURGICAL HISTORY:
  - None

MEDICATIONS:
  - Melatonin 3 mg QHS (sleep)
  - Multivitamin daily

ALLERGIES:  NKDA

FAMILY HISTORY:
  - Mother: healthy, age 31
  - Father: healthy, age 33
  - No known family history of scoliosis or neuromuscular disease
  - Maternal grandmother: type 2 diabetes
  - No consanguinity

SOCIAL HISTORY:
Patient lives with mother and father. Attends 2nd grade at Columbus City
Schools with IEP and 1:1 aide. Receives school-based OT and speech therapy.

REVIEW OF SYSTEMS:
  Constitutional:   Decreased energy, tires easily. Weight stable.
  HEENT:            No visual complaints per parent, last eye exam 6 months ago
  Musculoskeletal:  Progressive gait difficulty, frequent falls, trunk asymmetry
  Neurological:     Staring episodes, speech regression, clumsiness
  All other systems reviewed and negative

PHYSICAL EXAMINATION:
  Vitals:
    Height:         119.4 cm (10th percentile)
    Weight:          21.3 kg (8th percentile)
    BMI:             14.9 (32nd percentile)

  General:          Thin male, cooperative but limited verbal interaction.
                    Fidgeting in chair. Intermittent hand-wringing movements.

  Spine:
    - Standing forward bend test: mild rightward thoracic prominence
    - Scoliometer reading: 8 degrees at T8 level
    - No palpable midline defect
    - No skin stigmata (café-au-lait spots, hairy patch, dimple)
    - Shoulder heights asymmetric, right slightly elevated
    - Pelvis level

  Extremities:
    - Full ROM bilateral upper and lower extremities
    - No limb length discrepancy
    - Bilateral pes planus, moderate
    - Hindfoot valgus bilaterally, left > right
    - No joint contractures

  Gait:
    - Wide-based gait
    - Truncal sway prominent
    - Difficulty with tandem gait — unable to complete
    - Mild circumduction of left lower extremity
    - Romberg: positive (sways significantly with eyes closed)

  Neurological (screening):
    - Tone: mildly increased bilateral lower extremities
    - Strength: grossly 4/5 bilateral LE, 4+/5 bilateral UE
    - DTRs: 2+ bilateral upper extremities, 3+ bilateral LE (brisk)
    - Babinski: equivocal bilaterally
    - Finger-to-nose: mild dysmetria bilaterally
    - Heel-to-shin: poor tracking bilaterally

IMAGING:
  PA and lateral standing scoliosis radiographs obtained today:
    - Right thoracic curve measuring 12 degrees (Cobb angle), apex T8
    - No left lumbar compensatory curve
    - Risser sign: 0 (skeletally immature)
    - No congenital vertebral anomalies identified
    - No pedicle widening or canal abnormality

ASSESSMENT:
1. Juvenile idiopathic scoliosis, right thoracic curve 12 degrees — mild.
   Given skeletal immaturity (Risser 0), risk of progression is moderate.
2. Progressive gait abnormality with wide-based ataxic pattern — NOTABLY
   ATYPICAL for ASD. The combination of positive Romberg, dysmetria,
   brisk reflexes, and equivocal Babinski raises concern for an underlying
   neurological process beyond ASD.
3. Bilateral pes planus with hindfoot valgus — contributing to gait
   instability.

PLAN:
1. Scoliosis: Monitor with repeat radiographs in 3 months. No bracing
   indicated at this time (curve <20 degrees). If progression >5 degrees
   or curve exceeds 20 degrees, will initiate bracing.
2. Custom bilateral ankle-foot orthoses (AFOs) — articulating, to address
   pes planus, hindfoot valgus, and provide ankle stability during gait.
   Referral to orthotics placed today.
3. Physical therapy referral — Nationwide Children's Outpatient Rehab for
   gait training, balance, and core strengthening. Recommend 2x/week.
4. *** I am noting concern regarding the PROGRESSIVE nature of this child's
   motor difficulties. The neurological examination findings (dysmetria,
   ataxia, brisk reflexes, equivocal plantar responses) are NOT consistent
   with a static encephalopathy or typical ASD motor features. I would
   strongly recommend re-evaluation by Pediatric Neurology to rule out
   a progressive neurological condition. Discussed with mother who reports
   she has raised similar concerns with Dr. Huang previously. ***
5. Return to clinic in 3 months for scoliosis monitoring and AFO check.

Electronically signed by:

Mother also reports Eli has started physical therapy at Nationwide Children's

has expressed concern that Eli's presentation is "not typical" for ASD.

Speech has continued to decline — now primarily single words and gestures.
Staring episodes continue 2–3 times daily. Mother has noticed Eli's body
"jerking" during sleep several times per week.

PHYSICAL EXAMINATION:
  Vitals:

    Weight:          21.0 kg (7th percentile)
    BMI:             14.4 (27th percentile)

  General:    Thin male, wearing bilateral AFOs. Limited verbal output.
              Said "hi" and "mama" during visit.

  Spine:
    - Forward bend: persistent right thoracic prominence, possibly increased
    - Scoliometer: 10 degrees at T8

  AFO Assessment:
    - Bilateral articulating AFOs in good condition
    - Appropriate fit, no skin breakdown
    - Hindfoot alignment improved to neutral in orthoses

  Gait (with AFOs):
    - Remains wide-based
    - Increased truncal sway compared to July visit
    - Now intermittently reaching for walls/furniture for support
    - Unable to perform tandem gait

  Neurological (screening):
    - Tone: increased bilateral LE, mild spasticity at ankles
    - DTRs: 3+ bilateral LE
    - Dysmetria: worsened — now overshooting on finger-to-nose by ~3 cm
    - Intention tremor noted bilateral UE (NEW finding)

IMAGING:
  PA standing scoliosis radiograph:
    - Right thoracic curve now measures 14 degrees (Cobb), apex T8
    - Progression of 2 degrees over 3 months
    - Risser: 0

ASSESSMENT:
1. Juvenile scoliosis — progressed from 12 to 14 degrees over 3 months.
   Still below bracing threshold but trajectory concerning given skeletal
   immaturity.
2. Progressive gait deterioration with worsening ataxia, NEW intention
   tremor, and increasing spasticity — this is a PROGRESSIVE neurological
   process. I reiterate my concern from July that this is NOT consistent
   with ASD alone.
3. Nocturnal myoclonus per parent report — needs neurological evaluation.

PLAN:
1. Continue scoliosis monitoring — return in 5 months for repeat films.
   If curve reaches 20 degrees, will initiate TLSO bracing.
2. Continue bilateral AFOs. Mother reports good compliance. Added velcro
   strap modification for easier donning.
3. Continue PT — will communicate with Sarah Lindgren, DPT regarding
   worsening function.
4. *** URGENT: I am writing a formal addendum to my July recommendation
   for Pediatric Neurology re-evaluation. The appearance of intention
   tremor and progressive spasticity in a child with an existing ASD
   diagnosis demands urgent neurological workup. I will send a direct
   message to Dr. Huang requesting neurology referral. The scoliosis
   itself may be neuromuscular in etiology rather than idiopathic. ***
5. Mother tearful during visit. She states she has been researching her

──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

INTERVAL HISTORY:
Eli returns for scoliosis and gait follow-up. It has been approximately
5 months since last orthopedic visit. Mother reports SIGNIFICANT functional
decline since October. Eli now uses a posterior walker for distances greater
than 50 feet and is using a wheelchair for community mobility (school,
grocery store). He can still walk short distances indoors with the walker
and AFOs, but falls are now daily events.

Mother reports she requested a neurology referral through Dr. Huang in
November 2019 but was told the wait time for a new neurology appointment
is 4–5 months. An appointment is scheduled for March 2021 with Dr. Amara
Osei in Pediatric Neurology.

*** NOTE: The neurology appointment was initially scheduled for June 2020 but
was delayed to March 2021 due to the COVID-19 pandemic and clinic
shutdowns. Mother is extremely frustrated by this delay. ***

Speech has further declined — Eli now uses approximately 10–15 single words
and relies primarily on a picture communication board. Staring episodes
continue. Sleep jerking has increased to nightly per parent report.

Mother brought a letter from Eli's physical therapist, Sarah Lindgren, DPT,
dated March 4, 2020, expressing concern about a progressive neurological
condition. This letter is scanned into the chart (see Provider 7, Record 7.4).

PHYSICAL EXAMINATION:
  Vitals:
    Height:         122.1 cm (8th percentile — dropping)
    Weight:          20.8 kg (6th percentile — dropping)
    BMI:             14.0 (19th percentile)

  General:    Thin male in posterior walker with bilateral AFOs. No verbal
              output during visit. Communicated by pointing to pictures
              on communication board.

  Spine:
    - Forward bend: definite right thoracic prominence, increased
    - Scoliometer: 14 degrees at T8
    - Truncal lean to right in standing

  Gait (with walker and AFOs):
    - Severely ataxic, wide-based
    - Significant truncal sway even with walker support
    - Scissoring pattern emerging at ankles
    - Walks approximately 30 feet before fatiguing
    - Cannot walk without assistive device

  Upper Extremities:
    - Marked intention tremor bilaterally
    - Difficulty grasping small objects
    - Cannot write name (previously could at age 6)

IMAGING:
  PA and lateral standing scoliosis radiographs (in walker):
    - Right thoracic curve now measures 18 degrees (Cobb), apex T7-8
    - Progression of 4 degrees over 5 months (6 degrees total since July)
    - Mild rotation at apex
    - Risser: 0

ASSESSMENT:
1. Progressive scoliosis — now 18 degrees, approaching bracing threshold.
   Rate of progression (6 degrees in 8 months) is RAPID for a prepubertal
   child. I am now classifying this as SECONDARY SCOLIOSIS, likely
   neuromuscular in etiology, rather than juvenile idiopathic. The pattern
   of long C-curve without compensatory curve is consistent with
   neuromuscular scoliosis.
2. Severe progressive gait deterioration — this child has gone from
   independent ambulation to walker-dependent in under 12 months. This
   is NOT consistent with ASD. This is a progressive neurological disease.
3. Progressive upper extremity dysfunction — intention tremor, loss of
   fine motor skills.

PLAN:
1. Initiate TLSO bracing for scoliosis. Referral to orthotics for custom
   TLSO. Curve approaching 20 degrees with rapid progression in a Risser 0
   child — high risk for continued progression.
2. Continue bilateral AFOs. Current pair showing wear — will need
   replacement in 2–3 months.
3. Continue PT for as long as functional gains or maintenance are possible.
4. *** I am writing a FORMAL LETTER to Dr. Patricia Huang and the
   Nationwide Children's Neurology department requesting EXPEDITED
   evaluation. This child has a clearly progressive neurodegenerative
   condition that has been attributed to ASD for over 4 years. The rate
   of motor decline is alarming. Every month of delay in diagnosis is a
   month of lost opportunity for potential treatment.

   I have discussed with mother the possibility of seeking a second
   opinion at Cincinnati Children's or Cleveland Clinic if the local
   neurology appointment cannot be moved up. Mother states she will
   call neurology scheduling tomorrow to request urgent status.

   For the record: I first recommended neurology re-evaluation on
   07/22/2019 and reiterated this on 10/28/2019. As of today's date,
   the patient has still not been seen by neurology. ***

5. Return in 4 months for scoliosis check and TLSO fitting assessment.
6. If scoliosis progresses beyond 40 degrees despite bracing, surgical
   consultation (posterior spinal fusion) will be discussed.

Time spent: 45 minutes (>50% counseling/coordination)

PATIENT BACKGROUND/REFERRAL INFORMATION:
Eli Reeves is a 7-year-5-month-old male referred by Dr. Michael Okafor
(Orthopedic Surgery) for gait training, balance intervention, and core
strengthening. Diagnosis of autism spectrum disorder (age 3). Progressive
gait abnormality and developing scoliosis. Recently fitted for bilateral
articulating AFOs (pending fabrication). Dr. Okafor's referral notes concern
for neurological process beyond ASD.

PARENT INTERVIEW:
Mother, Danielle Reeves, accompanied patient. She reports Eli walked
independently at 15 months. Gait was "always a little clumsy" but began
noticeably worsening around age 5. Over the past year, falls have increased
from occasional to 3–4 times daily. He can no longer run. He needs
hand-holding on stairs. He has difficulty getting up from the floor (uses
furniture to pull up). Mother is concerned his motor skills are going
"backwards" — she states he could ride a tricycle at age 4 but can no longer
do so. He used to be able to kick a ball but now cannot balance on one foot
at all.

Mother also reports speech regression (from sentences to short phrases),
staring episodes (2–3/day), and hand tremor when reaching for objects.

PRIOR THERAPY HISTORY:
  - Early Intervention PT (ages 18 months–3 years) — discharged
  - School-based OT (current, 1x/week, 30 min)
  - School-based speech therapy (current, 2x/week, 30 min)
  - No prior outpatient PT

STANDARDIZED TESTING:

  Gross Motor Function Measure (GMFM-88):
    Dimension A (Lying & Rolling):        92%
    Dimension B (Sitting):                 84%
    Dimension C (Crawling & Kneeling):     71%
    Dimension D (Standing):                56%
    Dimension E (Walking, Running, Jump):  37%
    ─────────────────────────────────────────
    TOTAL GMFM-88 SCORE:                  68%

  GMFCS Level:                             Level II (walks with limitations)

  Pediatric Balance Scale (PBS):           28/56
    (Normal for age: >50; at risk for falls: <40)

  Timed Up and Go (TUG):                  18.4 seconds
    (Normal for age 7: 5–6 seconds; SIGNIFICANTLY DELAYED)

  6-Minute Walk Test:                      142 meters
    (Normal for age 7: ~470–550 meters; 26% of expected)

CLINICAL OBSERVATIONS:

  Posture (standing):
    - Wide base of support (~18 inches between heels)
    - Mild right thoracic scoliotic curve visible
    - Forward head posture
    - Bilateral genu recurvatum
    - Bilateral hindfoot valgus (corrected in AFOs — not yet received)

  Gait Analysis (without AFOs):
    - Cadence: 82 steps/min (normal ~130 for age)
    - Step length: shortened bilaterally, asymmetric (L < R)
    - Wide-based, lurching quality
    - Truncal sway — lateral excursion ~6 cm (excessive)
    - Absent heel strike bilaterally — flat foot contact
    - No reciprocal arm swing
    - Frequent loss of balance requiring recovery steps
    - Unable to walk >100 feet without rest break

  Balance:
    - Single-leg stance: R = 1.2 sec, L = 0.8 sec (norm >10 sec at age 7)
    - Tandem stance: unable to maintain >2 seconds
    - Tandem gait: unable — immediately steps out of line
    - Romberg: positive — marked posterior sway with eyes closed

  Coordination:
    - Finger-to-nose: dysmetric bilaterally, overshooting target
    - Rapid alternating movements (forearm pronation/supination): slow,
      irregular, dysdiadochokinetic
    - Heel-to-shin: unable to track smoothly, jerky trajectory

  Tone & Reflexes:
    - Mildly increased tone bilateral LE (Modified Ashworth 1)
    - Patellar DTRs: brisk (3+)
    - Ankle clonus: 2–3 beats bilaterally (unsustained)

  Functional Mobility:
    - Floor to stand: requires furniture support, Gowers-like pattern
    - Stair ascent: step-to pattern with bilateral rail, requires verbal
      cuing and intermittent physical assist
    - Stair descent: step-to pattern, max assist for safety
    - Seated balance: fair — can maintain but sways with perturbation

CLINICAL IMPRESSION:
This is a 7-year-old male presenting with a movement pattern that includes
significant cerebellar signs (ataxia, dysmetria, dysdiadochokinesia,
intention tremor, wide-based gait) AND upper motor neuron signs (brisk
reflexes, ankle clonus, mildly increased tone). Per parent report, these
deficits are PROGRESSIVE — motor milestones were achieved and are now being
LOST. This pattern of regression is NOT consistent with a static condition
such as autism spectrum disorder or developmental coordination disorder.

While it is outside my scope of practice to diagnose, I want to clearly
document that Eli's presentation is concerning for a PROGRESSIVE
NEUROLOGICAL CONDITION and I support Dr. Okafor's recommendation for
urgent neurology evaluation.

GOALS (12-WEEK):
1. Improve PBS score from 28 to 35/56
2. Improve TUG from 18.4 to 14 seconds
3. Maintain GMFM-88 total score ≥68%
4. Independent stair ascent with one rail (step-to pattern)
5. Safe ambulation with AFOs for 200 feet without rest
6. Caregiver education: home exercise program, fall prevention

PLAN:
  Frequency: 2x/week, 45-minute sessions
  Duration: 12 weeks (24 visits — full authorization)
  Focus: Balance training, gait training with AFOs (once received),
         core/hip strengthening, functional mobility, caregiver training

        URGENT: REQUEST FOR EXPEDITED NEUROLOGICAL EVALUATION

Dear Dr. Huang, Dr. Okafor, and Dr. Osei:

I am writing this formal letter of clinical concern regarding my patient,
Eli Reeves, whom I have been treating in outpatient physical therapy since
August 12, 2019. I have now seen Eli for 36 sessions over approximately
7 months, and I am compelled to formally document my observations and
professional concerns.

SUMMARY OF MOTOR DECLINE:
Since initiating physical therapy, Eli has demonstrated CONTINUOUS AND
ACCELERATING motor regression despite consistent, intensive intervention
(2x/week, 45-minute sessions, 94% attendance rate). His standardized
assessment scores are as follows:

  GMFM-88 Total Score:
    August 2019 (Initial):     68%
    November 2019 (3 months):  64%  (decline of 4%)
    February 2020 (6 months):  58%  (decline of 10% total)

  GMFCS Level:
    August 2019:               Level II (walks with limitations)
    February 2020:             Level III (walks with handheld device)

  Pediatric Balance Scale:
    August 2019:               28/56
    February 2020:             18/56

  6-Minute Walk Test:
    August 2019:               142 meters
    February 2020:              64 meters (55% decline)

  Timed Up and Go:
    August 2019:               18.4 seconds
    February 2020:             34.7 seconds (89% slower)

  Equipment Progression:
    August 2019:               Independent ambulation (with falls)
    September 2019:            Bilateral AFOs prescribed
    November 2019:             Posterior walker introduced
    January 2020:              Wheelchair obtained

THIS PATTERN OF DECLINE IS NOT CONSISTENT WITH AUTISM SPECTRUM DISORDER.

In my 14 years of clinical practice in pediatric neurological rehabilitation,
I have treated hundreds of children with ASD. Children with ASD may have
motor clumsiness, hypotonia, motor planning difficulties, and coordination
deficits. However, these features are STATIC or SLOWLY IMPROVING with
intervention — they do NOT progressively worsen. The motor profile I am
observing in Eli is characteristic of a PROGRESSIVE NEUROLOGICAL CONDITION,
specifically one affecting cerebellar and upper motor neuron pathways.

SPECIFIC CLINICAL FINDINGS INCONSISTENT WITH ASD:

1. CEREBELLAR SIGNS: Eli demonstrates a textbook cerebellar syndrome
   including progressive ataxia (truncal and appendicular), dysmetria,
   dysdiadochokinesia, intention tremor, and what appears to be nystagmus.
   These findings are NOT features of ASD. They are features of cerebellar
   degeneration.

2. UPPER MOTOR NEURON SIGNS: Eli has brisk deep tendon reflexes (3+),
   ankle clonus (now 4–5 beats), and mildly increased tone in the lower
   extremities. These suggest corticospinal tract involvement. ASD does
   not cause upper motor neuron signs.

3. MYOCLONUS: I have directly observed myoclonic jerks during therapy
   sessions, increasing in frequency from 3 per session in November to
   5 per session in February. Mother reports nightly myoclonic jerks
   during sleep. This suggests a seizure disorder or progressive
   myoclonic condition.

4. REGRESSION: Eli is LOSING previously acquired motor skills. He could
   ride a tricycle at age 4 and cannot now. He could write his name at
   age 6 and cannot now. He was walking independently at age 7 and now
   requires a walker. Children with ASD do not lose gross motor milestones
   in this pattern.

5. SPEECH REGRESSION: Although speech is outside my primary scope, I note
   that Eli's verbal output has declined from short sentences to
   approximately 10 single words, with emerging dysarthria. Speech
   regression in a child over age 3 should prompt investigation for
   neurological disease.

6. NEW BULBAR SIGNS: Mother reports coughing with thin liquids and I have
   observed intermittent drooling — these suggest emerging dysphagia and
   are NOT features of ASD.

DIFFERENTIAL CONSIDERATIONS (for the treating physicians):
While diagnosis is outside my scope of practice, the combination of
progressive cerebellar ataxia, upper motor neuron signs, myoclonus, speech
regression, and possible seizures in a school-age child is a recognizable
clinical pattern. I would respectfully suggest that the following conditions
be considered in the neurological workup:
  - Neuronal ceroid lipofuscinosis (Batten disease)
  - Juvenile-onset leukodystrophy
  - Mitochondrial disease
  - Ataxia-telangiectasia
  - Wilson disease
  - Progressive myoclonic epilepsy syndrome

REQUEST:
I am formally requesting that Eli's upcoming neurology appointment (currently
scheduled for March 2021 — ONE FULL YEAR from now) be EXPEDITED to the
earliest possible date. This child is deteriorating rapidly. Based on the
trajectory of his decline, he will likely be non-ambulatory within 6 months
and may require gastrostomy tube feeding if dysphagia progresses.

I am also requesting that this letter be placed in Eli's medical record and
that I receive confirmation of receipt from all addressees.

I remain available to discuss my findings with any member of Eli's care team
at any time. I can be reached directly at (614) 722-6200 ext. 4418 or by
email at sarah.lindgren@nationwidechildrens.org.

Thank you for your attention to this urgent matter.

Respectfully,

Sarah Lindgren, DPT, PCS

Mother states this is typical of his seizures and occurs 2–3 times per
month. No rescue medication was administered as the seizure self-resolved

BY A PHYSICIAN. This child has an active seizure disorder that has
NEVER BEEN EVALUATED with EEG. ***

CLINICAL IMPRESSION:

This is a 9-year-old male with a PROGRESSIVE NEURODEGENERATIVE DISEASE
that has been misattributed to autism spectrum disorder for approximately
6 years. I want to state clearly and unequivocally:

*** THIS CHILD'S CLINICAL PRESENTATION IS NOT AUTISM SPECTRUM DISORDER. ***

While Eli may have had some features consistent with ASD (restricted
interests, repetitive behaviors) in early childhood — and ASD may be a
comorbid condition — the DOMINANT clinical picture is one of a progressive
neurodegenerative disease characterized by:
  1. Progressive cerebellar ataxia (truncal and appendicular)
  2. Progressive spasticity (upper motor neuron involvement)
  3. Progressive cognitive decline (dementia)
  4. Progressive speech loss (aphasia and dysarthria)
  5. Seizures (generalized tonic-clonic and myoclonic)
  6. Progressive dysphagia
  7. Macular changes on fundoscopy
  8. Visual decline

This constellation — particularly the combination of progressive dementia,
seizures, motor regression, myoclonus, and MACULAR CHANGES — is highly
suggestive of NEURONAL CEROID LIPOFUSCINOSIS (Batten disease), most likely
the late-infantile or juvenile form (CLN2 or CLN3).

The macular changes I observed on fundoscopy today are characteristic of
lipofuscin accumulation in retinal neurons and are a hallmark of NCL.

DIFFERENTIAL DIAGNOSIS:
  1. Neuronal ceroid lipofuscinosis (CLN2 or CLN3) — HIGHEST SUSPICION
  2. Juvenile-onset GM2 gangliosidosis (Tay-Sachs variant)
  3. Mitochondrial disease (MERRF, MELAS)
  4. Niemann-Pick type C
  5. Wilson disease (less likely given age of onset)
  6. Juvenile-onset metachromatic leukodystrophy

PLAN:
  1. URGENT EEG — 24-hour ambulatory EEG with video monitoring. Scheduled
     for April 5–6, 2021. Expecting to see generalized spike-and-wave
     discharges, possible photoparoxysmal response, background slowing.
  2. URGENT MRI Brain with and without contrast — with specific attention
     to cerebellar volume, white matter signal, cortical atrophy.
     Comparison with any prior imaging. Scheduled April 7, 2021.
  3. Laboratory workup:
     - CBC, CMP, hepatic function panel
     - Lactate, pyruvate (mitochondrial screen)
     - Ceruloplasmin, serum copper (Wilson disease screen)
     - Urine organic acids
     - Plasma amino acids
     - Lysosomal enzyme panel (including TPP1/CLN2 enzyme activity)
     - Acylcarnitine profile
     - Very long chain fatty acids
  4. Ophthalmology referral — URGENT — for formal retinal examination,
     OCT, electroretinography (ERG).
  5. Genetics referral — for genetic counseling and anticipated genetic
     testing (NCL gene panel or whole exome sequencing).
  6. Initiate anti-seizure medication: LEVETIRACETAM (Keppra) 250 mg

     for years that "something more is going on." She is correct. I spent
     30 minutes counseling her on what we are investigating, what the
     possible diagnoses mean, and what the next steps are. I provided her
     with written information about NCL and the Batten Disease Support
     and Research Association (BDSRA) website. Social work consult placed.

Time spent: 90 minutes face-to-face, 45 minutes chart review and
documentation. Total: 135 minutes. >50% counseling and coordination.

RECORD 8.2 — EEG REPORT
Date of Service:    April 5–6, 2021
Patient Age:        9 years, 0 months
Study Type:         24-Hour Continuous Video EEG Monitoring
Ordering Physician: Amara Osei, MD
Interpreting:       Amara Osei, MD, PhD & James Whitfield, MD (EEG Fellow)
ICD-10:             G40.309 (Generalized epilepsy, not intractable)
                    R56.9 (Unspecified convulsions)

──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

              NATIONWIDE CHILDREN'S HOSPITAL
              Department of Neurology
              Neurodiagnostic Laboratory — EEG

              ELECTROENCEPHALOGRAPHY REPORT

CLINICAL INDICATION:
Progressive neurodevelopmental regression with witnessed generalized
tonic-clonic seizures and myoclonus. Evaluate for seizure type, epilepsy
syndrome classification, and background abnormalities consistent with
neurodegenerative disease.

TECHNICAL PARAMETERS:
  Recording type:       Continuous video-EEG monitoring
  Duration:             23 hours, 47 minutes
  Electrode placement:  International 10-20 system, 21 scalp electrodes
  Additional:           EKG channel, bilateral EMG (deltoid)
  Montages:             Bipolar (longitudinal, transverse), referential
  Activation procedures: Hyperventilation (not performed — patient unable
                         to cooperate), photic stimulation at 1–30 Hz
  Medications:          Levetiracetam 250 mg BID (started 03/08/2021,
                         titrating — subtherapeutic dose)
  Clinical state:       Awake, drowsy, Stage I, Stage II, Stage III sleep
                         recorded

BACKGROUND ACTIVITY:
  Awake state:
    - Posterior dominant rhythm: 5–6 Hz theta activity over occipital
      regions. This is MARKEDLY SLOW for age (expected: 9–10 Hz alpha).
    - No well-formed alpha rhythm identifiable.
    - Diffuse excess theta (4–7 Hz) activity throughout recording.
    - Intermittent bursts of diffuse delta (1–3 Hz) activity, maximal
      over frontal regions, occurring in runs of 2–5 seconds.
    - Background is POORLY ORGANIZED and DIFFUSELY SLOW, consistent with
      moderate-to-severe diffuse cerebral dysfunction.

  Sleep state:
    - Sleep architecture is DISORGANIZED.
    - Sleep spindles: present but poorly formed and reduced in amplitude.
    - K-complexes: present.
    - Vertex waves: present but low amplitude.
    - Slow-wave sleep achieved but with persistent epileptiform activity
      (see below).

EPILEPTIFORM ACTIVITY:

  1. GENERALIZED SPIKE-AND-WAVE DISCHARGES:
     - Frequency: 2–2.5 Hz generalized spike-and-slow-wave complexes
     - Distribution: bilateral, synchronous, maximum amplitude frontally
     - Occurrence: 47 discrete bursts recorded during 24-hour monitoring
     - Duration: bursts lasting 1.5–8 seconds
     - *** Several bursts at 2 Hz with duration >3 seconds are associated
       with behavioral arrest (staring, cessation of movement) on video —
       these represent ABSENCE SEIZURES ***

  2. GENERALIZED POLYSPIKE-AND-WAVE:
     - Generalized polyspike-and-wave discharges at 3–4 Hz
     - Occur primarily during drowsiness and light sleep
     - 12 discrete bursts recorded
     - Associated with myoclonic jerks visible on video and confirmed
       on EMG channels

  3. MULTIFOCAL EPILEPTIFORM DISCHARGES:
     - Independent sharp waves and spikes arising from:
       * Left temporal (T3/T5): 34 discharges
       * Right temporal (T4/T6): 28 discharges
       * Left frontal (F3/F7): 18 discharges
       * Right occipital (O2): 14 discharges
     - Multifocal distribution suggests widespread cortical dysfunction

  4. PHOTOPAROXYSMAL RESPONSE:
     - During photic stimulation, a ROBUST photoparoxysmal response was
       elicited at flash frequencies of 8–18 Hz.
     - Response: generalized spike-and-wave discharge with associated
       myoclonic jerk
     - *** Photoparoxysmal response is a characteristic finding in
       neuronal ceroid lipofuscinosis and progressive myoclonic
       epilepsies ***

  5. SLEEP-ACTIVATED EPILEPTIFORM ACTIVITY:
     - Marked increase in epileptiform discharges during NREM sleep
     - Near-continuous spike-and-wave activity during Stage II sleep
       (electrical status epilepticus in sleep — ESES pattern)
     - This pattern is associated with progressive cognitive decline

CLINICAL EVENTS CAPTURED:

  Event 1 (04/05/2021, 14:32):
    Clinical: Patient stares, ceases hand movement, unresponsive x 6 sec
    EEG: 2 Hz generalized spike-and-wave, 6 seconds
    Classification: ABSENCE SEIZURE

  Event 2 (04/05/2021, 19:47):
    Clinical: Sudden bilateral arm jerk, drops toy
    EEG: Generalized polyspike, single discharge
    Classification: MYOCLONIC SEIZURE

  Event 3 (04/05/2021, 22:15 — asleep):
    Clinical: Bilateral arm and leg jerks, rhythmic, x 4 seconds
    EEG: Generalized polyspike-and-wave burst, 4 seconds
    Classification: MYOCLONIC SEIZURE (sleep)

  Event 4 (04/06/2021, 03:22 — asleep):
    Clinical: Eyes open, tonic stiffening bilateral UE, evolves to
    bilateral rhythmic jerking x 75 seconds. Desaturation to 91%.
    Postictal unresponsiveness x 3 minutes.
    EEG: Generalized fast activity → generalized rhythmic spike-and-wave
    at 3 Hz → gradual slowing → postictal suppression
    Classification: GENERALIZED TONIC-CLONIC SEIZURE

  Events 5–11: Multiple additional myoclonic events (7 total) and
  absence events (2 additional) recorded throughout monitoring.

TOTAL SEIZURES CAPTURED: 11 seizures in 24 hours
  - 3 absence seizures
  - 7 myoclonic seizures
  - 1 generalized tonic-clonic seizure

IMPRESSION:
1. MARKEDLY ABNORMAL 24-HOUR VIDEO EEG.

2. Severe diffuse background slowing (5–6 Hz) consistent with moderate-
   to-severe diffuse cerebral dysfunction / encephalopathy. This degree
   of slowing in a 9-year-old is highly abnormal.

3. Multiple seizure types captured: absence, myoclonic, and generalized
   tonic-clonic — indicating a GENERALIZED EPILEPSY SYNDROME.

4. Multifocal epileptiform discharges indicating widespread cortical
   hyperexcitability.

5. Robust photoparoxysmal response — characteristic of progressive
   myoclonic epilepsy / neuronal ceroid lipofuscinosis.

6. Near-continuous spike-and-wave during sleep (ESES pattern) —
   likely contributing to progressive cognitive decline.

7. CLINICAL CORRELATION: The combination of progressive dementia,
   multiple seizure types (including myoclonic and absence), background
   slowing, photoparoxysmal response, and ESES pattern is HIGHLY
   CONSISTENT with a progressive myoclonic epilepsy syndrome, most
   likely NEURONAL CEROID LIPOFUSCINOSIS given the clinical context.

RECOMMENDATIONS:
  - Optimize anti-seizure medication — current levetiracetam dose is
    subtherapeutic. Recommend uptitration and consideration of adding
    clobazam for myoclonic seizures.
  - AVOID valproic acid until mitochondrial disease is excluded.
  - AVOID carbamazepine/phenytoin — may worsen myoclonic seizures.
  - Repeat EEG in 3–6 months to assess treatment response and
    disease progression.

Date of Service:    April 7, 2021
Patient Age:        9 years, 0 months

Ordering Physician: Amara Osei, MD
Interpreting:       Rachel Zimmerman, MD (Pediatric Neuroradiology)
