                    G40.309 (Generalized epilepsy)

──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

              NATIONWIDE CHILDREN'S HOSPITAL
              Department of Radiology
              Section of Pediatric Neuroradiology

              MRI BRAIN — DIAGNOSTIC REPORT

CLINICAL INDICATION:
Progressive neurodegenerative condition with seizures, motor regression,
cognitive decline. Concern for neuronal ceroid lipofuscinosis. Evaluate
for cerebellar atrophy, cortical atrophy, white matter changes.

TECHNIQUE:
MRI of the brain performed on 3T Siemens Prisma scanner. Sequences:
  - Sagittal T1-weighted (pre- and post-contrast)
  - Axial T1-weighted (pre- and post-contrast)
  - Axial T2-weighted
  - Axial FLAIR
  - Axial DWI/ADC
  - Axial SWI (susceptibility-weighted imaging)
  - Coronal T2-weighted (high resolution, hippocampal protocol)
  - 3D volumetric T1 (for volumetric analysis)
  - MR spectroscopy (single voxel, cerebellar vermis and left basal ganglia)

Gadolinium contrast: Gadavist 0.1 mmol/kg IV, 2.2 mL administered.
Sedation: General anesthesia (propofol/sevoflurane) per anesthesia team.

COMPARISON:
No prior brain MRI available for direct comparison. Per records, no
neuroimaging has been performed previously.

*** NOTE: The absence of any prior brain MRI in a child with a 6-year
history of developmental concerns, progressive motor decline, and seizures
represents a significant gap in the diagnostic workup. ***

FINDINGS:

  Cerebral Cortex:
    - Diffuse cortical volume loss, most pronounced in the occipital and
      parietal regions. Sulci are widened globally.
    - The degree of cortical atrophy is MARKEDLY ABNORMAL for a 9-year-old
      and is consistent with a neurodegenerative process.
    - Cortical thinning is most severe in the visual cortex (calcarine
      region) and posterior parietal cortex.
    - No focal cortical lesion or malformation.

  White Matter:
    - Diffuse T2/FLAIR hyperintensity in the periventricular white matter,
      extending into the corona radiata bilaterally.
    - Confluent T2 signal abnormality in the posterior periventricular
      regions and posterior corpus callosum (splenium).
    - The white matter volume is reduced, with prominent ventriculomegaly
      that is EX VACUO (secondary to parenchymal loss rather than
      hydrocephalus).
    - These findings suggest both demyelination and gliosis.

  Cerebellum:
    - *** SEVERE CEREBELLAR ATROPHY — this is the most striking finding
      on this study. ***
    - Cerebellar hemispheric volume is markedly reduced bilaterally.
    - Prominent widening of the cerebellar fissures.
    - Vermian atrophy with widening of the primary and secondary fissures.
    - The cerebellar volume loss is DISPROPORTIONATE to the cerebral
      atrophy, suggesting a process with particular predilection for the
      cerebellum.
    - 3D volumetric analysis: Cerebellar volume measures approximately
      92 cm³ (expected for age/sex: ~135–145 cm³) — representing
      approximately 35% volume loss.
    - Cerebellar cortex shows abnormal T2 hyperintensity, suggesting
      active degeneration.

  Brainstem:
    - Mild volume loss of the pons.
    - Thinning of the middle cerebellar peduncles bilaterally.
    - Medulla appears relatively preserved.

  Deep Gray Matter:
    - Thalami: mildly reduced in volume bilaterally. T2 signal is mildly
      increased, particularly in the pulvinar regions bilaterally.
      *** Thalamic signal change with pulvinar predominance can be seen
      in certain forms of NCL. ***
    - Basal ganglia: globus pallidus and putamen appear mildly small.
      No abnormal signal. No calcification on SWI.
    - Caudate nuclei: mildly atrophic.

  Ventricles:
    - Lateral ventricles: moderately enlarged (ex vacuo).
    - Third ventricle: mildly enlarged.
    - Fourth ventricle: prominent, reflecting cerebellar atrophy.
    - No obstructive hydrocephalus.
    - No periventricular edema.

  Hippocampi:
    - Bilateral hippocampal volume loss. Temporal horns mildly prominent.
    - No mesial temporal sclerosis.

  Post-Contrast:
    - No abnormal parenchymal enhancement.
    - No leptomeningeal enhancement.
    - No mass lesion.

  MR Spectroscopy:
    Cerebellar vermis:
      - Decreased N-acetylaspartate (NAA) peak — indicating neuronal
        loss/dysfunction
      - Elevated myoinositol (mI) — indicating gliosis
      - NAA/Cr ratio: 1.1 (normal >1.6 for age) — MARKEDLY REDUCED
      - Choline/Cr ratio: mildly elevated — suggesting membrane turnover
      - No lactate peak identified in this voxel

    Left basal ganglia:
      - Mildly decreased NAA
      - NAA/Cr ratio: 1.4 (low-normal)
      - Small lactate doublet detected at 1.3 ppm — *** ABNORMAL,
        suggesting impaired mitochondrial function ***

  Other:
    - Orbits: optic nerves appear mildly thin bilaterally (volume loss)
    - No extra-axial collection
    - Calvarium: normal
    - Paranasal sinuses: mild mucosal thickening in maxillary sinuses

IMPRESSION:
1. MARKEDLY ABNORMAL MRI demonstrating a pattern of PROGRESSIVE
   NEURODEGENERATIVE DISEASE in a 9-year-old male.

2. SEVERE CEREBELLAR ATROPHY — disproportionate to cerebral atrophy,
   with estimated 35% volume loss. Cerebellar cortex shows signal
   change suggesting active degeneration. This correlates with the
   patient's progressive ataxia.

3. DIFFUSE CEREBRAL CORTICAL ATROPHY with occipital-parietal predominance.
   Cortical thinning is most severe in the visual cortex, correlating
   with the patient's visual decline.

4. DIFFUSE WHITE MATTER SIGNAL ABNORMALITY — periventricular and posterior
   predominant, consistent with demyelination/gliosis.

5. Ex vacuo ventriculomegaly secondary to parenchymal volume loss.

6. Thalamic signal change (pulvinar regions) — can be associated with
   neuronal ceroid lipofuscinosis.

7. MR spectroscopy: Decreased NAA in cerebellum (neuronal loss), elevated
   myoinositol (gliosis), and small lactate peak in basal ganglia
   (possible mitochondrial dysfunction).

8. OVERALL PATTERN: The combination of progressive cerebellar atrophy,
   cortical atrophy (occipital predominant), white matter changes, and
   thalamic signal abnormality is CONSISTENT WITH NEURONAL CEROID
   LIPOFUSCINOSIS. The cerebellar predominance of the atrophy may
   suggest CLN2 (late-infantile) form, though overlap between subtypes
   exists.

9. Given the absence of prior imaging, it is not possible to document
   the rate of progression radiographically. Recommend follow-up MRI
   in 12 months for comparison.

PURPOSE OF VISIT:
This visit was scheduled to review all diagnostic results, confirm the
diagnosis, discuss prognosis, and develop a comprehensive care plan.
Father is present for the first time — mother reports he has been
"struggling to accept" the possibility of a serious diagnosis.

DIAGNOSTIC SUMMARY:

After comprehensive evaluation including clinical examination, 24-hour
video EEG, MRI brain, extensive laboratory workup, and lysosomal enzyme
analysis, I am confirming the diagnosis of:

  ╔══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╗
  ║  CLN2 DISEASE (LATE-INFANTILE NEURONAL CEROID LIPOFUSCINOSIS)  ║
  ║  Also known as: Jansky-Bielschowsky disease                    ║
  ║  Also known as: Classic Late-Infantile Batten Disease           ║
  ║                                                                ║
  ║  Confirmed by:                                                 ║
  ║    - TPP1 enzyme activity: 0.8 nmol/hr/mg (<5% of normal)     ║
  ║    - Clinical phenotype consistent with CLN2 disease            ║
  ║    - EEG findings consistent with CLN2 disease                  ║
  ║    - MRI findings consistent with CLN2 disease                  ║
  ║    - Macular changes on fundoscopy consistent with NCL          ║
  ║                                                                ║
  ║  *** Genetic confirmation pending — CLN2/TPP1 gene sequencing  ║
  ║  has been sent (results expected in 4–6 weeks) ***             ║
  ╚══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════╝

DISCUSSION OF RESULTS WITH FAMILY:

I spent 45 minutes reviewing each piece of evidence with both parents:

  1. EEG: I explained that Eli's EEG showed multiple types of seizures
     (11 in 24 hours), severe background slowing indicating widespread
     brain dysfunction, and a photoparoxysmal response that is
     characteristic of NCL. His staring episodes are absence seizures.
     His sleep jerking consists of myoclonic seizures. The episodes of
     body stiffening and jerking are generalized tonic-clonic seizures.

  2. MRI: I showed both parents the MRI images on my screen. I pointed
     out the severe cerebellar atrophy (35% volume loss) and the diffuse
     cortical atrophy. I compared Eli's scan to a normal 9-year-old
     brain image. Mother gasped. Father was silent.

  3. Labs: I explained that the TPP1 enzyme level of 0.8 (normal >5.0)
     confirms that Eli's body cannot produce sufficient amounts of the
     enzyme needed to break down certain cellular waste products
     (lipofuscins). These waste products accumulate in neurons, causing
     progressive neuronal death.

  4. Prognosis: This was the most difficult part of the conversation.
     I explained that CLN2 disease is a progressive, currently
     incurable condition. Typical life expectancy with CLN2 disease
     is 6–15 years from symptom onset, though some patients survive
     into their teens or early twenties with supportive care. I
     explained that Eli's symptoms likely began around age 4–5, which
     means he has been living with active disease for approximately
     4–5 years.

     I discussed the expected trajectory:
       - Continued loss of motor function → becoming fully dependent
       - Continued cognitive decline
       - Seizures may become more difficult to control
       - Progressive visual loss → likely blindness
       - Progressive dysphagia (G-tube already in place)
       - Respiratory complications in later stages

     Mother was crying. Father stood and left the room for several
     minutes. Social worker followed him. When he returned, he asked:
     "How did no one catch this sooner?"

  5. Treatment Options: I discussed the following:
     a) CERLIPONASE ALFA (Brineura) — FDA-approved enzyme replacement
        therapy for CLN2 disease, administered via intracerebroventricular
        infusion every 2 weeks. I explained that this treatment has been
        shown to slow the rate of motor and language decline in children
        treated EARLY in the disease course. However, I was honest with
        the family that Eli's disease is ADVANCED, and the benefit of
        initiating ERT at this stage is uncertain. I recommended
        referral to a CLN2 treatment center for evaluation.

     b) Ongoing seizure management with optimization of anti-seizure
        medications.

     c) Supportive/palliative care to maximize quality of life.

     d) Genetic testing of both parents (carrier testing) and genetic
        counseling for future family planning.

MEDICATION CHANGES:
  - Levetiracetam: increase to 500 mg PO BID (from 250 mg BID)
  - ADD: Clobazam (Onfi) 5 mg PO BID for myoclonic seizures
  - Continue melatonin 5 mg QHS
  - ADD: Diazepam rectal gel (Diastat) 10 mg PRN for seizures >5 minutes
    (emergency rescue medication). Mother and father trained on
    administration. School nurse to be trained as well.

REFERRALS:
  1. Ophthalmology (Dr. Karen Whitlow) — formal retinal exam, OCT, ERG
     — Appointment: June 28, 2021

  5. Psychology — for family support, grief counseling
     — Referral placed

     home nursing, respite care, equipment needs
     — Social worker assisting family with application

UPDATED DIAGNOSIS LIST:
  1. CLN2 disease (late-infantile neuronal ceroid lipofuscinosis) — E75.4
  2. Generalized epilepsy (absence, myoclonic, tonic-clonic seizures) —
     G40.409
  3. Progressive cerebellar ataxia — G11.1

     *** Note: The original ASD diagnosis from age 3 may represent early
     manifestations of CLN2 disease rather than true autism spectrum
     disorder. The behavioral features that led to the ASD diagnosis
     (restricted interests, repetitive behaviors, social withdrawal) can
     be seen in the early stages of NCL as the brain begins to
     degenerate. I am listing ASD as a "possible comorbid" condition
     rather than removing it entirely, as I cannot retrospectively
     determine whether true ASD was present in addition to CLN2. ***

FOLLOW-UP:
  - Neurology: 3 months (September 2021)
  - Sooner if seizures worsen or new symptoms develop

A NOTE ON DIAGNOSTIC DELAY:
For the medical record, I want to document the following timeline:
  - Age 3 (2015): ASD diagnosed. Motor exam "mildly clumsy."
  - Age 5 (2017): Mother raising concerns about worsening. Attributed
    to ASD.
  - Age 7 (2019): Orthopedic surgeon documents concern and recommends
    neurology re-evaluation. PT documents progressive cerebellar signs.
  - Age 7 (2019): Neurology referral placed (November 2019).
  - Age 8 (2020): PT writes formal letter requesting expedited evaluation.
    Appointment delayed by COVID-19 from June 2020 to March 2021.
  - Age 9 (2021): First neurology evaluation. Diagnosis confirmed.

The total time from first documented provider concern to diagnosis
was approximately 20 months (July 2019 to April 2021). The total time
from probable symptom onset to diagnosis was approximately 5–6 years
(age 4 to age 9). Earlier diagnosis — particularly if obtained before
age 6–7 — might have allowed for earlier initiation of cerliponase alfa
therapy, potentially preserving more neurological function.

I raise this not to assign blame, but to emphasize the importance of
maintaining a broad differential diagnosis in children with
developmental concerns, particularly when regression is present. The
diagnosis of ASD, once applied, created a framework through which ALL
subsequent symptoms were interpreted — a phenomenon known as "diagnostic
overshadowing." Progressive motor decline, seizures, and speech
regression were attributed to ASD or "just how he is" rather than
triggering re-evaluation of the underlying diagnosis.

Time spent: 60 minutes face-to-face, 30 minutes documentation.
>50% counseling and coordination.

COMPILED BY: Emergent Diagnostic Institution — Medical Records Analysis

SECTION 3 OVERVIEW:
This section covers the period from July 2019 to September 2021 (Eli ages
7–10), during which the true nature of Eli's condition finally came to light.
After years of progressive decline attributed to autism spectrum disorder,
three key events converged to break through the diagnostic overshadowing:
an orthopedic surgeon who recognized the atypicality of the motor findings,
a physical therapist who meticulously documented progressive decline and
wrote a landmark letter of concern, and a pediatric neurologist who — when
finally given the opportunity to evaluate Eli — immediately recognized the
neurodegenerative pattern.

PROVIDERS IN THIS SECTION:
  Provider 6: Dr. Michael Okafor, MD — Orthopedic Surgery (3 records)
  Provider 7: Sarah Lindgren, DPT — Physical Therapy (5 records)
  Provider 8: Dr. Amara Osei, MD, PhD — Pediatric Neurology (5 records)

KEY FINDINGS AND DIAGNOSTIC MILESTONES:

  1. SCOLIOSIS PROGRESSION (Orthopedics):
     - July 2019: Cobb angle 12 degrees (mild)
     - October 2019: Cobb angle 14 degrees (+2 in 3 months)
     - March 2020: Cobb angle 18 degrees (+6 in 8 months)
     - September 2021: Cobb angle 34 degrees (per PT discharge note)
     - Reclassified from "idiopathic" to "neuromuscular" scoliosis

  2. MOTOR DECLINE TRAJECTORY (Physical Therapy — GMFM-88):
     - August 2019:    68% | GMFCS Level II
     - November 2019:  64% | GMFCS Level II–III
     - February 2020:  58% | GMFCS Level III
     - July 2020:      48% | GMFCS Level III–IV
     - January 2021:   38% | GMFCS Level IV
     - September 2021: 22% | GMFCS Level IV–V

     Total decline: 46 percentage points in 25 months.
     Rate: approximately 1.8 percentage points per month.

  3. EQUIPMENT PROGRESSION:
     - August 2019: Independent ambulation (with falls)
     - September 2019: Bilateral AFOs
     - November 2019: Posterior walker
     - January 2020: Wheelchair (manual, tilt-in-space)
     - September 2021: Hoyer lift, hospital bed, fully dependent

  4. COMMUNICATION DECLINE:
     - Age 7 (2019): Short 2–3 word phrases
     - Age 8 (2020): ~10–15 single words
     - Age 9 (2021): <5 words, severely dysarthric
     - Age 9 (2021): Eye-gaze AAC device

  5. DIAGNOSTIC RESULTS (Neurology):
     - EEG: 11 seizures in 24 hours (absence, myoclonic, GTC); severe
       background slowing; photoparoxysmal response; ESES pattern
     - MRI: Severe cerebellar atrophy (35% volume loss), diffuse cortical
       atrophy (occipital predominant), white matter changes, thalamic
       signal abnormality
     - Labs: TPP1 enzyme 0.8 nmol/hr/mg (critically low, <5% normal);
       lactate 2.8 (elevated); ceruloplasmin/copper normal
     - DIAGNOSIS: CLN2 disease (late-infantile neuronal ceroid
       lipofuscinosis / Batten disease)

══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════
              CRITICAL MISSED OPPORTUNITIES — TIMELINE
══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

  OPPORTUNITY 1 (Age 3, 2015):
    What happened: ASD diagnosed based on behavioral features.
    What was missed: No neurological examination beyond basic screening.
                     No baseline brain MRI. No metabolic screening.
    What should have occurred: A more comprehensive differential diagnosis
    that included neurodegenerative conditions, particularly given the
    report of "mild clumsiness" that was already present.

  OPPORTUNITY 2 (Ages 4–5, 2016–2017):
    What happened: Mother reported increasing clumsiness, falls, and
    speech plateau. PCP attributed to ASD.
    What was missed: The transition from "clumsy" to "falling frequently"
    represents a RED FLAG for progressive disease. Loss of tricycle-riding
    ability (a previously mastered skill) is REGRESSION, not ASD.
    What should have occurred: Referral to pediatric neurology when motor
    regression was first reported.

  OPPORTUNITY 3 (Ages 5–6, 2017–2018):
    What happened: Staring episodes began. Attributed to "ASD zoning out."
    What was missed: Staring episodes in a child with developmental
    regression should be evaluated with EEG. Any child with new-onset
    staring spells deserves seizure evaluation.
    What should have occurred: EEG. If performed at this time, the
    characteristic NCL EEG pattern would likely have been present and
    could have led to diagnosis 3–4 years earlier.

  OPPORTUNITY 4 (Age 7, July 2019):
    What happened: Dr. Okafor (orthopedics) documented cerebellar signs,
    ataxia, and concern for progressive neurological condition. Recommended
    neurology re-evaluation.
    What was missed: The referral process was slow. No urgent pathway.
    What should have occurred: STAT neurology consultation. A child with
    documented cerebellar signs and progressive motor decline should be
    seen urgently, not placed on a 4–5 month waitlist.

  OPPORTUNITY 5 (Age 7, January 2020):
    What happened: Eli was seen in the Nationwide Children's ED after a
    witnessed seizure at school. Diagnosed as "seizure vs. behavioral
    staring spell in child with ASD." Discharged without EEG.
    What was missed: A witnessed tonic event in a child with progressive
    neurological decline was DISMISSED as possibly behavioral because of
    the ASD label. This is diagnostic overshadowing at its most dangerous.
    What should have occurred: Emergent or urgent EEG. Neurology consult
    from the ED. Admission for workup.

  OPPORTUNITY 6 (Age 8, March–June 2020):
    What happened: PT wrote a detailed, evidence-based letter requesting
    expedited neurology evaluation. COVID-19 pandemic delayed the
    appointment from June 2020 to March 2021.
    What was missed: Despite a compelling clinical case documented by
    PT and orthopedics, the system failed to expedite care even before
    COVID. The pandemic added 9 months to an already lengthy wait.
    What should have occurred: Telehealth neurology evaluation during
    COVID. Record review and remote assessment could have initiated the
    diagnostic workup months earlier.

══════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

IMPACT OF DIAGNOSTIC DELAY:

CLN2 disease has an FDA-approved enzyme replacement therapy (cerliponase
alfa / Brineura), which was approved in April 2017. Clinical trials have
demonstrated that early initiation of treatment can significantly slow
the rate of motor and language decline. The treatment is most effective
when started BEFORE significant neurological damage has occurred — ideally
at the earliest signs of disease.

Had Eli been diagnosed at age 5–6 (when motor regression was first
evident), he could have been started on cerliponase alfa at a time when
he was still walking independently, still speaking in sentences, and
still had preserved cognitive function. By the time he was diagnosed at
age 9, he was wheelchair-dependent, nearly nonverbal, cognitively
severely impaired, and had a G-tube. The window for maximum therapeutic
benefit had largely closed.

This does not mean treatment at age 9 is futile — the family has been
referred to a CLN2 treatment center for evaluation — but the potential
for preserved function is significantly diminished compared to what
might have been achieved with earlier diagnosis.

The estimated delay from recognizable symptoms to diagnosis: 5–6 years.
The estimated delay from first provider concern to diagnosis: 20 months.
The estimated delay attributable to COVID-19: 9 months.
The estimated delay attributable to diagnostic overshadowing by ASD: 4+ years.

  1. Danielle Reeves (Mother) — raised concerns as early as age 4–5,
     repeatedly, at every visit. Researched symptoms online. Was told
     her son "has autism" and his difficulties were "part of ASD."

  2. Dr. Michael Okafor, MD (Orthopedics) — first physician to formally
     document that Eli's presentation was "NOT consistent with ASD" and
     recommend neurology re-evaluation (July 2019). Reiterated concern
     at every subsequent visit. Wrote formal letter (March 2020).

  3. Sarah Lindgren, DPT (Physical Therapy) — meticulously documented
     progressive decline with standardized measures. Wrote the landmark
     formal letter of concern (March 2020) detailing specific clinical
     findings inconsistent with ASD, including a differential diagnosis
     that CORRECTLY listed NCL as the first consideration. This letter
     is a model of interprofessional advocacy.

  4. Dr. Amara Osei, MD, PhD (Neurology) — upon first evaluation,
     immediately recognized the neurodegenerative pattern, ordered
     appropriate workup, and confirmed the diagnosis within 6 weeks.
     Documented the phenomenon of diagnostic overshadowing in the record.

    Does not consistently track moving objects. Oropharynx moist, mild

  Respiratory: Clear bilaterally. No increased work of breathing.
  Abdomen: Soft, non-distended today. G-tube site — N/A (not placed).

neurological evaluation in the setting of a reportedly progressive

  Birth — 12 months: Unremarkable. Full-term, normal delivery, APGAR 8/9.

  levetiracetam. Speech limited to 4-5 recognizable words. Prolonged seizure

  2. Diazepam rectal gel (Diastat) 10mg PRN seizure >2 min

  Mental Status:

  - CN II — Fundoscopic exam: SIGNIFICANT FINDINGS. Optic discs appear pale

      withdrawal to pain. Estimated 1/5 throughout.
    * Trunk: Cannot sit unsupported. Requires full wheelchair support.
  - Involuntary movements:
    * Hand-wringing stereotypies: CONSTANT when hands are not engaged.
      Bilateral, rhythmic, midline hand-wringing/washing movements. These
      are notable for their persistence and automaticity.
    * Myoclonic jerks: FREQUENT. During the 60-minute evaluation, I observed
      12 distinct myoclonic events — bilateral, symmetric, brief (<0.5 sec)
      jerks predominantly involving shoulders and arms. These occurred
      irregularly, sometimes in clusters of 2-3.
    * Dystonic posturing: Intermittent left wrist flexion/finger extension.

  Cerebellar Examination:
  - Severely impaired. Finger-to-object testing (using a toy): profound
    intention tremor and past-pointing bilaterally. Dysmetria severe —
    consistently overshoots or undershoots target. These findings are
    consistent with SEVERE CEREBELLAR DYSFUNCTION and are markedly worse
    than would be expected from the available MRI report (age 6) which
    described only "mildly prominent ventricles."

  Reflexes:
  - Biceps: 3+ bilaterally
  - Brachioradialis: 3+ bilaterally
  - Triceps: 3+ bilaterally
  - Patellar: 3+ bilaterally with crossed adductor response
  - Achilles: 3+ bilaterally with sustained clonus (4-5 beats)
  - Plantar response: EXTENSOR bilaterally (Babinski positive)
  - Jaw jerk: 2+ (mildly brisk)

  Sensation: Withdraws to pain in all four extremities. Light touch — less
  reliable response, may reflect cognitive limitation rather than sensory
  deficit. Proprioception — unable to test reliably.

  Gait: Non-ambulatory. Unable to stand or bear weight.

  SEIZURE ACTIVITY DURING EXAMINATION: Three clinical seizure events were
  observed during the 60-minute evaluation:
    Event 1 (09:35): Behavioral arrest lasting approximately 8 seconds.
    Staring, subtle eyelid flutter, cessation of hand-wringing. Followed
    by brief confusion (~5 seconds). Clinically consistent with absence
    seizure.
    Event 2 (09:48): Same as above, approximately 12 seconds duration.
    Event 3 (10:02): Cluster of 4 myoclonic jerks over approximately 3
    seconds, larger amplitude than the isolated myoclonic jerks observed
    at other times. No behavioral arrest.

ADDITIONAL EVALUATION — REVIEW OF IMAGING AND RECORDS TIMELINE:
I have spent approximately 2 hours reviewing all available records,
mother's binder, and imaging. Based on this review, I have constructed
the following diagnostic timeline:

  Age 3: MRI brain — "normal for age" (Virginia, report in binder)
  Age 3.5: Diagnosed ASD — NO genetic testing, NO metabolic testing, NO EEG
  Age 6: MRI brain — "mildly prominent ventricles, normal variant" — NOT
    compared to age-3 MRI (different state, different system)
  Age 6: Neurology (Dr. Chen) — single visit, accepted ASD diagnosis, no
    further workup
  Age 9: EEG — epileptiform activity (report not available, referenced)
  Age 9: MRI brain — cerebellar atrophy (report not available, referenced)
  Age 9: Labs — elevated lactate 2.8 mmol/L (per mother's notes, lab report
    not available)
  Age 9: Dr. Osei — recommended genetic workup — NOT COMPLETED (family PCS)
  Age 10: Transfer to NC — records incomplete. Key findings (EEG, MRI age 9,
    lactate, Dr. Osei's genetic recommendation) NOT in transferred file.
  Age 11: Current evaluation — first comprehensive review.

ASSESSMENT:

Based on thorough review of the complete developmental trajectory as
documented in the mother's records, and corroborated by the available medical
records and today's neurological examination, this patient has been
experiencing PROGRESSIVE NEURODEGENERATION since approximately age 2-3 years,
initially mischaracterized as autism spectrum disorder.

The clinical trajectory is as follows:
  - Normal early development (0-12 months)
  - Developmental plateau then regression beginning at 18-24 months
  - Progressive loss of language, motor, and cognitive skills
  - Emergence of seizures (likely onset age 2-3 as absence seizures;
    formally diagnosed age 9)
  - Progressive cerebellar dysfunction (ataxia → immobility)
  - Progressive visual impairment (emerging)
  - Cerebellar and cerebral atrophy on imaging (progressive over serial
    studies)

This trajectory — normal early development followed by progressive
regression across ALL domains, combined with seizures, cerebellar atrophy,
and visual concerns — is CLASSIC for a neuronal ceroid lipofuscinosis (NCL)
or similar progressive neurodegenerative storage disorder.

The ASD diagnosis rendered at age 3.5 was likely INCORRECT. The behavioral
features that led to the ASD diagnosis — hand-wringing stereotypies, social
withdrawal, limited communication, and reduced eye contact — are far more
likely to have been early manifestations of the neurodegenerative process
rather than features of primary autism spectrum disorder. Specifically:
  - The hand-wringing is stereotypic movement secondary to cerebellar/basal
    ganglia dysfunction, not autistic stimming behavior
  - The social withdrawal reflects progressive cognitive decline and visual
    impairment, not a primary social communication deficit
  - The language limitation reflects neurodegeneration of language networks,
    not the qualitative language differences seen in ASD
  - The "regression" attributed to ASD-related regression or anxiety was in
    fact progressive neurodegenerative disease

The anchoring on the ASD diagnosis at age 3.5 appears to have influenced
every subsequent provider evaluation for the next 8 years. Each new provider
accepted the historical diagnosis rather than re-evaluating from first
principles. The PROGRESSIVE nature of the decline — which is NOT a feature
of autism spectrum disorder — was either not recognized or was attributed to
comorbid conditions.

DIFFERENTIAL DIAGNOSIS (in order of clinical suspicion):
  1. Neuronal ceroid lipofuscinosis (NCL) — HIGHEST SUSPICION
     - CLN5 (Finnish variant late infantile) — onset age 4-7, progressive
       motor/cognitive decline, seizures, visual loss, cerebellar atrophy
     - CLN6 (variant late infantile) — similar phenotype to CLN5
     - CLN7 (Turkish variant late infantile) — similar
     - CLN8 (Northern epilepsy / Turkish late infantile) — similar
     - Late-onset CLN2 — less likely given age of onset
  2. Mitochondrial disorder (e.g., MERRF, MELAS, Leigh syndrome variant)
  3. Neurodegeneration with brain iron accumulation (NBIA)
  4. GM2 gangliosidosis (late-onset Tay-Sachs or Sandhoff)
  5. Niemann-Pick type C
  6. Other lysosomal storage disorder
  7. Progressive myoclonic epilepsy — other genetic causes

PLAN:

1. MRI BRAIN — 3 Tesla with dedicated sequences:
   - Standard anatomical sequences (T1, T2, FLAIR, DWI)
   - SWI (susceptibility-weighted imaging) for iron deposition
   - MR spectroscopy (single-voxel, parietal cortex and cerebellum)
   - COMPARISON TO ALL PRIOR IMAGING (I have obtained the age-3 and age-6
     reports; will attempt to obtain actual DICOM images for side-by-side
     comparison. Age-9 MRI images requested AGAIN from Piedmont Columbus.)
   - Scheduled: 10/15/2023 at Duke Radiology (Fayetteville satellite)

   - To characterize seizure types, frequency, and EEG background
   - To assess for photosensitivity (exaggerated photic response is
     characteristic of NCL)
   - Scheduled: 11/06-08/2023, Womack AMC EEG Lab

3. OPHTHALMOLOGY — URGENT REFERRAL:
   - Comprehensive eye exam with dilated fundoscopy
   - Electroretinogram (ERG) — CRITICAL for NCL diagnosis
   - Visual evoked potentials (VEP)
   - OCT (optical coherence tomography) if patient can cooperate
   - Referred to Dr. Aisha Patel, Pediatric Ophthalmology, Duke Eye Center
     (Fayetteville)

4. LABORATORY STUDIES (ordered today):
   - Levetiracetam level (trough)
   - Comprehensive metabolic panel
   - Lactate and pyruvate (fasting, free-flowing venipuncture — critical
     for accurate measurement)

5. GENETICS REFERRAL:
   - Refer to Duke Genetics Clinic (Dr. Rachel Kim) for comprehensive

   - Recommend: Whole exome sequencing (WES) OR comprehensive
     neurodegeneration/NCL gene panel (CLN1-14 at minimum)

     implications (enzyme replacement therapy, gene therapy trials)

6. MEDICATION ADJUSTMENT:
   - Continue levetiracetam 750mg BID
   - ADD: Clobazam (Onfi) 5mg PO BID — for additional broad-spectrum

DIAGNOSTIC IMAGING — MRI BRAIN WITH AND WITHOUT CONTRAST (3 TESLA)
----------------------------------------------------------------------------
Facility: Duke Radiology — Fayetteville Satellite
3100 Village Drive, Suite 200, Fayetteville, NC 28304
Date of Study: 10/15/2023
Patient: REEVES, ELI MARCUS | DOB: 03/15/2012 | Age: 11 years 7 months
MRN: DUKE-4829175 | Accession: MR-2023-FC-09841
Ordering Physician: Samuel Park, MD — Womack AMC Neurology

EXAMINATION: MRI Brain with and without gadolinium contrast, 3 Tesla.

CLINICAL INDICATION: Progressive neurodegenerative process. Seizures.
Cerebellar ataxia. Visual decline. Suspected neuronal ceroid lipofuscinosis.
Please compare to all prior imaging.

TECHNIQUE: Multiplanar, multisequence MRI of the brain performed on a 3T
Siemens Prisma scanner. Sequences obtained: sagittal 3D T1 MPRAGE, axial
T2, axial FLAIR, axial DWI/ADC, axial SWI, coronal T2, post-contrast
axial and coronal T1. MR spectroscopy: single-voxel PRESS (TE 35 and
TE 144) from right parietal cortex and cerebellar vermis.

CONTRAST: Gadolinium-based contrast (Gadavist) 0.1 mmol/kg IV administered
without complication. Sedation with propofol administered by pediatric
anesthesiology team (patient unable to cooperate for unsedated scan).

COMPARISON: MRI brain age 3 (05/2015, report only — Virginia); MRI brain
age 6 (09/2018, report and DICOM images obtained from Riverside Radiology,
Columbus, GA); MRI brain age 9 (02/2021, report and DICOM images FINALLY
obtained from Piedmont Columbus Regional on 10/02/2023).

FINDINGS:

Cerebellum:
  - SEVERE cerebellar volume loss, diffuse, affecting both vermis and
    hemispheres. The cerebellar folia are markedly thinned with prominent
    interfolial fissures. Cerebellar volume is estimated at <50% of
    expected for age.
  - COMPARISON: The age-6 MRI, when reviewed in retrospect, shows SUBTLE
    cerebellar volume loss that was not identified in the original read
    (reported as "normal variant"). The age-9 MRI shows moderate cerebellar
    atrophy, which has SIGNIFICANTLY PROGRESSED on the current study.
  - The trajectory across the four imaging time points (ages 3, 6, 9, 12)
    demonstrates ACCELERATING cerebellar atrophy, particularly between
    ages 9 and 12.

Cerebral cortex:
  - Cerebral cortical atrophy has progressed, now rated as moderate. Most
    prominent in the PARIETAL and OCCIPITAL lobes. Sulci are diffusely
    widened. Cortical ribbon appears thinned, particularly in the parieto-
    occipital regions.
  - COMPARISON: The age-6 MRI showed mildly prominent ventricles (originally
    attributed to "normal variant") — in retrospect, this likely represented
    early cerebral volume loss. The age-9 MRI shows mild cortical volume
    loss. The current study shows clear progression.

Ventricles:
  - Lateral ventricles are prominently dilated (ex vacuo), reflecting
    parenchymal volume loss. Third ventricle mildly prominent. Fourth
    ventricle prominent relative to the decreased cerebellar volume.
  - No evidence of obstructive hydrocephalus. Ventricular enlargement is
    proportional to parenchymal loss.

Corpus callosum:
  - Diffuse thinning of the corpus callosum, most prominent in the body
    and splenium. This has progressed compared to the age-9 MRI, where
    early thinning was noted.

Hippocampal formations:
  - Bilateral hippocampal volume loss now apparent, not present on prior
    imaging. Hippocampi appear mildly atrophic with subtle T2 signal
    increase.

White matter:
  - T2/FLAIR: Subtle but definite periventricular and deep white matter
    signal abnormality, NEW since the age-9 study. Patchy areas of T2/FLAIR
    hyperintensity in the periventricular white matter, centrum semiovale,
    and posterior corona radiata bilaterally. This may represent
    demyelination or gliosis in the setting of neurodegeneration.

Thalami:
  - LOW T2 SIGNAL in the thalami bilaterally, particularly in the pulvinar
    nuclei. This finding was not present on prior imaging and may represent
    iron deposition or mineralization. This finding has been reported in
    certain NCL subtypes and in neurodegeneration with brain iron
    accumulation (NBIA). SWI sequences confirm susceptibility effect in
    these regions, consistent with paramagnetic material (likely iron or
    calcium).

Basal ganglia:
  - Normal signal. No volume loss. No mineralization.

Brainstem:
  - Mild volume loss of the pons and midbrain. Medulla appears preserved.
  - No signal abnormality.

Post-contrast imaging:
  - No abnormal enhancement. No mass lesion. No leptomeningeal enhancement.

MR Spectroscopy:
  Right parietal cortex (single voxel):
    - Decreased N-acetylaspartate (NAA) peak — consistent with neuronal
      loss/dysfunction
    - Mildly elevated myoinositol (mI) peak — consistent with gliosis
    - Choline (Cho) normal
    - Lactate: small inverted doublet at 1.3 ppm — MILDLY ELEVATED
      (consistent with either mitochondrial dysfunction or cellular stress
      in neurodegenerative process)
    - NAA/Cr ratio: 1.2 (low; normal >1.6)
    - mI/Cr ratio: 0.9 (elevated; normal <0.7)

  Cerebellar vermis (single voxel):
    - Markedly decreased NAA — consistent with severe neuronal loss
    - Elevated mI
    - NAA/Cr ratio: 0.8 (severely reduced)

IMPRESSION:

1. PROGRESSIVE AND ACCELERATING cerebellar and cerebral atrophy over a
   6-year imaging span, now severe in the cerebellum and moderate in the
   cerebrum. The pattern of volume loss — cerebellar predominant with
   parieto-occipital cortical involvement — is characteristic of neuronal
   ceroid lipofuscinosis (NCL).

2. New periventricular and deep white matter T2/FLAIR signal abnormality
   since prior imaging, likely representing demyelination or gliosis
   secondary to the neurodegenerative process.

3. Low T2/SWI signal in the thalami bilaterally (pulvinar nuclei) — may
   represent iron deposition or mineralization. This finding has been
   described in some NCL subtypes, particularly CLN5 and CLN8.

4. MR spectroscopy demonstrates reduced NAA (neuronal loss) and elevated
   myoinositol (gliosis) in both sampled regions, with more severe changes
   in the cerebellum. Mild lactate elevation in the parietal cortex.

5. Progressive corpus callosum thinning and new hippocampal volume loss.

CLINICAL CORRELATION: The progressive neurodegenerative imaging pattern is
most consistent with neuronal ceroid lipofuscinosis or a mitochondrial
disorder. GENETIC TESTING IS ESSENTIAL for definitive diagnosis. Clinical
and imaging findings favor NCL over primary mitochondrial disease.

DIAGNOSTIC STUDY — CONTINUOUS VIDEO-EEG MONITORING (48-HOUR)
----------------------------------------------------------------------------
Facility: Womack Army Medical Center — Neurophysiology Laboratory
Date of Study: 11/06/2023 — 11/08/2023 (48 hours)
Patient: REEVES, ELI MARCUS | DOB: 03/15/2012 | Age: 11 years 8 months
Ordering Physician: Samuel Park, MD
Interpreting Physician: Samuel Park, MD / Jennifer Liu, MD (EEG fellow)

CLINICAL INDICATION: Progressive neurodegenerative disease. Multiple seizure
types. Characterization of epilepsy syndrome. Assess for photosensitivity
(NCL suspected).

TECHNIQUE: Continuous digital video-EEG monitoring using the international
10-20 electrode placement system with additional T1/T2 electrodes. 48-hour
recording with continuous video and audio. Activation procedures: hypervent-
ilation (not performed — patient unable to cooperate), photic stimulation
(performed at 1, 2, 3, 5, 8, 10, 15, 20, 25, and 30 Hz at multiple
intervals), and sleep recording (natural sleep captured — no sedation
administered for EEG). Anti-seizure medications continued at current doses
during recording (levetiracetam 750mg BID, clobazam 5mg BID).

BACKGROUND ACTIVITY:
  Wakefulness: The background is SEVERELY DISORGANIZED and DIFFUSELY SLOW.
  There is NO discernible posterior dominant rhythm (alpha rhythm). The
  predominant background frequency is 3-5 Hz theta and 1-3 Hz delta
  activity, moderate to high amplitude (100-200 microvolts), distributed
  diffusely but with maximal amplitude over posterior regions. No reactive
  or organized alpha activity is identified. There is no anterior-posterior
  gradient. Continuous irregular slow activity with intermixed sharp
  transients is present throughout the recording.

  Sleep: Sleep architecture is SEVERELY DISRUPTED. Sleep spindles and vertex
  waves are ABSENT. K-complexes are not identifiable. NREM sleep is
  characterized by continuous high-amplitude irregular delta activity with
  frequent epileptiform discharges (see below). REM sleep was not clearly
  identified during the recording.

EPILEPTIFORM ACTIVITY:
  Interictal: FREQUENT generalized epileptiform discharges are present
  throughout the recording, occurring in wakefulness and sleep.
    - Generalized 1.5-2.5 Hz spike-and-wave complexes: occurring in runs
      of 1-5 seconds duration, sometimes with associated subtle clinical
      change (behavioral arrest), sometimes subclinical. These are
      frontally predominant with diffuse field.
    - Generalized polyspike-and-wave discharges: 2-3 Hz, high amplitude
      (200-400 microvolts), occurring in brief bursts (0.5-2 seconds).
      These are frequently followed by myoclonic jerks (correlating with
      video).
    - Focal sharp waves: intermittent right posterior (P4, O2) sharp waves
      and sharp-slow complexes, occurring independently of the generalized
      discharges. These suggest a right posterior epileptogenic focus.
    - Multifocal sharp waves: occasional independent sharp waves from left
      temporal, right frontal, and bilateral parietal regions — consistent
      with diffuse cortical irritability.

ICTAL EVENTS CAPTURED:

  Total seizure events captured during 48-hour recording: 32

  Type 1 — Absence seizures (28 events):
    Clinical: Behavioral arrest lasting 5-20 seconds (mean 10 seconds).
    Subtle eyelid flutter in most events. Cessation of ongoing activity
    (hand-wringing stops). Brief post-ictal confusion (5-10 seconds).
    EEG: Generalized 2-2.5 Hz spike-and-wave discharge, onset and offset
    abrupt, duration correlating with clinical event. Maximum amplitude
    frontally. No focal onset identified — generalized from onset.
    Frequency: 28 events over 48 hours (average 14/day).

  Type 2 — Myoclonic seizures (3 events):
    Clinical: Brief (<1 second) bilateral symmetric myoclonic jerks of
    shoulders and arms, higher amplitude than the isolated interictal
    myoclonic jerks. One event resulted in arms flying upward. No loss
    of consciousness.
    EEG: Generalized high-amplitude polyspike discharge (200-400 uV)
    lasting 0.5-1.5 seconds, immediately preceding and time-locked to the
    myoclonic jerk. Generalized distribution with frontocentral maximum.
    These events are distinct from the more frequent isolated myoclonic
    jerks, which correlate with lower-amplitude polyspike fragments.

  Type 3 — Focal seizure with secondary generalization (1 event):
    Clinical (captured on video at 02:14 AM on night 2): Patient was in
    sleep. Onset: right head and eye deviation, followed by right arm
    tonic extension. After 15 seconds, bilateral tonic stiffening of all
    extremities, followed by bilateral clonic (rhythmic jerking) activity
    lasting approximately 30 seconds. Total seizure duration: 45 seconds.
    Post-ictal: unresponsive for approximately 3 minutes, then gradually
    returned to baseline sleep.
    EEG: Onset with rhythmic 8-10 Hz beta activity maximal at P4-O2
    (RIGHT POSTERIOR), evolving over 10 seconds to rhythmic 4-5 Hz
    theta, then spreading bilaterally with generalized 2-3 Hz rhythmic
    delta during the bilateral clonic phase. Post-ictal diffuse
    suppression for approximately 60 seconds followed by gradual return
    of background.

PHOTIC STIMULATION RESULTS:
  GROSSLY EXAGGERATED photic driving response. At LOW flash frequencies
  (1-3 Hz), photic stimulation elicited high-amplitude (300-500 uV)
  spike-and-wave discharges that were time-locked to the flash frequency.
  These discharges were associated with subtle myoclonic jerks of the
  eyelids and face. At 1 Hz, giant spike-and-wave complexes were elicited
  with each flash. At 2-3 Hz, runs of spike-and-wave activity were
  triggered that persisted for 2-3 seconds after cessation of the photic
  stimulus. At higher frequencies (>5 Hz), photic driving was present but
  without the exaggerated spike-and-wave response.

  This finding — an exaggerated photoparoxysmal response at LOW flash
  frequencies (1-3 Hz) — is CHARACTERISTIC of neuronal ceroid
  lipofuscinosis and is among the most specific EEG findings associated
  with NCL. It has been particularly well-described in CLN2, CLN5, and
  CLN6 disease.

INTERPRETATION:

1. SEVERELY ABNORMAL 48-hour continuous EEG.

2. Background: Severely disorganized and diffusely slow, consistent with
   diffuse cerebral dysfunction / encephalopathy. Absence of normal sleep
   architecture indicates severe thalamocortical network dysfunction.

3. Progressive myoclonic epilepsy with multiple seizure types:
   a. Absence seizures (28 events / 48 hours) — generalized spike-and-wave
   b. Myoclonic seizures (3 events) — generalized polyspike-and-wave
   c. Focal seizure with secondary generalization (1 event) — right
      posterior onset (P4-O2)
   d. Frequent interictal generalized and multifocal epileptiform discharges

4. The focal onset of the seizure originating from the right posterior
   region is consistent with the predominant parieto-occipital cortical
   atrophy observed on MRI.

5. GROSSLY EXAGGERATED LOW-FREQUENCY PHOTIC DRIVING RESPONSE — this
   finding is highly characteristic of neuronal ceroid lipofuscinosis.

6. CLINICAL CORRELATION: The combination of progressive myoclonic epilepsy
   with an exaggerated low-frequency photic response, in the setting of
   progressive neurodegeneration, cerebellar atrophy, and visual loss, is
   HIGHLY SUGGESTIVE of neuronal ceroid lipofuscinosis (NCL). This EEG
   pattern, combined with the clinical and imaging findings, constitutes
   a compelling case for NCL. Genetic confirmation is essential.

  Full-field ERG performed under sedation (as above) using DTL corneal

      prolonged bilaterally.
    - 3.0 cd.s/m² (combined rod-cone response): SEVERELY ATTENUATED.
      a-wave: OD 25 uV, OS 28 uV (normal >100 uV). b-wave: OD 42 uV,
      OS 48 uV (normal >200 uV). b/a ratio preserved.
    - 3.0 cd.s/m² oscillatory potentials: Markedly reduced amplitude.

    Light-adapted (photopic) responses:
    - 3.0 cd.s/m² (cone response): SEVERELY ATTENUATED. a-wave: OD 8 uV,
      OS 10 uV (normal >30 uV). b-wave: OD 22 uV, OS 26 uV (normal
      >80 uV).
    - 30 Hz flicker: SEVERELY ATTENUATED. Amplitude: OD 10 uV, OS 13 uV
      (normal >50 uV). Implicit time: prolonged.

    ERG Summary: SEVERELY ABNORMAL — grossly attenuated scotopic and
    photopic responses bilaterally, indicating combined ROD AND CONE
    DYSFUNCTION. The degree of attenuation (>75% reduction from normal
    in all parameters) indicates severe, widespread photoreceptor
    degeneration.

  OCT (Optical Coherence Tomography) — limited study (patient sedated,
  alignment challenging):
    Central foveal thickness: OD 185 um, OS 190 um (reduced; normal
    ~230-260 um for age).
    Peripapillary RNFL thickness: OD average 52 um, OS average 56 um
    (severely reduced; normal >85 um for age).
    Outer nuclear layer: Diffusely thinned bilaterally — consistent with
    photoreceptor loss.
    Ellipsoid zone: Disrupted and discontinuous throughout the macula
    bilaterally — indicates photoreceptor inner segment/outer segment
    junction damage.

ASSESSMENT:

1. SEVERE RETINAL DEGENERATION with combined rod and cone photoreceptor
   dysfunction, bilateral, as demonstrated by grossly abnormal ERG and
   OCT findings.
2. BILATERAL OPTIC ATROPHY — secondary to retinal degeneration and/or
   primary optic nerve involvement by the neurodegenerative process.
3. BULL'S EYE MACULOPATHY — bilateral, consistent with lysosomal storage
   disease-related retinal degeneration.
4. Functional vision: SEVERELY IMPAIRED. Likely meets criteria for legal
   blindness (best corrected visual acuity estimated at count fingers or
   worse bilaterally).

CLINICAL CORRELATION:
In the clinical context of progressive neurodegeneration, epilepsy,
cerebellar atrophy, and the EEG findings reported by Dr. Park, these
ophthalmologic findings are MOST CONSISTENT WITH NEURONAL CEROID
LIPOFUSCINOSIS (NCL). The triad of:
  (a) progressive retinal degeneration with rod-cone dysfunction,
  (b) bull's eye maculopathy, and
  (c) optic atrophy
is characteristic of NCL, particularly the late-infantile variants (CLN5,
CLN6, CLN7, CLN8). The severity of retinal involvement and the pattern
of combined rod-cone dysfunction distinguish this from other causes of
pediatric retinal degeneration.

RECOMMENDATIONS:
1. Genetic confirmation of NCL diagnosis is essential.
2. Low-vision services referral — orientation and mobility training,
   adaptive devices.
3. Certification for visual impairment/blindness for educational and
   support services.
4. Follow-up in 6 months to monitor progression.
5. Report sent to Dr. Samuel Park and Dr. Lisa Washington.

Aisha Patel, MD

INSURANCE CORRESPONDENCE — PRIOR AUTHORIZATION DENIAL AND APPEAL
----------------------------------------------------------------------------

=== TRICARE PRIOR AUTHORIZATION DETERMINATION ===
Date: 07/02/2024
Reference #: PA-2024-TC-4481920
Beneficiary: REEVES, ELI MARCUS | DEERS ID: 1098745632
Sponsor: SGT Marcus Reeves, USA
Requesting Provider: Samuel Park, MD — Womack AMC
Requested Service: Whole Exome Sequencing (WES), CPT 81415/81416
Testing Laboratory: GeneDx, Inc.

DETERMINATION: DENIED — NOT MEDICALLY NECESSARY

RATIONALE:
The requested genetic testing panel (whole exome sequencing, CPT 81415 and
81416) is not medically necessary per TRICARE plan guidelines and clinical
policy bulletin CPB-2023-GEN-047 (Genetic and Genomic Testing).

Per policy, whole exome sequencing may be considered medically necessary when:
  (a) The patient has a phenotype strongly suggestive of a genetic etiology,
      AND
  (b) A specific clinical diagnosis has not been established after standard
      clinical evaluation, AND
  (c) Single-gene or targeted panel testing has been performed and is
      non-diagnostic, OR a specific single-gene test is not available for
      the suspected condition.

In this case, criterion (c) has not been met. The clinical documentation
indicates a suspected diagnosis of neuronal ceroid lipofuscinosis (NCL).
Targeted gene panel testing for NCL (CLN1-14) is available and is a more
cost-effective initial approach than whole exome sequencing. ALTERNATIVE:
Single-gene testing or a targeted NCL gene panel may be considered if a
specific diagnosis is suspected. The requesting provider may resubmit with
a request for targeted panel testing, or may appeal this determination.

Appeal rights: You may appeal this determination within 90 days. Appeals
should be submitted in writing to TRICARE Appeals, P.O. Box 7031,
Camden, SC 29020, or faxed to (844) 555-0199.

TRICARE Health Plan Medical Director
Electronically generated: 07/02/2024

=== APPEAL LETTER — DR. SAMUEL PARK ===

Date: 07/18/2024
To: TRICARE Appeals Department
Re: Appeal of Prior Authorization Denial — PA-2024-TC-4481920
Patient: Eli Marcus Reeves, DOB 03/15/2012, DEERS ID 1098745632

Dear TRICARE Medical Director:

I am writing to appeal the denial of prior authorization for whole exome
sequencing (WES) for my patient, Eli Reeves, a 12-year-old male with a
progressive, undiagnosed neurodegenerative condition.

I respectfully submit that the denial rationale reflects a misunderstanding
of the clinical situation and the appropriate diagnostic approach for this
patient. I address each point below:

REGARDING THE RECOMMENDATION FOR SINGLE-GENE OR TARGETED PANEL TESTING:

The denial letter suggests that "single-gene testing or a targeted NCL gene
panel" is an appropriate alternative to WES. I disagree for the following
reasons:

1. While the clinical presentation is MOST CONSISTENT with neuronal ceroid
   lipofuscinosis (NCL), the differential diagnosis includes multiple other
   neurodegenerative conditions (mitochondrial disorders, NBIA,
   gangliosidoses, Niemann-Pick type C, and others). Single-gene testing
   is NOT appropriate in this case because the clinical presentation is
   consistent with multiple possible NCL subtypes (CLN1 through CLN14, each
   caused by a different gene) AS WELL AS numerous other neurodegenerative
   conditions. Sequential single-gene testing would require testing 14+
   genes individually, at a cost and time burden that far exceeds WES.

2. Even a targeted NCL panel (CLN1-14) may be insufficient. If NCL is
   ruled out, additional panels would be required (mitochondrial
   genome, NBIA genes, lysosomal storage genes, etc.). WES evaluates all
   of these simultaneously and is BOTH MORE COST-EFFECTIVE AND MORE
   TIME-EFFICIENT than sequential panel testing in the setting of an
   undiagnosed neurodegenerative condition.

3. Whole exome sequencing is the STANDARD OF CARE for undiagnosed
   neurodegenerative conditions per the American College of Medical
   Genetics and Genomics (ACMG) guidelines (2023). The ACMG position
   statement on genomic sequencing in pediatric patients states that WES
   (or whole genome sequencing) should be considered a first-tier test
   for patients with: (a) intellectual disability or developmental delay
   of suspected genetic etiology, (b) multiple congenital anomalies, or
   (c) suspected genetic conditions where the differential diagnosis
   includes multiple genes. This patient meets criteria (a) and (c).

4. TIME IS OF THE ESSENCE. This patient has had symptoms for over 10 years
   with a diagnostic delay due to misdiagnosis and fragmented care across
   multiple states. His condition is progressive and deteriorating. Rapid
   definitive diagnosis may identify targeted therapeutic options including
   enzyme replacement therapy (available for CLN2) or gene therapy clinical
   trials (ongoing for CLN3, CLN5, CLN6). Every month of additional
   diagnostic delay represents further irreversible neurological decline.

CLINICAL SUMMARY SUPPORTING MEDICAL NECESSITY:

This 12-year-old male has a 10-year history of progressive neurological
decline initially misdiagnosed as autism spectrum disorder. His clinical
features include:
  - Progressive cognitive and motor regression since age 2-3
  - Progressive myoclonic epilepsy (multiple seizure types, refractory)
  - Progressive cerebellar and cerebral atrophy (documented on serial MRI)
  - Progressive retinal degeneration with rod-cone dysfunction (ERG
    severely abnormal)
  - Bilateral optic atrophy
  - Exaggerated low-frequency photic response on EEG (characteristic of NCL)
  - Persistently elevated lactate
  - Growth failure

Despite evaluation by multiple specialists in three states over 12 years,
he does NOT have a definitive genetic diagnosis. The etiology is UNKNOWN.
WES is the most appropriate, efficient, and cost-effective path to
diagnosis.

I strongly urge reconsideration of this denial. I am available to discuss
this case by phone at any time.

Sincerely,

Samuel Park, MD
Pediatric Neurology
Womack Army Medical Center
Fort Liberty, NC 28310
Phone: (910) 555-0277

  of facial coarsening, hepatosplenomegaly, skeletal dysplasia, and
  angiokeratomas narrows the differential significantly and is
  CONSISTENT with NCL, which is a "non-dysmorphic" neurodegenerative
  storage disorder.

V. REVIEW OF DIAGNOSTIC DATA:

  Imaging: [See detailed MRI reports in chart — summarized in Dr. Park's
  notes. Progressive cerebellar > cerebral atrophy, white matter changes,
  thalamic mineralization. Pattern consistent with NCL.]

  Electrophysiology: [See detailed EEG report — progressive myoclonic
  epilepsy with exaggerated low-frequency photic response, characteristic
  of NCL.]

  Ophthalmology: [See Dr. Patel's report — severe retinal degeneration,
  rod-cone dysfunction, optic atrophy, bull's eye maculopathy. Consistent
  with NCL.]

  Metabolic studies: Persistently elevated lactate (2.8 at age 9, 3.1 at
  age 12). Mildly abnormal MR spectroscopy. Otherwise non-diagnostic
  metabolic workup.

VI. LITERATURE REVIEW AND DIFFERENTIAL DIAGNOSIS:

I have performed a focused literature review to inform this evaluation.
The clinical features most relevant to the differential diagnosis are:
  (1) Age of onset: 18-24 months (regression); clinical diagnosis at age 3
  (2) Progressive course affecting cognition, motor, language, vision
  (3) Seizure types: absence, myoclonic, focal — progressive myoclonic
      epilepsy
  (4) Imaging: Cerebellar > cerebral atrophy, thalamic T2 hypointensity
  (5) ERG: Severely abnormal (rod-cone dystrophy)
  (6) EEG: Exaggerated low-frequency photic response
  (7) No dysmorphic features, no hepatosplenomegaly

Based on these features, the differential diagnosis is:

  1. NEURONAL CEROID LIPOFUSCINOSIS (NCL) — HIGHEST CLINICAL SUSPICION
     The clinical phenotype is classic for late-infantile NCL. Based on the
     age of onset (2-3 years) and the specific constellation of features,
     the most likely subtypes are:

     a. CLN5 disease (Finnish variant late infantile NCL):
        - Typical onset age 4-7 years (but reported as early as 2-3)
        - Progressive motor and cognitive decline, seizures (including
          myoclonic), visual loss, cerebellar atrophy
        - Caused by biallelic pathogenic variants in CLN5 (soluble
          lysosomal protein)
        - Autosomal recessive. Originally described in Finland but now
          recognized worldwide.
        - The age of onset in this patient (2-3 years) is somewhat earlier
          than the classic CLN5 description, but early-onset cases have
          been reported, particularly with certain severe mutations.

     b. CLN6 disease (variant late infantile NCL):
        - Typical onset age 18 months — 8 years
        - Very similar phenotype to CLN5
        - Caused by biallelic pathogenic variants in CLN6 (ER membrane
          protein)
        - Autosomal recessive. Reported worldwide.

     c. CLN7 disease (Turkish variant late infantile NCL):
        - Caused by MFSD8 gene variants. Similar phenotype. Less common.

     d. CLN8 disease:
        - Caused by CLN8 gene variants. Can present as late infantile or
          as "Northern epilepsy" (milder form).

     e. Late-onset CLN2:
        - Caused by TPP1 gene variants. Classic form presents at 2-4
          years but late-onset forms exist. TPP1 enzyme activity testing
          can screen for this.

  2. Mitochondrial disorder — LOWER SUSPICION given overall phenotype
     - Elevated lactate is nonspecific and can be secondary in NCL
     - Absence of characteristic MRI pattern for mitochondrial disease
       (no basal ganglia involvement, no brainstem lesions)
     - Progressive retinal degeneration is atypical for most mitochondrial
       disorders (except Kearns-Sayre, which has different features)

  3. NBIA (Neurodegeneration with brain iron accumulation) — LOW SUSPICION
     - Thalamic T2 hypointensity could suggest iron; however, the
       characteristic "eye of the tiger" sign in globus pallidus is absent
     - Retinal degeneration not typical for most NBIA subtypes

  4. GM2 gangliosidosis (late-onset Tay-Sachs or Sandhoff) — LOW
     - No cherry-red spot. Late-onset forms possible but typically have
       different presentation.

  5. Niemann-Pick Type C — LOW
     - No hepatosplenomegaly. Vertical supranuclear gaze palsy not noted.

VII. ASSESSMENT:

This patient has a progressive neurodegenerative condition that has been
symptomatic since approximately age 2-3 years. The clinical features —
progressive cognitive and motor regression, seizures (absence, myoclonic,
and focal), cerebellar atrophy, retinal degeneration, and optic atrophy —
are most consistent with Neuronal Ceroid Lipofuscinosis.

Based on the age of onset and phenotypic features, the most likely subtypes
are CLN5 (Finnish variant late infantile) or CLN6 (variant late infantile),
though CLN7 and CLN8 cannot be excluded on clinical grounds alone.

This patient has been misdiagnosed with autism spectrum disorder for
approximately 10 years. While some behavioral features at the time of
initial evaluation overlapped with ASD diagnostic criteria (limited speech,
repetitive hand movements, reduced social engagement), the progressive
course clearly distinguishes this from a primary neurodevelopmental
condition. Autism spectrum disorder, by definition, is not characterized
by progressive loss of motor function, progressive seizure disorder,
cerebellar atrophy, or retinal degeneration. The features attributed to
ASD — the hand-wringing stereotypies, the social withdrawal, the
communication limitations — were, with high probability, early
manifestations of the neurodegenerative process.

This represents a significant diagnostic delay — approximately 10 years
from the onset of symptoms to the initiation of the genetic workup that
will likely yield a definitive diagnosis. This delay was contributed to by
multiple factors including: (1) diagnostic anchoring on the ASD diagnosis,
(2) fragmented care across three states due to military relocations,
(3) incomplete records transfer, (4) failure of multiple providers to
recognize the PROGRESSIVE nature of the condition as inconsistent with
ASD, and (5) insurance barriers to genetic testing. While the disease
itself has no cure at present, earlier diagnosis would have provided:
accurate prognostic information, access to supportive services, potential
enrollment in clinical trials at an earlier disease stage, and avoidance
of years of inappropriate ASD-directed therapies.

VIII. PLAN:

1. GENETIC TESTING (insurance now approved — appeal granted 10/28/2024):
   a. Whole exome sequencing (WES) — GeneDx, Inc.
      - Trio analysis: patient + both parents (to determine inheritance
        pattern and variant phasing)
      - Priority analysis: NCL gene panel (CLN1-14) with reflex to full
        exome if NCL panel negative
      - Both parents consented and blood drawn today for parental samples
      - Patient blood drawn today
      - Estimated turnaround: 12-16 weeks

   b. Enzyme activity assays (send-out to Batten Disease Diagnostic and
      Clinical Research Center at University of Rochester):
      - PPT1 (palmitoyl-protein thioesterase 1) — enzyme deficient in
        CLN1 disease
      - TPP1 (tripeptidyl peptidase 1) — enzyme deficient in CLN2
        disease
      - These enzyme assays can rapidly rule in or rule out CLN1 and CLN2,
        which have available enzyme replacement therapies (cerliponase alfa
        for CLN2)
      - Results expected in 2-4 weeks

   c. Skin biopsy for electron microscopy:
      - Performed today (3mm punch biopsy, left upper arm, local
        anesthesia)
      - Specimens sent to Duke Pathology EM laboratory
      - If NCL is present, EM will show characteristic storage material
        inclusions:
        * Curvilinear profiles (CLN2, CLN5, CLN6)
        * Fingerprint bodies (CLN5, CLN6, CLN8)
        * Granular osmiophilic deposits (CLN1)
        * Rectilinear profiles (CLN3)
      - The pattern of inclusions can help narrow the NCL subtype while
        awaiting genetic results
      - Results expected in 4-6 weeks

   d. Mitochondrial DNA sequencing panel — ordered as secondary
      consideration given persistently elevated lactate. If WES identifies
      a nuclear gene cause, mitochondrial sequencing may not be needed.

2. GENETIC COUNSELING:
   - Extensive genetic counseling provided by Lauren Ashford, MS, CGC
     during today's visit (45 minutes).
   - Topics covered: autosomal recessive inheritance, carrier status,
     implications for future family planning, cascade testing for
     extended family members if pathogenic variants identified.
   - Both parents understand that if Eli has NCL:
     * Both parents are obligate carriers (one variant each)
     * Each future pregnancy would have a 25% chance of being affected
     * Carrier testing and prenatal diagnosis would be available
   - Family provided written materials on NCL/Batten disease.

3. Follow-up appointment scheduled in 4 weeks for enzyme assay results,
   and in 12-16 weeks for WES results.

SKIN BIOPSY — ELECTRON MICROSCOPY REPORT:
Date Resulted: 01/22/2025
Performing Laboratory: Duke University Medical Center — Electron Microscopy
  Laboratory, Department of Pathology
Pathologist: Dr. Thomas Nakamura, MD — Ultrastructural Pathology

SPECIMEN: Skin punch biopsy, left upper arm (3mm), received 12/02/2024.

LIGHT MICROSCOPY: Unremarkable skin biopsy. Epidermis and dermis
within normal limits. Eccrine sweat glands present and unremarkable on
H&E staining.

ELECTRON MICROSCOPY:

  Lymphocytes (peripheral blood buffy coat, submitted concurrently):
    Numerous MEMBRANE-BOUND CYTOPLASMIC INCLUSIONS identified within
    lymphocytes. The inclusions measure 0.5-2.0 micrometers in diameter
    and contain:
    - CURVILINEAR PROFILES: The predominant pattern. Dense, curved
      lamellae arranged in stacks, with a characteristic "fingerprint"-
      like periodicity of approximately 5 nm. These are typical of the
      storage material found in several NCL subtypes.
    - FINGERPRINT BODY PROFILES: Admixed with the curvilinear profiles.
      These show parallel curved lines with a wider periodicity
      (~8-10 nm) resembling dermatoglyphic ridges.
    - The MIXED curvilinear and fingerprint pattern is characteristic of
      CLN5 and CLN6 disease.

  Eccrine sweat gland epithelium:
    Similar membrane-bound inclusions identified within sweat gland
    epithelial cells, confirming widespread storage material
    accumulation. The inclusions contain predominantly curvilinear
    profiles.

  Fibroblasts:
    Rare inclusions identified. Less prominent than in lymphocytes.

  Neurons (not available in this specimen — skin biopsy).

INTERPRETATION:
Numerous curvilinear and fingerprint profile inclusions identified in
lymphocytes and sweat gland epithelium. These findings are DIAGNOSTIC
for neuronal ceroid lipofuscinosis (NCL). The mixed curvilinear /
fingerprint pattern is most commonly associated with CLN5 or CLN6
disease, though it has also been reported in CLN8. Correlation with
genetic testing is recommended for definitive subtype classification.

GENETIC TESTING RESULTS — WHOLE EXOME SEQUENCING
----------------------------------------------------------------------------
Date Resulted: 03/28/2025
Patient: REEVES, ELI MARCUS | DOB: 03/15/2012 | Age: 13 years 0 months
MRN: DUKE-4829175
Ordering Physician: Rachel Kim, MD, PhD — Duke Genetics

=== CLINICAL GENETIC TESTING REPORT ===
Laboratory: GeneDx, Inc., 207 Perry Parkway, Gaithersburg, MD 20877
CLIA #: 21D0919868 | CAP #: 7193779
Accession #: GDX-2025-0441829
Test Performed: Whole Exome Sequencing — Trio (Proband + Parents)
Specimen: Whole blood (proband), whole blood (mother), whole blood (father)
Date Received: 12/10/2024
Date Reported: 03/28/2025

CLINICAL INDICATION: Progressive neurodegenerative disease. Suspected
neuronal ceroid lipofuscinosis. Progressive myoclonic epilepsy. Cerebellar
atrophy. Retinal degeneration. EM confirms NCL storage material.

METHODOLOGY: Genomic DNA was extracted from peripheral blood. Exome capture
was performed using the Agilent SureSelect Clinical Research Exome V2 kit.
Sequencing was performed on an Illumina NovaSeq 6000 platform with paired-
end reads. Mean coverage depth: >100x. >98% of targeted bases covered at
>20x. Sequence data was aligned to the GRCh38 reference genome. Variant
calling, annotation, and analysis were performed using GeneDx's proprietary
bioinformatics pipeline. Trio analysis was performed with parental samples
to determine de novo status and inheritance pattern of identified variants.
Copy number analysis from exome data was performed using GeneDx's CNV
detection algorithm. Variants were classified according to ACMG/AMP 2015
guidelines.

RESULTS:

  PRIMARY FINDING:

  TWO PATHOGENIC VARIANTS IDENTIFIED IN THE CLN5 GENE

  ---------------------------------------------------------------
  Gene:     CLN5 (OMIM *608102)
  ---------------------------------------------------------------

  Variant 1:
    Nucleotide change:  c.291+1G>A
    Protein change:     N/A (splice site variant)
    Variant type:       Splice donor site variant
    Zygosity:           Heterozygous
    Inheritance:        PATERNALLY INHERITED (confirmed in father's sample)
    Classification:     PATHOGENIC (Class 5)

    Evidence for pathogenicity:
    - PVS1: Null variant in a gene where loss of function is a known
      mechanism of disease
    - PM2: Absent from gnomAD population database (not observed in
      >250,000 alleles)
    - PM3: Detected in trans with a second pathogenic variant in a
      patient with a phenotype consistent with CLN5 disease
    - PP4: Patient's phenotype is highly specific for CLN5 disease
    - Literature: This variant has been previously reported in two
      unrelated families with CLN5 disease (Xin et al., 2010; Mancini
      et al., 2015). The c.291+1G>A variant disrupts the canonical GT
      splice donor site of intron 2 of the CLN5 gene, predicted to
      result in aberrant splicing and loss of functional protein.

  Variant 2:
    Nucleotide change:  c.726G>A
    Protein change:     p.Trp242Ter (p.W242*)
    Variant type:       Nonsense (stop-gain) variant
    Zygosity:           Heterozygous
    Inheritance:        MATERNALLY INHERITED (confirmed in mother's sample)
    Classification:     PATHOGENIC (Class 5)

    Evidence for pathogenicity:
    - PVS1: Null variant (premature stop codon) in a gene where loss
      of function is a known mechanism of disease
    - PM2: Rare in gnomAD (allele frequency 0.000008, 2 heterozygous
      carriers in African/African American population, no homozygotes)
    - PM3: Detected in trans with a second pathogenic variant in a
      patient with a phenotype consistent with CLN5 disease
    - PP4: Patient's phenotype is highly specific for CLN5 disease
    - Literature: The c.726G>A (p.Trp242Ter) variant introduces a
      premature termination codon at position 242 of the 407-amino-acid
      CLN5 protein. This variant has been reported in one prior case of
      CLN5 disease in a patient of African American ancestry (Kousi et
      al., 2012). The truncated protein is predicted to be nonfunctional
      and subject to nonsense-mediated mRNA decay.

  Compound heterozygous configuration: CONFIRMED by trio analysis. The
  c.291+1G>A variant was identified in the father's sample (heterozygous
  carrier), and the c.726G>A variant was identified in the mother's sample
  (heterozygous carrier). The proband carries both variants, one on each
  allele (compound heterozygous). This is consistent with autosomal
  recessive inheritance of CLN5 disease.

  ---------------------------------------------------------------

  SECONDARY FINDINGS:

  Mitochondrial DNA analysis (performed concurrently):
    One variant of uncertain significance (VUS) identified:
    m.4216T>C (MT-ND1, p.Tyr304His)
    - This is a known polymorphism present in ~2% of the European
      population. It has been reported in some studies as associated with
      Leber hereditary optic neuropathy (LHON) as a "secondary" variant,
      but its pathogenicity is uncertain, particularly in the context
      of a confirmed CLN5 diagnosis. This variant is NOT considered to
      be the cause of the patient's condition and is likely an incidental
      finding.

  No additional pathogenic or likely pathogenic variants identified in
  other genes associated with neurodegenerative disease, epilepsy, or
  intellectual disability.

  ACMG Secondary Findings (V3.1): No reportable secondary findings in
  the 78 ACMG recommended genes.

INTERPRETATION:

Whole exome sequencing identified two pathogenic variants in the CLN5
gene in compound heterozygous configuration. These findings are
DIAGNOSTIC for CLN5 disease (Finnish variant late infantile neuronal
ceroid lipofuscinosis; OMIM #256731).

CLN5 disease is an autosomal recessive lysosomal storage disorder caused
by biallelic loss-of-function variants in the CLN5 gene, which encodes a
soluble lysosomal protein of incompletely understood function (likely
involved in lipid metabolism and intracellular trafficking). Disease
results in accumulation of ceroid lipofuscin storage material in neurons
and other cell types, leading to progressive neurodegeneration.

The two identified variants — a splice site variant (c.291+1G>A,
paternal) and a nonsense variant (c.726G>A, maternal) — are both predicted
to result in complete loss of CLN5 protein function, consistent with the
severe phenotype observed in the patient.

Parental carrier testing confirms that each parent carries one pathogenic
variant in the heterozygous state, as expected for autosomal recessive
inheritance.

  ---------------------------------------------------------------
  RECOMMENDATIONS:
  1. This result confirms the diagnosis of CLN5 disease.
  2. Genetic counseling for the family regarding recurrence risk and
     reproductive options.
  3. Carrier testing should be offered to at-risk relatives.
  4. Clinical management per published guidelines for NCL/Batten disease.
  5. Consider referral for clinical trial eligibility (gene therapy
     trials for CLN5 are in development).
  ---------------------------------------------------------------

Report reviewed and approved by:
  Sarah Chen, PhD, FACMG — Laboratory Director, GeneDx
  Date: 03/28/2025
