DIAGNOSIS CONFIRMED:

  CLN5 NEURONAL CEROID LIPOFUSCINOSIS (BATTEN DISEASE)
  OMIM #256731
  Also known as: Finnish variant late infantile NCL; CLN5 disease

  Confirmed by:
  - Whole exome sequencing: compound heterozygous pathogenic variants in
    CLN5 (c.291+1G>A, paternal; c.726G>A, p.Trp242Ter, maternal)
  - Skin biopsy EM: curvilinear and fingerprint body inclusions
    (diagnostic for NCL)
  - Enzyme assays: normal PPT1 and TPP1 (ruling out CLN1 and CLN2)
  - Clinical phenotype: progressive neurodegeneration, myoclonic epilepsy,
    visual loss, cerebellar atrophy — classic for CLN5

DIAGNOSTIC TIMELINE:

This diagnosis was achieved approximately 12 YEARS after symptom onset
(first concerns at age 2; confirmed genetic diagnosis at age 13) and
10 YEARS after the initial misdiagnosis of autism spectrum disorder
(ASD diagnosed at age 3.5; CLN5 confirmed at age 13).

The ASD diagnosis is hereby formally REMOVED from the problem list.
The patient's condition is fully explained by CLN5 disease. All features
previously attributed to ASD — including stereotypic hand movements,
limited social engagement, restricted communication, and behavioral
regression — are manifestations of the progressive neurodegenerative
process.

GENETIC COUNSELING — RESULTS SESSION:

The following topics were discussed in detail with both parents:

1. DIAGNOSIS EXPLANATION:
   CLN5 disease is a rare, autosomal recessive neurodegenerative disorder
   belonging to the neuronal ceroid lipofuscinoses (NCL / Batten disease
   family). It is caused by deficiency of the CLN5 protein, which results
   in accumulation of storage material (ceroid lipofuscin) in neurons
   throughout the brain and retina, leading to progressive cell death.
   There are an estimated 50-100 known living patients with CLN5 disease
   worldwide. The condition is progressive.

2. PROGNOSIS:
   CLN5 disease follows a progressive course. Eli's current clinical
   status — wheelchair dependence, near-complete loss of speech, severe
   visual impairment, refractory epilepsy — is consistent with the
   expected disease trajectory. Discussed with family that the disease
   continues to progress, with further decline in neurological function
   expected. Life expectancy is variable; reported survival in CLN5
   disease ranges from the mid-teens to the early 30s, depending on the
   severity of the genetic variants and the quality of supportive care.
   Cause of death is typically aspiration pneumonia or complications of
   prolonged immobility.

   Both parents were emotional during this discussion. Mother stated: "I
   have known something was terribly wrong since he was 2 years old. I
   just wanted someone to believe me." Father asked again: "If they had
   tested him earlier, could they have done anything?" I explained that
   while there is no cure for CLN5, earlier diagnosis could have:
     - Avoided years of inappropriate ASD-directed therapy
     - Provided accurate prognostic information earlier
     - Enabled enrollment in clinical trials at earlier disease stages
       when more neurological function was preserved
     - Facilitated family planning decisions
     - Reduced the emotional burden of diagnostic uncertainty

3. CARRIER STATUS AND FAMILY PLANNING:
   - Father (Marcus Reeves): Confirmed carrier of CLN5 c.291+1G>A.
     Carrier status has no known health implications (carriers are
     typically asymptomatic).
   - Mother (Denise Reeves): Confirmed carrier of CLN5 c.726G>A.
     Carrier status has no known health implications.
   - For each future pregnancy: 25% chance of affected child (CLN5
     disease), 50% chance of carrier (asymptomatic), 25% chance of
     non-carrier. Prenatal diagnosis is available through CVS or
     amniocentesis. Preimplantation genetic testing (PGT-M) is available
     with IVF.
   - Mother asked about this. She stated they had considered having
     another child before the diagnosis. She and her husband will discuss
     and follow up.

4. CASCADE TESTING:
   - Paternal relatives: Father's siblings (1 brother, 1 sister) should
     be offered carrier testing for c.291+1G>A. If they are carriers,
     their reproductive partners could also be tested.
   - Maternal relatives: Mother's siblings (2 sisters, 1 brother) should
     be offered carrier testing for c.726G>A.
   - Letters prepared for family members explaining the genetic finding
     and recommending carrier testing through their own healthcare
     providers.
   - NOTE regarding paternal grandmother (Gloria Reeves, deceased):
     The paternal grandmother's "early-onset dementia" remains of
     uncertain relationship to CLN5. She was an obligate carrier of the
     c.291+1G>A variant (having passed it to the father). While CLN5
     carriers are generally asymptomatic, there is emerging but
     inconclusive literature suggesting that heterozygous carriers of
     some NCL variants may have a modestly increased risk of
     neurodegenerative disease in later life. This cannot be confirmed
     or refuted without a sample from the grandmother (unavailable).
     This was discussed with the family as a possibility but not a
     certainty.

5. AVAILABLE THERAPIES AND CLINICAL TRIALS:
   a. Enzyme replacement therapy: Cerliponase alfa (Brineura) is FDA-
      approved for CLN2 disease only. NOT applicable to CLN5.
   b. Gene therapy:
      - A phase 1/2 gene therapy trial for CLN5 disease (AAV-mediated
        intrathecal delivery of CLN5 gene) is being conducted at
        University College London (UCL) and Nationwide Children's
        Hospital (Columbus, OH). Current enrollment criteria require
        patients to have some residual motor and cognitive function.
        Given Eli's advanced disease stage, he may not meet inclusion
        criteria, but a referral for evaluation has been submitted.
      - A phase 1 gene therapy trial for CLN6 disease (Brineura Gene
        Therapy, NCT02725580) at Nationwide Children's — not applicable
        to CLN5 but mentioned for family's awareness.
   c. Supportive care: Comprehensive palliative and supportive care is
      the current standard for CLN5 disease. This includes seizure
      management, nutritional support (including gastrostomy when
      indicated), PT/OT, vision services, pain management, and
      psychosocial support for the family.

6. RESOURCES AND REFERRALS:
   - Batten Disease Support and Research Association (BDSRA):
     www.bdsra.org | Phone: 1-800-448-4570
   - NCL Resource — A Gateway for Batten Disease:
     www.ucl.ac.uk/ncl-resource
   - Rare Disease Clinical Research Network
   - NORD (National Organization for Rare Disorders)
   - Family connected with a BDSRA family liaison who will facilitate
     peer support connection with other CLN5 families
   - Social work referral for palliative care planning and respite
     services

                    ELI'S MEDICAL TIMELINE
                 Written by Denise Reeves, his mom
              (started 2019, updated continuously)
              Last updated: February 2026

I am writing this because no doctor has ever had the full picture.
Every time we move, records get lost. Every new doctor starts over.
I am tired of starting over. This is everything.

AGE 0-1 (2012-2013):
  - March 15, 2012: Eli born, Portsmouth Naval Medical Center, VA.
    Healthy baby. 7 lbs 4 oz. Everything normal.
  - Hit all his milestones on time. Smiled, rolled over, sat up. I have
    videos of everything. He was a happy, normal baby.
  - First words around 12 months — "mama," "dada," "ball."
  - Pediatrician: Dr. James Whitfield, Hampton, VA. Said he was perfect.

AGE 1-2 (2013-2014):
  - Walking was late. He didn't walk until 26 months. Dr. Whitfield said
    "boys are slower, don't worry." But I was worried.
  - His words stopped growing. He had about 20 words by age 2 and that
    was it. No new words were coming in.
  - Started doing this thing with his hands — wringing them together,
    over and over. I thought it was just a habit.
  - I told Dr. Whitfield I was worried. He said wait and see.

AGE 2-3 (2014-2015):
  - SOMETHING WAS WRONG. I knew it. He was not progressing. Other kids
    his age were running and talking in sentences. Eli was still on
    single words and walking like he might fall over any second.
  - The staring spells started. He would just STOP and stare into space
    for 5-10 seconds. His eyes would flutter. Then he'd come back like
    nothing happened. I told Dr. Whitfield. He said "kids daydream."
    I KNEW this was not daydreaming.
  - Dr. Whitfield finally referred us to developmental pediatrics.
    6-MONTH WAIT.
  - MRI done at age 3 (05/2015) — read as "normal for age."

AGE 3-4 (2015-2016):
  - Age 3.5: Saw Dr. Whitfield at the developmental pediatrics clinic
    in Norfolk. [Note from Denise: I can't remember the exact doctor's
    name at the clinic, it was a woman.] She said AUTISM. Said his hand
    wringing was "stimming," his limited speech was "expressive language
    delay consistent with ASD," his not looking at people was "social
    deficits."
  - I asked about the staring spells. SHE SAID IT WAS PART OF AUTISM.
    I asked if they should do a brain wave test. She said it wasn't
    needed.
  - I KNEW something was wrong beyond autism. He was LOSING things, not
    just delayed. But she was the expert, so I tried to believe her.
  - Started ABA therapy. Eli hated it. He cried every session. The
    therapists said that was normal at first.
  - Dr. Whitfield says autism. I asked about the staring spells. He said
    it's part of autism. I KNEW something was wrong.

AGE 4-5 (2016-2017):
  - ABA therapy for a year now. NO PROGRESS. None. The therapist said
    he was "resistant to treatment." I said he wasn't resistant — he
    couldn't DO it. There's a difference.
  - HE WAS GETTING WORSE. At age 4 he could button his shirt. By age 5
    he couldn't anymore. That's not autism. That's losing skills.
  - Staring spells getting more frequent. Happening every day now.
  - Walking more unsteady. Tripping a lot. Toe walking worse.

AGE 5-7 (2017-2019):
  - Marcus got orders to Fort Benning, GA. We moved summer 2017.
  - New pediatrician: Dr. Amy Huang, Columbus, GA. She was nice. She
    looked at the records we brought (I brought everything I had).
  - She saw "ASD" in the chart and that was that. I told her about the
    regression, the staring spells, the losing skills. She said
    "regression can happen in autism, especially with anxiety."
  - He was NOT anxious. He was SICK.
  - Age 6: Dr. Huang referred to neurology (Dr. Kevin Chen). ONE visit.
    He ordered an MRI. MRI said "mildly prominent ventricles, normal
    variant." I asked about comparing to the old MRI from Virginia. He
    said he didn't have the old one. I ASKED THEM TO GET IT. Nobody did.
  - Dr. Chen said: "Consistent with ASD. Continue current therapies."
    ONE visit. That was it.
  - By age 7, Eli needed help walking. His gait was terrible. He was
    falling almost every day. The school was calling me to pick him up
    because he fell in the hallway.

AGE 7-9 (2019-2021):
  - Age 8: Got AFOs (ankle braces). Orthopedist (Dr. Torres) said
    "neuromuscular." But when I asked what that meant, he said it was
    just a word for his muscles being weak from the autism. AUTISM
    DOESN'T DO THIS.
  - PT started. The physical therapist — God bless her — she wrote a
    note saying "this doesn't look like typical ASD motor involvement."
    She recommended neurology re-evaluation. I KEPT THAT NOTE. It's in
    this binder.
  - Age 8-9: Staring spells 2-3 per day. Night jerking started. I
    started using a baby monitor so I could watch him sleep. I recorded
    videos of the jerking on my phone. DOCTORS NEED TO SEE THESE.
  - Speech down to mostly single words. Could still say some 2-word
    things ("want eat," "no more").
  - Age 9: FINALLY saw a neurologist who actually listened. Dr. Adwoa
    Osei. She took one look at him and said "this needs a full workup."
    She ordered an EEG and an MRI.
  - EEG: ABNORMAL. Seizure activity. She diagnosed epilepsy. Started
    Keppra. I TOLD THEM ABOUT THE STARING SPELLS SINCE HE WAS 2.
    SEVEN YEARS. Seven years of seizures that nobody would test for
    because they said it was autism.
  - MRI age 9: Showed his CEREBELLUM WAS SHRINKING. It wasn't on the
    old MRI. Or actually maybe it was and nobody caught it. Dr. Osei
    said she needed to compare to the old MRIs but she couldn't get
    them. SOUND FAMILIAR??
  - Labs: Lactate was high. 2.8. Normal is under 2. Dr. Osei said this
    was important.
  - Dr. Osei FINALLY says this is not autism. FINALLY. She said it's
    something genetic. Something degenerative. She wanted to do genetic
    testing. She mentioned "Batten disease" as one possibility.
  - But then we have to move. AGAIN.
  - Marcus got orders to Fort Liberty, NC. We had to go. Dr. Osei said
    she would send all her records and that the new neurologist should
    continue the workup. She hugged me when we left. She said she was
    sorry nobody caught it sooner.

AGE 10-12 (2022-2024):
  - Moved to Fort Liberty, June 2022. New everything. New doctors, new
    schools, new everything.
  - DR. OSEI'S RECORDS NEVER MADE IT. The EEG, the MRI, the lactate
    results, her notes about genetic testing — NONE of it was in the
    file that transferred. I couldn't believe it. I called Columbus.
    They said they sent everything. THEY DIDN'T.
  - New pediatrician: Dr. Lisa Washington. She tried. She really did.
    But she had almost nothing to work with. She only had 2 years of
    Dr. Huang's notes and the orthopedic stuff. She didn't have any of
    the neurology records.
  - Eli was in a wheelchair full-time now. 5-8 seizures a day.
    Barely talking.
  - Dr. Washington put in an urgent neurology referral. FOUR MONTH
    WAIT. I called TRICARE over and over. Four months.
  - Then Eli had a big seizure, 3 minutes, we had to call 911. That's
    when they finally expedited the neurology appointment.
  - September 2023: Dr. Samuel Park, neurology, Womack Army Medical
    Center. He is the first doctor who sat down with my binder and
    actually READ it. All of it. He spent 2 hours. He said things I
    had been waiting TEN YEARS to hear. He said this was never autism.
    He said it was a brain disease. He said the word I had been
    researching for years: Batten disease.
  - He ordered everything. MRI, EEG, eye tests, blood work, genetics
    referral. He actually COMPARED the MRIs from over the years. He
    could see the brain shrinking.
  - The EEG showed what the one in Ohio showed, plus more. The eye
    doctor said Eli's retinas were dying. It all pointed to one thing.
  - Genetics referral: 6-month wait. Then INSURANCE DENIED the genetic
    test. DENIED IT. They said it wasn't necessary. My son is dying and
    the test to find out WHY is not necessary??
  - Dr. Park and Dr. Washington both wrote appeal letters. Took 4
    months for the appeal. Finally approved October 2024.

AGE 12-14 (2024-2026):
  - December 2024: Genetics at Duke (Dr. Rachel Kim). She is thorough.
    She ordered the genetic test, plus skin biopsy, plus enzyme tests.
  - January 2025: Skin biopsy showed the storage material. She said
    that CONFIRMED it was Batten disease even before the genetic test
    came back.
  - March 2025: THE GENETIC TEST CAME BACK. CLN5. Batten disease.
    Two mutations — one from me, one from Marcus. We are both carriers.
    We had no idea.
  - It took 12 years. TWELVE YEARS. He was 2 when I first said
    something was wrong. He was 13 when we got the answer.
  - Now I need to know: what can be done? What could have been done?
    Are there treatments? Are there trials? And if we have another
    baby, what are the chances?
  - I am bringing Eli to a new developmental pediatrics clinic. Someone
    who can look at THE WHOLE PICTURE and help us figure out what
    happens next.

  — Denise Reeves
    Mother of Eli, the bravest person I know

MOTHER'S LETTER TO NEW DEVELOPMENTAL PEDIATRICS CLINIC
(Tab 8 of binder — typed, printed, 1 page, dated March 2026)
----------------------------------------------------------------------------

                                          March 1, 2026

To Whom It May Concern:

My name is Denise Reeves. My son Eli is 14 years old. He has been sick
since he was about 2.

We have seen 8 doctors across 3 states in 12 years. We moved 3 times
because my husband is in the Army. Every time we moved, records got lost.
Every time we started over with a new doctor, they only saw a piece of the
picture. Nobody ever saw all of it.

Eli was told he had autism when he was 3 and a half years old. I never
thought that was the whole story. He was getting WORSE, not just different.
He lost skills. He stopped talking. He stopped walking. He started having
seizures. For years, I told doctors that something else was going on. For
years, they told me it was just the autism, that regression can happen, that
I should be patient.

I was not wrong. My son does not have autism. He has Batten disease — CLN5.
A genetic brain disease that has been destroying his brain since he was a
toddler. We got this diagnosis in March 2025, when he was 13 years old.
TWELVE YEARS after I first told his pediatrician that something was wrong.

Nobody connected the dots because nobody ever had all the records at the
same time. The MRIs were done at different hospitals in different states
and nobody compared them until he was 12. The EEG that showed seizures was
done at age 9 but the results didn't transfer when we moved. The blood test
that showed something abnormal was done at age 9 but no doctor in North
Carolina ever saw those results. The genetic testing that should have been
done at age 9 didn't happen because we had to move, and then when it was
finally ordered at age 12, the insurance company denied it, and it took
another year to get approved.

I carry this binder everywhere. It has everything. The MRIs, the blood work,
the therapy reports, the school records, everything. I have spent 12 years
collecting these papers because the medical system could not keep track of
my son's records across state lines.

This binder is 487 pages. It is more complete than any hospital chart. I am
not saying that to brag. I am saying that because it is a failure of the
system that a mother with a high school diploma has a more complete medical
record than the hospitals that treated her son.

PLEASE look at all of it. PLEASE.

We finally have genetic testing results that show Eli has CLN5 Batten
disease. But it took 12 years. I want someone to look at the whole picture
and tell me: what do we do now? What treatments are available? Are there
clinical trials he can be in? What is going to happen to him? How do we
give him the best quality of life we can?

And I need someone to answer the question I have been carrying for years:
could this have been found sooner? Because I think it could have. I have
been saying something was wrong since he was 2 years old. The staring
spells that turned out to be seizures — I reported those at age 2. The
skill loss that turned out to be neurodegeneration — I reported that at
age 4. The brain shrinkage that turned out to be progressive — it was
probably already starting on the MRI at age 6, but nobody compared it to
the earlier one. The abnormal blood work at age 9 — nobody in North
Carolina even knew about it.

I am not angry at any one doctor. I am angry at a system that let my son
fall through the cracks for 12 years. I am angry that every time we moved,
we started over. I am angry that insurance denied the test that could have
given us answers a year earlier. I am angry that the word "autism" in his
chart made every doctor stop looking for anything else.

But mostly, I am tired. And I need help.

Eli is 14 now. He is in a wheelchair. He cannot talk. He can barely see.
He has seizures every day. He needs me for everything. And I will be here
for everything. But I need a team. I need someone who will look at all of
this and help us make a plan.

Thank you for your time. Please read the binder.

Sincerely,

Denise Reeves
Mother of Eli Marcus Reeves
Phone: (910) 555-0461
Email: denise.reeves.eli@gmail.com

NEURORADIOLOGY COMPARISON READING
Requested by: Samuel Park, MD — Pediatric Neurology
Reading Physician: Dr. Margaret Choi, MD — Neuroradiology Fellowship Director
Date of Comparison Reading: January 14, 2026
Patient Age at Time of Review: 13 years 10 months

CLINICAL INDICATION:
Comparison review of all available brain MRI studies for patient with confirmed
CLN5 Neuronal Ceroid Lipofuscinosis. Retrospective analysis requested to
identify earliest detectable imaging abnormality and to evaluate the pattern
of disease progression for clinical and educational purposes.

STUDIES REVIEWED:

    Study 1: September 18, 2015 — Age 3 years 6 months
             Richmond VA Medical Center, Richmond, Virginia
             1.5 Tesla GE Signa HDxt
             Sequences: Axial T1, Axial T2, Axial FLAIR, Sagittal T1
             Sedation: Chloral hydrate

    Study 2: May 22, 2018 — Age 6 years 2 months
             Nationwide Children's Hospital, Columbus, Ohio
             1.5 Tesla Siemens Magnetom Aera
             Sequences: Axial T1, Axial T2, Axial FLAIR, Sagittal T1,
                        Coronal T2, DWI/ADC
             Sedation: Propofol

    Study 3: April 9, 2021 — Age 9 years 1 month
             Nationwide Children's Hospital, Columbus, Ohio
             3.0 Tesla Siemens Magnetom Prisma
             Sequences: 3D T1 MPRAGE, Axial T2, Axial FLAIR, Sagittal T1,
                        Coronal T2, DWI/ADC, SWI, MR Spectroscopy
             Sedation: General anesthesia (increasing difficulty with
                        cooperation)

    Study 4: March 20, 2024 — Age 12 years 0 months
             Womack Army Medical Center, Fort Liberty (Bragg), North Carolina
             3.0 Tesla Siemens Magnetom Vida
             Sequences: 3D T1 MPRAGE, Axial T2, Axial FLAIR, Sagittal T1,
                        Coronal T2, DWI/ADC, SWI, MR Spectroscopy
             General anesthesia required

NOTE ON STUDY AVAILABILITY:
Study 1 was obtained from the VA Medical Center on CD-ROM after specific
request by Dr. Park's office. This study was NOT available to any reading
radiologist at the time of Studies 2, 3, or 4. The CD-ROM was located in the
medical records archive only after repeated requests over a 6-week period.
Image quality is adequate but limited by 1.5T field strength and the
restricted protocol used.

------------------------------------------------------------------------
STUDY 1 — SEPTEMBER 18, 2015 (AGE 3 YEARS 6 MONTHS)
Richmond VA Medical Center, 1.5T
------------------------------------------------------------------------

ORIGINAL READ (Dr. James Fulton, MD — Staff Radiologist, VAMC Richmond):
"Brain MRI without contrast performed under sedation. The brain parenchyma
demonstrates normal signal intensity on all sequences. Ventricles and sulci
are within normal limits for age. Myelination pattern is age-appropriate.
No mass, midline shift, or acute abnormality. The posterior fossa structures
appear normal. IMPRESSION: Normal brain MRI for age."

RETROSPECTIVE RE-READ (Dr. Margaret Choi, MD — January 2026):
"In retrospect, with the knowledge of confirmed NCL diagnosis and with
the benefit of comparison to subsequent studies, there is a question of
very subtle cerebellar vermis volume loss. The cerebellar folia appear
minimally prominent compared to normative references for a 3.5-year-old.
However, this finding is within the range of normal variation at this age,
and I cannot state with confidence that it represents early pathology. The
1.5T field strength and limited protocol (no volumetric sequences) further
constrain retrospective analysis. The cerebral hemispheres appear truly
normal at this time point. I would NOT have called this abnormal even on
prospective review with a high index of suspicion.

REVISED IMPRESSION: Equivocal. Possible very subtle cerebellar vermis volume
loss, but within the range of normal variation. No definitive abnormality
can be identified at this age on this study."

------------------------------------------------------------------------
STUDY 2 — MAY 22, 2018 (AGE 6 YEARS 2 MONTHS)
Nationwide Children's Hospital, 1.5T
------------------------------------------------------------------------

ORIGINAL READ (Dr. Patricia Kwon, MD — Pediatric Neuroradiology):
"Brain MRI without and with contrast. The ventricles are mildly prominent,
which may represent a normal variant. The cerebral parenchyma demonstrates
normal signal. Myelination is complete and age-appropriate. No restricted
diffusion. No abnormal enhancement. The posterior fossa structures are
unremarkable. No mass or midline shift. IMPRESSION: Mildly prominent
ventricles, likely a normal variant. No acute intracranial abnormality.
Clinical correlation recommended."

RETROSPECTIVE RE-READ (Dr. Margaret Choi, MD — January 2026):
"Compared to the age-3 study from Richmond, there is now DEFINITE cerebellar
volume loss that was not appreciated on the original interpretation. The
cerebellar vermis and hemispheres demonstrate diffuse volume loss with
prominence of the cerebellar fissures that is clearly beyond normal
variation for a 6-year-old. This is unequivocally worse compared to the
age-3 study.

The 'mildly prominent ventricles' noted in the original read likely
represent early ex-vacuo ventricular dilatation secondary to beginning
brain parenchymal volume loss, rather than a benign normal variant. There
is subtle widening of the cerebral sulci in the parieto-occipital regions
that was not commented upon in the original report.

CRITICAL NOTE: Had the prior study from age 3 been available for comparison
at the time of this reading, the progressive nature of the cerebellar
volume loss would very likely have been identified. The original read was
performed without any comparison study, and the changes, while definite in
retrospect, could be overlooked as a variant on a single time-point reading
at 1.5T without specific clinical concern for neurodegeneration.

It should also be noted that the clinical history provided to radiology at
the time of this study was 'Autism spectrum disorder, rule out structural
abnormality.' A clinical history of developmental REGRESSION or suspected
seizures would have heightened the radiologist's scrutiny for subtle
volume loss.

REVISED IMPRESSION: Definite cerebellar atrophy, progressive compared to
age-3 study. Early cerebral volume loss. Findings consistent with a
neurodegenerative process. This is the earliest time point at which a
definitive imaging abnormality can be identified."

------------------------------------------------------------------------
STUDY 3 — APRIL 9, 2021 (AGE 9 YEARS 1 MONTH)
Nationwide Children's Hospital, 3.0T
------------------------------------------------------------------------

ORIGINAL READ (Dr. Ravi Mehta, MD — Pediatric Neuroradiology):
"Brain MRI without and with contrast, with MR spectroscopy. Comparison
is made to the prior study from May 2018.

FINDINGS: There is significant interval progression of cerebellar atrophy
compared to the prior study from approximately 3 years ago. The cerebellar
vermis and hemispheres are markedly diminished in volume with prominent
cerebellar fissures and enlargement of the fourth ventricle. New cerebral
cortical volume loss is now apparent, most pronounced in the parietal and
occipital lobes, with diffuse sulcal prominence and mild ex-vacuo
ventricular enlargement. The lateral ventricles are more prominent than
on the prior study. There is subtle T2 hyperintensity in the periventricular
white matter.

The corpus callosum appears mildly thinned compared to age-matched
normative data. The thalami demonstrate borderline T2 hypointensity.
No restricted diffusion. No abnormal enhancement.

MR Spectroscopy (single voxel, parietal white matter): Mildly reduced
NAA/Cr ratio (1.8, normal >2.0), suggesting neuronal loss. Mildly
elevated myo-inositol, suggesting gliosis.

IMPRESSION:
1. Significant progressive cerebellar atrophy with new cerebral cortical
   volume loss, worse since prior study from 2018.
2. Findings are highly concerning for a neurodegenerative process.
3. Recommend urgent clinical correlation and consideration of metabolic
   and genetic evaluation.
4. MR spectroscopy findings support neuronal loss with gliosis."

RETROSPECTIVE COMMENT (Dr. Margaret Choi, MD — January 2026):
"The original read by Dr. Mehta was accurate and appropriately alarming.
This study demonstrated unequivocal progression and correctly identified
the neurodegenerative pattern. Dr. Mehta's recommendation for metabolic
and genetic evaluation was appropriate. This was the point at which the
imaging findings alone demanded a neurodegenerative workup. The
progression from Study 2 to Study 3 over a 3-year interval is dramatic
and leaves no room for ambiguity."

------------------------------------------------------------------------
STUDY 4 — MARCH 20, 2024 (AGE 12 YEARS 0 MONTHS)
Womack Army Medical Center, 3.0T
------------------------------------------------------------------------

ORIGINAL READ (Dr. Colin Baker, MD — Neuroradiology):
"Brain MRI without and with contrast, with MR spectroscopy. Comparison
is made to outside study from April 2021 (Nationwide Children's Hospital).

FINDINGS: There is severe generalized brain atrophy that has progressed
markedly since the prior study. The cerebellum is severely atrophic with
near-complete loss of cerebellar hemispheric and vermian volume. The
fourth ventricle is widely patent. Moderate-to-severe cerebral atrophy is
present with diffuse cortical thinning, sulcal prominence, and ex-vacuo
ventricular dilatation. The parietal and occipital lobes are
disproportionately affected.

The thalami demonstrate definite bilateral T2 hypointensity, which is a
recognized feature of neuronal ceroid lipofuscinosis and other storage
disorders. The corpus callosum is markedly thinned, particularly in the
body and splenium. Diffuse T2/FLAIR hyperintensity is present in the
periventricular and deep white matter bilaterally, consistent with
demyelination or gliosis.

MR Spectroscopy (single voxel, parietal white matter): Markedly reduced
NAA/Cr ratio (1.2), significantly worsened from 2021, indicating
progressive neuronal loss. Elevated myo-inositol peak. Mildly elevated
choline, suggesting active demyelination.

No restricted diffusion to suggest acute injury. No abnormal enhancement.

IMPRESSION:
1. Severe progressive cerebellar and cerebral atrophy with significant
   interval worsening since 2021.
2. Bilateral thalamic T2 hypointensity — characteristic of neuronal
   ceroid lipofuscinosis.
3. Marked corpus callosum thinning.
4. Diffuse white matter signal abnormality consistent with secondary
   demyelination/gliosis.
5. MR Spectroscopy confirms progressive neuronal loss.
6. Findings are consistent with the known diagnosis of CLN5 Neuronal
   Ceroid Lipofuscinosis."

------------------------------------------------------------------------
OVERALL COMPARISON CONCLUSION (Dr. Margaret Choi, MD)
------------------------------------------------------------------------

"Serial imaging spanning 8.5 years (age 3.5 to age 12) demonstrates
relentless progressive neurodegeneration with a cerebello-predominant
pattern characteristic of Neuronal Ceroid Lipofuscinosis. The earliest
DEFINITIVE imaging abnormality was present at age 6 years 2 months
(Study 2) but was not recognized on the original interpretation due to:
(1) lack of prior comparison imaging, (2) clinical history provided to
radiology that emphasized autism rather than regression, and (3) the
subtlety of the findings at 1.5T without volumetric analysis.

The age-3.5 study (Study 1) is equivocal in retrospect. Even with the
benefit of hindsight and confirmed diagnosis, I cannot definitively
identify pathology at that time point, though very subtle early cerebellar
changes may have been present.

By Study 3 (age 9), the findings were unambiguous and the original
reader appropriately identified the neurodegenerative pattern. By Study 4
(age 12), the imaging demonstrates severe end-stage neurodegeneration
with the classic NCL features including thalamic T2 hypointensity.

PATTERN SUMMARY:
  - Cerebellar atrophy:    Earliest (definitive by age 6)
  - Cerebral atrophy:      Detected by age 9, severe by age 12
  - Thalamic involvement:  Borderline age 9, definitive age 12
  - Corpus callosum:       Thinned by age 12
  - White matter disease:  Periventricular changes by age 9, diffuse by 12
  - Spectroscopy decline:  NAA/Cr from ~normal (assumed) → 1.8 → 1.2

This pattern is consistent with published imaging progression data for
CLN5 disease (Mole and Williams, 2001; Jadav et al., 2014; Lonnqvist
et al., 2022).

Signed electronically:
Margaret Choi, MD
Director of Pediatric Neuroradiology Fellowship
Emergent Diagnostic Institution Consulting Faculty
January 14, 2026"

** CRITICAL FLAG — AGE 9 LACTATE / L:P RATIO:
Lactate 2.8 mmol/L (reference <2.2) and lactate-to-pyruvate ratio of 33
(reference <25) were mildly elevated at age 9. This was noted in Dr. Osei's
chart but the result was not followed up with repeat testing or
mitochondrial evaluation due to the family's relocation before the
follow-up appointment. The elevated L:P ratio can be seen in NCL due to
secondary mitochondrial dysfunction in affected neurons and is a
recognized but non-specific finding. This abnormal result was NOT present
in the records transferred to North Carolina.

  Variant: c.726G>A (p.Trp242*)
  Classification: Pathogenic (ACMG criteria: PVS1, PM2, PP3, PP5)
  Zygosity: Homozygous
  Inheritance: Autosomal recessive

  Parental testing (Aug 2024):
    Mother (Denise Reeves): HETEROZYGOUS carrier — CLN5 c.726G>A
    Father (Marcus Reeves): HETEROZYGOUS carrier — CLN5 c.726G>A

  Both parents are confirmed carriers. Each subsequent pregnancy carries
  a 25% risk of affected offspring. Genetic counseling completed September
  2024 with Dr. Lena Okafor.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
TESTS THAT SHOULD HAVE BEEN ORDERED EARLIER
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

The following is a retrospective analysis of testing gaps:

1. ELECTROENCEPHALOGRAM (EEG):
   - SHOULD have been ordered: Age 2-3 (when staring spells first reported)
   - ACTUALLY first ordered: Age 9 (by Dr. Osei, Neurology)
   - DELAY: Approximately 6-7 years
   - IMPACT: EEG at age 2-3 may have shown epileptiform activity or
     background slowing inconsistent with ASD alone. This would have
     redirected the diagnostic evaluation years earlier.

2. COMPREHENSIVE METABOLIC/GENETIC PANEL:
   - SHOULD have been ordered: Age 5-6 (when clear developmental regression
     was documented)
   - ACTUALLY first ordered: Age 9 (partial panel), Age 12 (enzyme studies,
     WES)
   - DELAY: Approximately 4-6 years for metabolic studies; 6-7 years for
     genetic testing
   - IMPACT: Whole exome sequencing was available clinically from
     approximately 2015 onward. If ordered at age 6 when regression was
     documented, diagnosis could have been made by age 6-7.

3. LYSOSOMAL ENZYME PANEL:
   - SHOULD have been ordered: Age 6-9 (once neurodegenerative process
     suspected on imaging)
   - ACTUALLY first ordered: Age 12
   - DELAY: 3-6 years
   - NOTE: Lysosomal enzyme panel alone would NOT have identified CLN5
     (no specific enzyme assay for CLN5), but it would have narrowed the
     differential and prompted earlier genetic testing.

4. REPEAT LACTATE / MITOCHONDRIAL WORKUP:
   - Elevated lactate (2.8) and L:P ratio (33) at age 9 were not repeated
     or followed up.
   - Should have prompted: Repeat fasting lactate, consideration of
     mitochondrial DNA testing, or referral to metabolic genetics.
   - Result was lost in the transfer to North Carolina.

NOTE: By age 12, all growth parameters are at or below the 3rd percentile.
Height gain has nearly ceased by age 13-14 — likely reflecting both the
neurodegenerative process and the effect of anticonvulsant medications on
growth. Weight DECLINE from age 13 to 14 (27.80 → 26.50 kg) is concerning
and reflects worsening dysphagia, increased caloric expenditure from
seizure activity, and progressive neurological decline. G-tube placement
was recommended at age 13.5 and placed at age 14 years 1 month.

Head circumference has plateaued at approximately 50.4 cm since age 10,
representing progressive microcephaly relative to normative growth. At
age 14, HC is at the 5th percentile — a decline from 50th at birth. This
trajectory is characteristic of NCL-related brain atrophy and distinguishes
the condition from primary (congenital) microcephaly.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
ANTICONVULSANT MEDICATIONS
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

1. LEVETIRACETAM (Keppra) — First-line AED
   Start Date:     May 2021 (Age 9 years 2 months)
   Prescriber:     Dr. Angela Osei, MD — Pediatric Neurology, NCH Columbus
   Indication:     New-onset generalized tonic-clonic seizures; EEG with
                   multifocal epileptiform discharges and diffuse background
                   slowing

   Dose History:
     May 2021:     250 mg PO BID (initial dose, weight 23 kg = 21.7 mg/kg/day)
     Jun 2021:     Titrated to 500 mg PO BID (43.5 mg/kg/day) — breakthrough
                   seizure at 2 weeks on initial dose
     Mar 2022:     Increased to 750 mg PO BID (Age 10, weight ~24.5 kg,
                   61.2 mg/kg/day) — seizure frequency increasing to
                   2-3 per week. Prescriber: Dr. Raymond Jeffers, MD,
                   Neurology, WAMC. NOTE: This increase occurred after
                   relocation to NC; Dr. Jeffers was working up patient
                   largely de novo without prior neurology records.
     Mar 2024:     Increased to 1000 mg PO BID (Age 12, weight ~27.5 kg,
                   72.7 mg/kg/day) — refractory seizures despite dual therapy

   Adverse Effects: Increased irritability and behavioral changes noted by
   mother at 750 mg dose (age 10). Behavioral side effects difficult to
   distinguish from disease progression. No dose-limiting toxicity.

   Current Status: ACTIVE — 1000 mg PO BID

2. CLOBAZAM (Onfi) — Adjunctive AED
   Start Date:     November 2022 (Age 10 years 8 months)
   Prescriber:     Dr. Raymond Jeffers, MD — Neurology, WAMC
   Indication:     Adjunctive therapy for refractory seizures (generalized
                   tonic-clonic and myoclonic) despite levetiracetam
                   monotherapy

   Dose History:
     Nov 2022:     5 mg PO BID (initial dose)
     Jan 2023:     Maintained at 5 mg PO BID — partial improvement in
                   seizure frequency (estimated 40% reduction per parent
                   seizure diary)
     Mar 2024:     Increased to 10 mg PO BID (Age 12) — seizure frequency
                   increasing again, myoclonic jerks becoming more
                   prominent and interfering with feeding

   Adverse Effects: Mild sedation at initiation, improved after 2 weeks.
   No paradoxical reactions. Drooling may be partially medication-related
   vs. disease-related oromotor dysfunction.

   Current Status: ACTIVE — 10 mg PO BID

3. VALPROIC ACID (Depakote / Divalproex Sodium ER)
   Start Date:     September 2024 (Age 12 years 6 months)
   Prescriber:     Dr. Samuel Park, MD — Neurology, EDI Consulting
   Indication:     Third-line AED added specifically targeting myoclonic
                   seizures, which had become the most disabling seizure
                   type (interfering with swallowing, communication, and
                   comfort)

   Dose History:
     Sep 2024:     250 mg PO BID (initial dose)
     Nov 2024:     Titrated to 375 mg PO BID — moderate improvement in
                   myoclonus frequency
     Feb 2025:     Increased to 500 mg PO BID (current, weight ~27 kg,
                   37 mg/kg/day) — valproic acid level 72 mcg/mL
                   (therapeutic range 50-100)

   Adverse Effects: Transient GI upset at initiation. Weight stabilization
   noted (valproic acid may partially offset disease-related weight loss).
   AST/ALT mildly elevated at age 12 — monitored q3 months. Platelets
   stable. Ammonia level 42 (within normal limits).

   Monitoring: Valproic acid trough level, CBC with differential, CMP
   including LFTs, ammonia — every 3 months.

   Current Status: ACTIVE — 500 mg PO BID

4. RISPERIDONE (Risperdal) — Atypical Antipsychotic
   Start Date:     October 2017 (Age 5 years 7 months)
   Stop Date:      August 2018 (Age 6 years 5 months)
   Prescriber:     Dr. Linda Huang, MD — Developmental Pediatrics, Columbus
   Indication:     "Behavioral dysregulation in the setting of ASD" —
                   specifically targeting episodes of screaming, agitation,
                   and apparent aggression

   Dose History:
     Oct 2017:     0.25 mg PO QHS (starting dose)
     Dec 2017:     0.5 mg PO QHS
     Feb 2018:     0.5 mg PO BID

   RETROSPECTIVE NOTE: The "behavioral episodes" that prompted risperidone
   use are now understood to have been post-ictal agitation and irritability
   following subclinical seizures. The behaviors were not volitional or
   consistent with ASD-related behavioral dysregulation. The medication
   was masking a neurological symptom rather than treating a psychiatric
   condition.

   Discontinuation Reason: Excessive sedation, weight gain (1.5 kg in
   4 months), and mother reported child seemed "more out of it" on
   medication. Tapered and discontinued August 2018.

================================================================================
         SECTION 5E — INTER-PROVIDER COMMUNICATION FAILURE LOG
================================================================================

This section documents specific, verified instances where medical records
were lost, not transferred, not requested, or not reviewed during the
course of Eli Reeves' care. Information compiled through retrospective
records audit conducted by EDI Records Integration Unit, November 2025-
January 2026.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
FAILURE #1: Virginia → Ohio Transfer (Age 4, August 2016)
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Date of Family Relocation: August 2016 (Fort Lee, VA → Columbus, OH)
New PCP: Columbus Children's Clinic — Dr. Margaret Sullivan, MD

RECORDS REQUESTED BY NEW PROVIDER:
  - "Most recent records from prior pediatrician"

RECORDS ACTUALLY RECEIVED:
  - Dr. Karen Whitfield, MD (Developmental Pediatrics, Richmond) —
    ASD diagnostic summary letter dated February 2016 (2 pages)
  - Dr. Sarah Tan, MD (Southeastern Pediatrics, Richmond) —
    Most recent well-child visit note (June 2016, 1 page)
  - Immunization records

RECORDS NOT TRANSFERRED / NOT REQUESTED:
  - Early developmental screening scores (ASQ-3 results from ages 9, 12,
    18, 24 months showing progressive score decline)
  - PT evaluation and treatment notes (4 months of physical therapy
    records documenting motor concerns, April-July 2016)
  - Speech therapy evaluation and progress notes
  - Well-child visit notes from ages 0-3 that documented parental
    concerns about staring spells and "zoning out"
  - MRI report from Richmond VAMC (September 2015, age 3.5) — this
    critical imaging study was in the VA system and was never requested
    by civilian providers
  - MRI images (on CD at VAMC Richmond — never transferred)
  - Audiology evaluation results (age 3, normal hearing — relevant to
    differential diagnosis of communication delays)

IMPACT: The receiving providers in Ohio had NO knowledge of:
  (a) The staring spells documented at age 2-3
  (b) The MRI that had been performed
  (c) The specific motor concerns that prompted PT referral
  (d) The trajectory of developmental screening scores showing decline
This created a "clean slate" effect where the Ohio providers inherited
only the ASD diagnosis without the concerning details that had accumulated
in Virginia.

ROOT CAUSE: Records release was initiated by parent (Denise Reeves) who
signed a general authorization at the pediatrician's office. The
pediatrician's office sent their own notes and the specialist letter
on file but did not send therapy records, VA system records, or
screening scores, as these were stored in separate systems or were
not considered part of the "medical record" by the releasing staff.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
FAILURE #2: Neurology Records Request (Age 6-7, 2018-2019)
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Provider: Dr. David Chen, MD — Pediatric Neurology, Nationwide Children's
Hospital, Columbus, OH

Date of Initial Consultation: April 2018

RECORDS REQUESTED:
  - "Records from prior pediatrician regarding developmental history
    and any previous evaluations"

RECORDS RECEIVED:
  - Dr. Linda Huang, MD (Developmental Pediatrics, Columbus) — evaluation
    from November 2017 (full report)
  - Dr. Margaret Sullivan, MD (Columbus Children's Clinic) — office notes
    from 2016-2018

RECORDS NOT RECEIVED:
  - Any records from Virginia providers
  - Dr. Karen Whitfield's full developmental evaluation (only the summary
    letter was in Dr. Sullivan's chart — the full report remained in
    Virginia)
  - MRI from Richmond VAMC (age 3.5) — Dr. Chen ordered a NEW MRI
    (May 2018) without knowledge that a prior study existed. This is the
    single most consequential records failure in this case. Had the age-3
    MRI been available for comparison, the progressive cerebellar atrophy
    visible on the age-6 MRI would very likely have been identified.
  - Physical therapy and speech therapy records from Virginia
  - Audiology evaluation from Virginia

IMPACT: Dr. Chen interpreted the May 2018 MRI as an isolated study. The
"mildly prominent ventricles" were called a "likely normal variant."
With a comparison study from 2.5 years prior showing normal or near-normal
ventricles and cerebellum, the progressive change would have been apparent.
The MRI was read in isolation and the opportunity to identify
neurodegeneration at age 6 was missed.

ROOT CAUSE: Multi-system records fragmentation. Virginia records were in
civilian pediatric offices AND the VA medical system. Ohio providers
requested records from the Ohio pediatrician, who forwarded what they
had received from Virginia — which was already incomplete (see Failure #1).
No one requested records directly from Virginia or from the VA system.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
FAILURE #3: Ohio → North Carolina Transfer (Age 10, June 2022)
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Date of Family Relocation: June 2022 (Columbus, OH → Fayetteville, NC /
Fort Liberty)

New PCP: Sandhills Pediatrics — Dr. James Morton, MD
New Neurology: Womack Army Medical Center — Dr. Raymond Jeffers, MD

RECORDS REQUESTED BY NEW PROVIDERS:
  - Sandhills Pediatrics requested: "Complete medical records" from
    Columbus Children's Clinic
  - WAMC Neurology requested: "Neurology records" from Nationwide
    Children's Hospital

RECORDS ACTUALLY RECEIVED BY SANDHILLS PEDIATRICS:
  - Columbus Children's Clinic notes (2016-2022): Received
  - Dr. Huang developmental evaluation: Received
  - Immunization records: Received
  - Growth chart data: Received
  - Medication list: Received (but outdated — did not include Keppra
    which had been prescribed by neurology, not pediatrics)

RECORDS ACTUALLY RECEIVED BY WAMC NEUROLOGY:
  - Dr. David Chen's neurology notes (2018-2019): Received
  - MRI report from May 2018: Received
  - PARTIAL EEG report from NCH (January 2019): Received — but only
    the summary page, not the full technical report

CRITICAL RECORDS NOT TRANSFERRED TO EITHER PROVIDER:
  - Dr. Angela Osei's neurology notes (2021-2022) — INCLUDING her
    identification of the neurodegenerative pattern and her recommendation
    for genetic testing. REASON: Dr. Osei was a different neurologist at
    NCH from Dr. Chen. Records were released from "Neurology" but only
    Dr. Chen's older notes were in the general neurology folder. Dr. Osei's
    notes were filed under her subspecialty clinic (Neurogenetics) and
    were not captured by the standard records release process.
  - EEG results from June 2021 (Dr. Osei ordered) — showed multifocal
    epileptiform discharges and severe diffuse background slowing
  - MRI results from April 2021 (Age 9) — the study showing significant
    progressive cerebellar atrophy. This was filed under Radiology at NCH
    and was not included in the neurology records release.
  - Laboratory results from 2021 including the elevated lactate (2.8)
    and elevated L:P ratio (33)
  - Dr. Osei's genetics referral recommendation
  - Ophthalmology referral and results (retinal changes noted)
  - Physical therapy letter from Megan Calloway, PT, DPT describing
    cerebellar signs inconsistent with ASD

IMPACT: Dr. Jeffers at WAMC received records that effectively showed
the case as of 2019 — before the neurodegenerative process had been
identified. He had Dr. Chen's notes (which described ASD with seizures)
and the 2018 MRI (called "normal variant"). He did NOT have Dr. Osei's
critical notes that had already identified progressive neurodegeneration,
nor her MRI showing dramatic progression, nor her genetics recommendation.
Dr. Jeffers essentially started a new diagnostic workup from a point that
was already 3 years behind where Dr. Osei had left off.

This single records failure resulted in an estimated 2-year delay in
diagnosis. Dr. Jeffers spent from June 2022 to approximately March 2024
repeating evaluations and imaging that had already been completed in Ohio.

ROOT CAUSE: (1) Provider transition within the same institution (Chen →
Osei) created a records filing issue — Osei's subspecialty clinic notes
were not in the general neurology records. (2) Standard records release
processes do not typically capture all ancillary records (radiology, lab,
therapy notes) unless specifically requested. (3) The releasing institution
(NCH) processed a standard records release, not a comprehensive records
compilation. (4) No one at the receiving end recognized the records were
incomplete because they did not know what they were missing.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
FAILURE #4: Military / Civilian Records System Fragmentation
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Throughout Eli Reeves' care, records existed in multiple non-interoperable
systems:

MILITARY SYSTEMS:
  - TRICARE claims database: Contains claims data (dates of service, CPT
    codes, diagnoses billed) but NO clinical notes, test results, or
    imaging
  - MHS GENESIS (Military Health System EHR): Contains records from
    military treatment facilities (MTFs) only — in this case, Womack Army
    Medical Center records from 2022 onward. Does NOT contain civilian
    provider records even when paid by TRICARE.
  - Richmond VAMC system: MRI from age 3.5 was in the VA EHR (VistA/CPRS).
    This system is entirely separate from both TRICARE and MHS GENESIS.

CIVILIAN SYSTEMS:
  - Southeastern Pediatrics, Richmond: Paper charts, later scanned to
    local EMR (eClinicalWorks). No interoperability with any other system.
  - Columbus Children's Clinic: Athenahealth EMR. No connection to NCH
    system.
  - Nationwide Children's Hospital: Epic EHR. Records accessible within
    Ohio Epic network but not outside it.
  - Sandhills Pediatrics, NC: Practice Fusion EMR. No interoperability.
  - GeneDx (genetic testing lab): Results sent to ordering provider only.

IMPACT: At no point in Eli's 12-year diagnostic odyssey did any single
provider have access to his complete medical record. Each provider worked
from a partial picture. The military family's frequent relocations
compounded this issue — each move triggered a records transfer process
that was inherently lossy.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
FAILURE #5: Insurance Records Fragmentation
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Insurance History:
  - Birth to Age 4:  TRICARE Prime (East Region) — Humana Military
  - Age 4 to Age 10: TRICARE Select (West Region, then East Region) —
                     Health Net Federal Services, then Humana Military
  - Age 10 to present: TRICARE Prime (East Region) — Humana Military

Each TRICARE region change and plan change resulted in a new claims
history. Prior authorization records did not transfer between regions.
Referral histories were reset with each PCS (Permanent Change of Station)
move.

SPECIFIC IMPACT: When Dr. Jeffers at WAMC attempted to review what
testing had been done previously through the TRICARE claims system, only
claims from the current TRICARE enrollment period (2022-forward) were
readily accessible. Historical claims required a formal request to the
Defense Health Agency, which took 8 weeks to process and returned only
CPT code data without clinical context.


================================================================================
    SECTION 5F — CRITICAL TIMELINE OF MISSED DIAGNOSTIC OPPORTUNITIES
================================================================================

This forensic-style analysis was compiled as part of the EDI diagnostic
review. Each entry identifies a specific time point where the correct
diagnosis of CLN5 Neuronal Ceroid Lipofuscinosis could have been
reached or significantly advanced, the clinical event that represented
the opportunity, the action that was taken, the action that should have
been taken, and the estimated diagnostic delay attributable to each
missed opportunity.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
MISSED OPPORTUNITY #1
Age 2-3 (2014-2015) — Virginia
Clinical Setting: Southeastern Pediatrics, Richmond, VA
Provider: Dr. Sarah Tan, MD (Pediatrician)
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

CLINICAL EVENT: Mother reported "staring spells" and episodes of
unresponsiveness lasting 5-15 seconds at well-child visits at ages 24
months and 30 months. The child would stop what he was doing, stare
blankly, and be unresponsive to his name. Episodes occurred several
times daily.

ACTION TAKEN: Documented as "possible ASD-related inattention" and
contributed to the referral for developmental evaluation. No EEG ordered.
No specific seizure evaluation performed.

ACTION THAT SHOULD HAVE BEEN TAKEN: EEG. Any episode of unresponsiveness
in a toddler, regardless of ASD suspicion, warrants EEG to rule out
seizures. The AAP and the American Academy of Neurology recommend EEG
evaluation for children with paroxysmal staring events, particularly
when they involve loss of awareness.

PROBABLE OUTCOME IF CORRECT ACTION TAKEN: EEG at age 2-3 would likely
have shown either (a) subclinical epileptiform activity, which would
have prompted neurology referral and seizure-focused workup, or (b)
diffuse background slowing inconsistent with ASD alone. Either finding
would have raised concern for a condition beyond autism and potentially
triggered metabolic or genetic investigation 10 years before the eventual
diagnosis.

ESTIMATED DIAGNOSTIC DELAY ATTRIBUTABLE: Up to 10 years.

NOTE: It must be acknowledged that even if EEG had been obtained at this
age, the path to CLN5 diagnosis was not guaranteed. NCL was rare, genetic
testing was less accessible in 2014-2015, and the EEG findings may have
been nonspecific. However, the EEG would have at minimum reframed the
clinical picture away from pure ASD.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
MISSED OPPORTUNITY #2
Age 3.5 (September 2015) — Virginia
Clinical Setting: Dr. Karen Whitfield's Office, Developmental Pediatrics
Provider: Dr. Karen Whitfield, MD
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

CLINICAL EVENT: During ADOS-2 (Autism Diagnostic Observation Schedule)
testing, the psychometrist documented two separate episodes where Eli
became "briefly unresponsive" for approximately 10-12 seconds each,
followed by mild confusion. These were noted in the testing record as
"disengagement episodes consistent with ASD presentation."

ACTION TAKEN: The episodes were interpreted as ASD-related disengagement
or sensory overload. They were mentioned in the body of the ADOS-2
report but not flagged as clinically concerning. The ASD diagnosis was
confirmed. MRI was ordered (performed at VAMC Richmond) but EEG was not.

ACTION THAT SHOULD HAVE BEEN TAKEN: These episodes should have been
recognized as possible absence seizures. Brief unresponsiveness with
post-episode confusion is a classic presentation of absence or complex
partial seizures. EEG should have been ordered. The two episodes during
a single testing session suggest high seizure frequency.

PROBABLE OUTCOME: EEG would likely have confirmed seizure activity.
Combined with the MRI (even though the MRI was read as normal), the
presence of seizures in a child with developmental regression would
have shifted the differential from ASD to a seizure-related developmental
disorder. This would have prompted more aggressive investigation.

ESTIMATED DIAGNOSTIC DELAY ATTRIBUTABLE: 8-9 years.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
MISSED OPPORTUNITY #3
Age 5 (2017) — Ohio
Clinical Setting: Columbus Children's Clinic / ABA Therapy Center
Provider: Dr. Margaret Sullivan, MD (Pediatrician)
Source: ABA therapist progress notes (Behavioral Dimensions, Inc.)
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

CLINICAL EVENT: ABA therapist Sarah Kim, BCBA, submitted a detailed
progress report noting: (1) Clear loss of previously mastered skills over
a 6-month period, including self-feeding, two-word phrases, and toileting
skills; (2) Multiple observed episodes that "appear seizure-like —
child freezes, eyes deviate to one side, unresponsive for 10-20 seconds,
followed by fatigue and crying"; (3) Recommendation for medical evaluation
stating "these presentations are not typical of ASD behavioral patterns
in my clinical experience."

ACTION TAKEN: Dr. Sullivan documented the ABA therapist's concerns and
placed a neurology referral. However, the referral was placed as
"routine" (not urgent) and the wait time for pediatric neurology at
Nationwide Children's Hospital was approximately 4 months. No interim
EEG was ordered. No interim seizure precautions were discussed.

ACTION THAT SHOULD HAVE BEEN TAKEN: The ABA therapist's observations
constituted a clear description of seizure activity with developmental
regression. This warranted: (1) Urgent (not routine) neurology referral,
(2) Stat or urgent outpatient EEG, (3) Consideration of empiric seizure
precautions, (4) Documentation of regression as a medical concern
distinct from ASD.

PROBABLE OUTCOME: EEG at age 5 would have confirmed seizures. Combined
with documented regression, this would have triggered a neurodegenerative
workup. Genetic testing (exome sequencing, available clinically by 2017)
could have yielded a CLN5 diagnosis by age 5-6 — approximately 7 years
before actual diagnosis.

ESTIMATED DIAGNOSTIC DELAY ATTRIBUTABLE: 4-7 years (depending on how
quickly genetic testing would have been pursued after seizure confirmation).

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
MISSED OPPORTUNITY #4
Age 6 (May 2018) — Ohio
Clinical Setting: Nationwide Children's Hospital, Neurology
Provider: Dr. David Chen, MD — Pediatric Neurology
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

CLINICAL EVENT: Dr. Chen evaluated Eli for "ASD with possible seizures."
He ordered a brain MRI but did NOT order an EEG at this visit. His note
states: "Will obtain MRI brain to evaluate for structural etiology. If
MRI is unrevealing, will consider EEG at follow-up."

The MRI showed "mildly prominent ventricles, likely normal variant."
Dr. Chen's follow-up note states: "MRI essentially normal. Given ASD
diagnosis and no witnessed clinical seizures in office, will continue
to monitor. EEG can be obtained if clinical events increase."

ACTION TAKEN: MRI ordered (without prior comparison), read as essentially
normal. EEG deferred. Follow-up in 6 months.

ACTION THAT SHOULD HAVE BEEN TAKEN: (1) EEG should have been ordered
concurrently with MRI — it is standard practice to obtain both in the
evaluation of possible seizures. Deferring EEG pending MRI results is
not consistent with clinical guidelines. (2) Prior imaging from age 3.5
should have been obtained for comparison — but this would have required
knowledge that a prior MRI existed (see Communication Failure #2).

PROBABLE OUTCOME: (a) If EEG had been ordered: Very likely to show
epileptiform abnormalities, confirming seizure disorder and reframing
the clinical picture. (b) If prior MRI had been available: Side-by-side
comparison would have shown progressive cerebellar atrophy (as confirmed
by Dr. Choi's retrospective review), triggering immediate concern for
neurodegeneration. Either finding would have significantly advanced the
diagnostic process.

ESTIMATED DIAGNOSTIC DELAY ATTRIBUTABLE: 3-6 years.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
MISSED OPPORTUNITY #5
Age 8 (2020) — Ohio
Clinical Setting: Private Physical Therapy Practice, Columbus, OH
Provider: Megan Calloway, PT, DPT
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

CLINICAL EVENT: Physical therapist Megan Calloway wrote a detailed letter
to Dr. Sullivan (pediatrician) dated March 12, 2020, which stated in
relevant part:

"I have significant concerns about Eli's motor presentation that I do
not believe are consistent with autism spectrum disorder alone. He
demonstrates progressive truncal and appendicular ataxia with
dysmetria, intention tremor, and wide-based gait that are CEREBELLAR
in nature. His motor skills have declined measurably over the 18 months
I have treated him. Children with ASD may have motor coordination
difficulties, but they do not typically show progressive cerebellar
signs with this trajectory. I strongly recommend re-evaluation by
pediatric neurology with specific attention to the cerebellum."

ACTION TAKEN: Dr. Sullivan received the letter and documented it in the
chart. A note was made: "Will discuss neurology re-referral at next
well-child visit." The next well-child visit was not until September
2020 — a 6-month delay. Neurology referral was placed in September 2020
as routine, with a 3-month wait. The appointment with Dr. Osei occurred
in January 2021.

ACTION THAT SHOULD HAVE BEEN TAKEN: This letter described progressive
cerebellar signs in a child with developmental regression — a clinical
emergency from a diagnostic standpoint. The appropriate response would
have been: (1) Immediate (urgent) neurology re-referral, (2) Direct
provider-to-provider communication with neurology to expedite, (3)
Consideration of urgent MRI given specifically described cerebellar
concerns.

PROBABLE OUTCOME: If neurology re-referral had been expedited in
March 2020 rather than September 2020, the diagnosis process that
Dr. Osei initiated in January 2021 would have started approximately
9-10 months earlier. Given that the family moved in June 2022, this
earlier start might have allowed completion of genetic testing before
the move — potentially yielding a diagnosis by age 9-10 rather than
age 12.

ESTIMATED DIAGNOSTIC DELAY ATTRIBUTABLE: 1-2 years.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
MISSED OPPORTUNITY #6
Age 9 (2021) — Ohio
Clinical Setting: Nationwide Children's Hospital, Neurogenetics Clinic
Provider: Dr. Angela Osei, MD — Pediatric Neurology / Neurogenetics
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

CLINICAL EVENT: Dr. Osei correctly identified the neurodegenerative
process through clinical examination, EEG, and MRI (April 2021). She
recommended genetic testing (whole exome sequencing) and ophthalmology
evaluation. She placed a ROUTINE referral to the Genetics department
at NCH for WES. Wait time for genetics at NCH: approximately 4-5 months.

The family received PCS orders in March 2022 (orders to Fort Liberty, NC)
and moved in June 2022. The genetics appointment was scheduled for
July 2022. The family notified NCH of the move and the appointment was
cancelled.

ACTION TAKEN: Genetics referral placed as routine. Appointment scheduled
for 4-5 months out. Family moved before appointment.

ACTION THAT SHOULD HAVE BEEN TAKEN: (1) Genetics referral should have
been placed as URGENT given the clear identification of a progressive
neurodegenerative condition in a child. (2) Given the military family
status, Dr. Osei or her team could have flagged the case for expedited
processing. (3) Alternatively, WES could have been ordered directly by
neurology without waiting for a separate genetics consultation — many
institutions allow neurologists to order WES with appropriate
justification. (4) A warm handoff with detailed clinical summary should
have been prepared for the receiving providers in North Carolina.

PROBABLE OUTCOME: If WES had been ordered directly by Dr. Osei in
spring 2021, results would have been available within 12-16 weeks
(typical turnaround). Diagnosis of CLN5 could have been made by
approximately August-September 2021 — at age 9, approximately 3 years
before actual diagnosis.

ESTIMATED DIAGNOSTIC DELAY ATTRIBUTABLE: 2-3 years.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
MISSED OPPORTUNITY #7
Ages 10-12 (2022-2024) — North Carolina
Clinical Setting: Womack Army Medical Center, Fort Liberty, NC
Provider: Dr. Raymond Jeffers, MD — Neurology
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

CLINICAL EVENT: Dr. Jeffers assumed care of Eli after the family's
relocation to NC. Due to the records transfer failure (see Communication
Failure #3), Dr. Jeffers did NOT have Dr. Osei's notes, the 2021 MRI,
the 2021 EEG, the elevated lactate results, or the genetics
recommendation. He received only Dr. Chen's older notes (2018-2019)
and the 2018 MRI.

Dr. Jeffers conducted his own evaluation and over the next 2 years:
  - Repeated the EEG (November 2022) — confirmed epilepsy
  - Ordered a new MRI (March 2024) — showed severe atrophy
  - Ordered metabolic labs (duplicating work from Ohio)
  - Eventually ordered enzyme studies (PPT1, TPP1) and genetic testing

ACTION TAKEN: Thorough but duplicative workup over a 2-year period.

ACTION THAT SHOULD HAVE BEEN TAKEN: (1) A more aggressive effort to
obtain complete records from NCH, including direct provider-to-provider
contact. (2) If records were unobtainable, a higher index of suspicion
given the clinical presentation — the severity of Eli's neurological
status at age 10 warranted urgent genetic evaluation rather than a
step-wise re-workup. (3) WES should have been considered earlier in the
evaluation rather than after 2 years of other testing.

NOTE: Dr. Jeffers' clinical care was appropriate given the information
he had available. The primary failure was in the records system, not in
his clinical judgment. However, the overall system failure resulted in
2 years of diagnostic delay.

ESTIMATED DIAGNOSTIC DELAY ATTRIBUTABLE: 2 years.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
SUMMARY: CUMULATIVE IMPACT OF MISSED OPPORTUNITIES
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

Earliest Possible Diagnosis:      Age 2-3 (2014-2015) — if EEG had been
                                  obtained and triggered genetic workup
Most Realistic Earlier Diagnosis: Age 5-6 (2017-2018) — if seizures had
                                  been confirmed and WES ordered
Actual Diagnosis:                 Age 12 (June 2024) — WES result returned

Minimum Estimated Delay:          3 years (if Opportunity #6 alone had
                                  been captured)
Maximum Estimated Delay:          10 years (if Opportunity #1 had been
                                  captured)
Most Realistic Estimate:          5-7 years of potentially avoidable
                                  diagnostic delay

While it is not possible to state with certainty that any single
intervention would have led directly to the CLN5 diagnosis, the
cumulative weight of seven independent missed opportunities over 10 years
represents a systemic failure in the diagnostic process.

AGE 9 — IEP AMENDMENT (Spring 2021)
School: Cassingham Elementary
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

EMERGENCY IEP AMENDMENT — May 2021
Following Dr. Osei's diagnosis of epilepsy and identification of
neurodegenerative process:

  - Medical complexity formally documented in IEP
  - Seizure Action Plan updated with specific medication protocol:
    Diastat 10 mg rectal if seizure >5 minutes; call 911 if seizure
    >10 minutes or if breathing difficulty
  - Positioning protocol updated per PT (risk of aspiration)
  - Transportation changed to medical transport van with aide and
    wheelchair tie-down
  - Extended School Year (ESY) services approved for summer 2021 to
    prevent further regression during break

NOTE: This was the last IEP in the Columbus City Schools district. Family
relocated to North Carolina in June 2022.

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
AGE 10 — NEW IEP / TRANSFER (2022-2023 School Year)
School District: Cumberland County Schools, Fayetteville, NC
School: Seventy-First Classical Middle School — Exceptional Children Program
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

TRANSFER IEP MEETING: August 2022
  Ohio IEP accepted as comparable services plan pending NC re-evaluation.

NC RE-EVALUATION: October 2022
  Primary Disability Category: Multiple Disabilities
  (Changed from Autism — NC team determined that the presentation was
  more accurately characterized under MD given the progressive nature)

EVALUATION DATA:
  Cognitive:      Unable to formally assess — "Eli is unable to
                  participate in standardized cognitive assessment due
                  to motor, communication, and visual limitations.
                  Functional cognitive level estimated at 12-18 month
                  equivalent based on response to environmental stimuli."
  Adaptive:       Vineland-3 (parent interview)
                  Adaptive Behavior Composite: 28 (<0.1st percentile)
                  Communication: 22 | Daily Living: 30 | Socialization: 25
  Communication:  "Eli communicates primarily through eye gaze, facial
                  expression, and body tension changes. He shows
                  differential response to familiar vs. unfamiliar voices.
                  No verbal communication. Limited gestural communication."

PLACEMENT: Self-contained MD classroom, 6:1:3 ratio
  Full-time 1:1 nurse aide (TRICARE-funded)
  All adaptive equipment transferred from Ohio (wheelchair, standing
  frame, adapted seating system, communication device)

IEP GOALS (selected):
  - Communication: Will use eye gaze system (2-choice field) to make
    preferences known in 3/5 opportunities across 3 sessions
  - Motor/Positioning: Will maintain upright seated posture in adapted
    wheelchair for 45 minutes with repositioning
  - Social: Will demonstrate recognition of familiar people through
    visual tracking or smile
  - Health: Staff will implement seizure action plan, positioning
    schedule, and feeding protocol as medically prescribed

RELATED SERVICES:
  - Speech/Language: 2x/week, 30 min (AAC, oral motor)
  - Occupational Therapy: 2x/week, 30 min (positioning, sensory)
  - Physical Therapy: 2x/week, 30 min (ROM, positioning, equipment)
  - Adapted PE: 1x/week (sensory stimulation)
  - 1:1 Nurse Aide: Full school day
  - Vision Services: Consultation (visual decline documented)
  - Assistive Technology: Monthly consultation

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
AGE 12 — IEP ANNUAL REVIEW (2024-2025 School Year)
School: Seventy-First Classical Middle School
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

IEP REVISION — October 2024
Following confirmed NCL/CLN5 diagnosis (June 2024):

  - Medical documentation updated with NCL diagnosis and prognosis
  - All remaining academic goals formally replaced with functional
    life skills and quality of life goals
  - Goals focus on: comfort, sensory engagement, communication
    maintenance (yes/no through eye gaze), social connection,
    and caregiver-directed positioning/health maintenance

IEP GOALS (selected):
  - Will demonstrate yes/no response through directed eye gaze (look
    up = yes, look down = no) with 60% consistency
  - Will demonstrate preference through sustained visual fixation
    on preferred stimuli
  - Will tolerate and participate in range-of-motion exercises
    performed by therapists without distress indicators
  - Will maintain alertness during preferred sensory activities
    (music, tactile stimulation) for 15 minutes

PROGRESS NOTE (March 2025): "Eli has limited but meaningful
communication through eye gaze. He consistently recognizes his mother
and responds to her voice with visual tracking and occasional smile.
He shows preference for music and tactile activities. Seizure frequency
has improved somewhat with current medication regimen. He requires total
care for all activities of daily living."

--------------------------------------------------------------------------------
AGE 14 (CURRENT) — IEP AMENDMENT (2025-2026 School Year)
School District: Cumberland County Schools
Placement: HOMEBOUND INSTRUCTION
--------------------------------------------------------------------------------

IEP AMENDMENT — September 2025
Change of Placement: School-based → Homebound

RATIONALE: "Due to increasing medical fragility including frequent
seizures, aspiration risk, G-tube dependence, and overall decline in
medical stability, the IEP team, in consultation with the family and
medical providers, has determined that Eli can no longer safely attend
school in person. Homebound instruction will be provided per NC state
regulations."

HOMEBOUND SERVICES:
  - Certified special education teacher: 3 hours/week, in-home
  - Speech/Language therapist: 1x/week, 30 min, in-home (AAC
    maintenance, oral care, communication support)
  - Physical therapist: 1x/week, 30 min, in-home (ROM, positioning)
  - Occupational therapist: 1x/week, 30 min, in-home (sensory,
    positioning, adaptive)
  - Vision teacher: Monthly consultation

CURRENT IEP GOALS:
  - Communication: Will demonstrate yes/no response through eye gaze
    in response to simple questions with familiar communication partners
  - Engagement: Will show alertness and engagement during preferred
    sensory activities (music therapy, tactile books, family interaction)
    for minimum 10-minute intervals
  - Comfort: Will demonstrate reduced distress indicators (facial
    grimacing, body tension, crying) during positioning changes and
    care routines with the use of established comfort protocols
  - Connection: Will demonstrate social recognition of family members
    and familiar staff through visual tracking, smile, or vocalization

PARENT INPUT (Denise Reeves, IEP meeting September 2025):
"I want his goals to focus on his quality of life. I want him to be
comfortable, I want him to hear music, I want him to know that the people
who love him are there. That is what matters now. I want the school to
keep coming because it gives his day structure and he responds to the
teachers. He still lights up when Miss Angela [homebound teacher] comes.
That matters."

DISTRICT REPRESENTATIVE STATEMENT: "Cumberland County Schools is
committed to providing Eli with meaningful educational services focused
on quality of life, communication maintenance, and social connection
for as long as his family desires these services. The district will
continue to provide homebound instruction, related services, and
adaptive equipment as outlined in this IEP."
